The Three Categories of Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the three categories of oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of oaths (ghamus, laghw, mun'aqidah), distinguish them by their conditions and rulings, and apply the correct ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Oaths are of three types: ghamus (deliberate falsehood about past), laghw (unintentional about past), and mun'aqidah (binding about future) — each with a distinct legal consequence.

Before we dive into the specific rulings on oaths, it is essential to understand the foundational classification that the Shari'ah has established. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that while oaths may appear many, only three categories carry distinct legal effects: ghamus (a deliberate false oath on the past), laghw (an unintentional oath about the past), and mun'aqidah (a binding oath about the future). This lesson will walk you through each category, its definition, and its ruling, along with the proofs from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Categories
From the passage, extract the three named categories of oaths: ghamus, laghw, and mun'aqidah. Understand that these are the only ones considered by the Shari'ah for rulings.
The passage emphasizes that the absolute oath is more than three, but only these three have legal consequences.
2
Define Each Category
For each category, note the key characteristics: ghamus is a deliberate false oath about a past act, laghw is an unintentional oath about the past, and mun'aqidah is an oath concerning a future act that is binding.
The commentary refines the definition of 'act' to include both human actions and events like wind, so that the classification covers all possibilities.
3
Determine the Legal Ruling for Each
For each category, state the ruling: ghamus is a major sin (kabirah) with no kaffarah (expiation), only repentance; laghw has no sin and no kaffarah; mun'aqidah requires kaffarah if broken and is sinful without valid excuse.
The ruling on ghamus is based on the hadith of al-Bukhari listing it among the major sins.
4
Analyze the Dispute about the Definition of 'Act'
The passage addresses an objection: if 'act' is understood grammatically, it includes actions of inanimate objects (e.g., wind blowing). The commentator clarifies that the legal classification is not affected; the rulings of ghamus, laghw, and mun'aqidah still apply appropriately.
This shows the precision of Hanafi legal analysis in distinguishing linguistic from legal definitions.
5
Note the Dispute on Conditional Divorce as an Oath
The analytical toolbox indicates a dispute with Dawood al-Isfahani regarding whether conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) is considered an oath. The Hanafi position includes it as a type of oath due to its binding nature.
This is an additional point not in the core passage but relevant to the overall classification.

They are three. They are three, meaning the oaths that the Shari'ah has considered and on which it has based rulings are three. We only say this because the absolute oath is more than three, like the oath on a past act truthfully. And we mean by 'based rulings on them' the imposition of blame on the ghamus (false oath)... His statement: 'ghamus' is derived from 'ghams' (immersion), so called because it immerses one in sin, for it is a major sin, whether one seizes a Muslim's right by it or not, due to the hadith of al-Bukhari: 'The major sins are: associating partners with Allah, disobeying parents, killing a soul, and the ghamus oath.' This is as stated in 'al-Nahr'.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah, and instead one should define them more precisely as stated.

Key Terms
يمين غموس (yamin ghamus): A false oath about a past act, sworn deliberately and with knowledge of falsehood; it immerses one in sin.
يمين لغو (yamin laghw): An unintentional oath about the past, spoken without deliberation, e.g., 'No, by Allah' in casual speech.
يمين منعقدة (yamin mun'aqidah): An oath concerning a future act or omission, which is binding; if broken, kaffarah (expiation) is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true about the ghamus oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) It requires kaffarah and repentance.
B) It is a major sin requiring only repentance.
C) It is permissible if no one's rights are taken.
D) It is a minor sin requiring kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
The Shari'ah recognizes exactly three categories of oaths with distinct rulings: ghamus (major sin, no kaffarah), laghw (no sin, no kaffarah), and mun'aqidah (kaffarah required if broken).

Definition and Ruling of the Ghammus Oath
Defines ghammus and laghw oaths and states their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the ghammus oath, state its ruling (major sin requiring only tawbah, no kaffarah), and distinguish it from laghw and mun'aqidah oaths.

Core Idea
A deliberate false oath on the past (ghammus) is a major sin requiring only repentance, not expiation.

This lesson explores the Hanafi classification of oaths, focusing on the ghammus (deliberate false oath on a past event). Unlike the binding oath on the future (mun'aqidah) which requires expiation if broken, the ghammus is a pure major sin that cannot be expiated through kaffarah but only through sincere tawbah. We will examine the textual proof from the hadith and the legal reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Define the categories of oaths
Identify the three types: ghammus (deliberate false on past), laghw (unintentional false on past), mun'aqidah (binding on future).
The text defines ghammus as 'his oath upon a past action or omission, falsely and deliberately'.
2
Identify the ruling of ghammus
From the text, the ghammus is a major sin (kabirah) and haram. It requires only tawbah and istighfar, no kaffarah.
The author states: 'he sins by it – then he must repent and seek forgiveness, and expiation is not obligatory'.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith from Bukhari listing the ghammus oath among the major sins. The text quotes: 'The major sins: associating partners with Allah, disobedience to parents, killing a soul, and the ghammus oath.'
This hadith establishes the gravity of the oath.
4
Understand the legal reasoning for no kaffarah
Kaffarah (expiation) is for matters between halal and haram, not for pure sins. The text argues: 'the kaffarah is performed by fasting, which is pure worship' – thus it cannot expiate a pure sin.
This is a key Hanafi principle: kaffarah is not for pure major sins.
5
Distinguish ghammus from laghw
Laghw is an oath on a past event where the person believes it true but is mistaken. No sin or expiation. Ghammus is deliberate falsehood, thus sinful.
The text quotes Ibn Abbas on Quran 2:225 to define laghw.

Source Text

كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه
فحلفه على فعل أو ترك ماض كاذباً عمداً غَموس
وعدمها على اللغو، والكفارة على المنعقدة.
(فحلفه على فعل أو ترك ماض كاذباً عمداً غَموس) ، يمكن أن يراد بالفعل مصطلح أهل النحاة ، أو مصطلح أهل الكلام ، وهو المصدر أعم من أن يكون قائماً بالعقلاء أو بالجمادات، نحو: والله لقد هبت الريح.
فإن قلت إذا قيل : والله إن هذا حجر
ولما كان يرد عليه أنّ هذا أيضاً يترتب عليه الأحكام، فإنّ حكم مثل هذه اليمين أنه لا مؤاخذة فيها ولا كفارة، وأنه يستحب تقليلها ، ولا ينبغي الحلف بالله الله بلا ضرورة،
فلا يخرج مثل هذا بعد ذلك التقييد أيضاً، أشار إلى دفعه بقوله : «وعنينا .... الخ.
وحاصله أن المراد بترتب الأحكام ترتب الأحكام التي سيأتي ذكرها من ترتب الكفّارة، والمؤاخدة في المنعقدة وعدمهما في اللغو مع وجودِ الكذب، وترتب المؤاخذة فقط على الغموس، ولا شك أنّ هذه الأحكام لا توجد في غير الأقسام الثلاثة.
قوله : على اللغو؛ يَرِدُ عليه أنّ هذا الحكم مترتب على اليمين بالله الله على ماض صادقاً أيضاً ، فلا بد أن يزاد به ما يخرجه كما أشرنا إليه، والأحسنُ أن يقال ترتب المؤاخذة والكفّارة على المنعقدة وعدمهما على اللغو مع وجودِ ما يقتضيه ؛ أي الكذب ، وترتب المؤاخذة فقط على الغموس.
قوله : غَموس ؛ فعول من الغمس سُمّي به لأنه تغمسه في الإثم، فإنه كبيرة، سواء اقتطع به حق مسلم أو لا ؛ لحديث البخاري : «الكبائر : الإشراك بالله ، وعقوق الوالدين، وقتل النفس، واليمين الغموس» ، كذا في «النهر »
قوله : يمكن ... الخ ؛ يرد عليه أنَّ المقابلة التركُ بالفعل تأبى عن حمل الفعل على الفعل النحوي ، فالأولى أن يحمل الفعلُ على المعنى اللغوي.
قوله : فإن قلت...الخ ؛ حاصلُ الإيرادِ أنَّ الحلف الكاذب قد يكون على غيرِ فعل وترك، بل يتعلّق بالنسب الخبرية الاسمية.
وحاصل الجواب : إنه متضمن لمعنى الفعل أو الترك.
قوله : يقدر ؛ ليس المراد به التقدير لفظاً، فإنّه مما لا حاجة إليه، بل الغرض أنَّ نسبة شيء إلى شيءٍ تتضمن معنى الكون، وهو لا يكون إلا في أحد الأزمنة أو جميعها.
قوله : يأثم به ؛ فتلزمه التوبة والاستغفار، ولا تجب الكفارة ؛ لأنها كبيرة محضة، والكفارة لا تجب في الكبائر المحضة، بل في الأمور الدائرة بين الإباحة والحرمة ،
كيف لا ؟ والكفّارة تتأدّى بالصوم، وهو عبادة محضة.
قوله : أو ظاناً ؛ يعني حلّفه على ماض ظانّاً أنه صدق مطابق للواقع، والحال أنه ليس كذلك لغو، وهذا التفسير للغو منقول عن ابن عباس في تفسير قوله : لَا يُؤَاخِذُكُمُ اللهُ بِاللَّغْوِ فِي أَيْمَنِكُمْ وَلَكِن يُؤَاخِذُكُم بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ ، أخرجه ابن جرير وابنُ المنذر، وعن عائشة رضي الله عنها ، أخرجه ابن أبي حاتم والبيهقي، وعن أبي هريرة ، أخرجه ابن جرير. وروى البخاري وغيره عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : «أنَّ اللغو كلام الرجل في بيته» [؟]
Book of Oaths / Definition and its Categories. His oath upon a past action or omission, falsely and deliberately, is a ghammus. And the absence of both [sin and expiation] applies to al-laghw, and expiation applies to the binding oath. (His oath upon a past action or omission, falsely and deliberately, ghammus). It is possible that by 'action' is meant the term of the grammarians, or the term of the theologians, and it is the verbal noun, more general than being restricted to rational beings or inanimate objects, like: 'By Allah, the wind has blown.' If you say: when it is said: 'By Allah, this is a stone'... and since it is objected that this also entails rulings, for the ruling of such an oath is that there is no punishment and no expiation, and it is recommended to minimize it, and one should not swear by Allah without necessity, then such [an oath] is not excluded after that restriction. He indicated its rebuttal by his saying: 'And we intended...' etc. The summary is that what is meant by 'entailing rulings' is the rulings that will come later: entailing expiation and punishment in the binding oath, and their absence in laghw despite the existence of falsehood, and entailing punishment only in ghammus. There is no doubt that these rulings are not found in other than the three categories. He said: 'upon al-laghw' – it is objected that this ruling also applies to an oath by Allah upon a past matter truthfully, so it must be restricted by what excludes it as we indicated. Better is to say: entailing punishment and expiation on the binding oath, and their absence on laghw with the existence of what necessitates it, i.e., falsehood, and entailing punishment only on ghammus. He said: 'ghammus' – is from ghams, named so because it plunges one into sin. It is a major sin, whether it cuts off a Muslim's right or not, due to the hadith of Bukhari: 'The major sins: associating partners with Allah, disobedience to parents, killing a soul, and the ghammus oath' – as in al-Nahr. He said: 'it is possible...' etc. – it is objected that the pairing of 'omission' with 'action' prevents the interpretation of 'action' as the grammatical action, so it is better to interpret 'action' in its linguistic meaning. He said: 'if you say...' etc. – the summary of the objection is that the false oath may be upon other than action and omission, but rather relates to predicative nominal relations. The summary of the answer is that it contains the meaning of action or omission. He said: 'he intends' – it is not meant to be estimated verbally, for there is no need for that, but rather the purpose is that the attribution of something to something contains the meaning of 'being', which is only in one of the tenses or all of them. He said: 'he sins by it' – then he must repent and seek forgiveness, and expiation is not obligatory because it is a pure major sin, and expiation is not obligatory in pure major sins, but rather in matters that are between permissibility and prohibition. How could it be? Expiation is fulfilled by fasting, which is pure worship. He said: 'or thinking' – meaning he swore upon a past matter thinking it true and in accordance with reality, while in fact it is not so; that is laghw. This interpretation of laghw is transmitted from Ibn 'Abbas in interpreting His statement: 'Allah will not call you to account for the laghw in your oaths, but He will call you to account for what your hearts have earned' – recorded by Ibn Jarir and Ibn al-Mundhir; and from 'A'ishah, recorded by Ibn Abi Hatim and al-Bayhaqi; and from Abu Hurayrah, recorded by Ibn Jarir. And al-Bukhari and others narrated from 'A'ishah: 'The laghw is a man's speech in his house.' [؟]
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, discussing the book of oaths (kitab al-ayman). It begins by defining the three categories of oaths: ghammus (deliberate false oath on a past event), laghw (unintentional false oath), and mun'aqidah (binding oath). The focus here is on ghammus and laghw. The author explains that a ghammus oath is one sworn upon a past action or omission with deliberate falsehood. It is called ghammus because it plunges the person into sin (ghams). The ruling is that it is a major sin (kabirah), whether or not it involves usurping a Muslim's right. The proof is the hadith from Bukhari listing the ghammus oath among the major sins. For such an oath, the only requirement is repentance (tawbah) and seeking forgiveness (istighfar); no expiation (kaffarah) is required because expiation is for matters between permissibility and prohibition, not for pure sins—expiation is performed by fasting, which is pure worship, so it cannot expiate a pure sin. The passage also discusses laghw: an oath on a past event where the person believes it to be true but it is actually false. This is considered laghw, and there is no sin or expiation. The interpretation is attributed to Ibn 'Abbas and others based on Quran 2:225. The text addresses an objection about the definition of 'fi'l' (action) – whether it is grammatical or theological. The preferred view is to take it in the linguistic sense to include both actions and states, since oaths can be about nominal predicates like 'this is a stone'. The response is that such statements imply a meaning of action or omission (e.g., 'being' in a state). The overall purpose is to clarify that the rulings (sin, expiation, etc.) apply only to these three categories.

Key Terms
اليمين الغموس (al-yamin al-ghammus): A deliberate false oath on a past action or omission, considered a major sin.
اللغو (al-laghw): An oath on a past event where the person believes it to be true but is mistaken; no sin or expiation.
المنعقدة (al-mun'aqidah): A binding oath on a future action, which if broken requires kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a ghammus oath according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is a minor sin, requiring kaffarah.
B) It is a major sin, requiring tawbah and kaffarah.
C) It is a major sin, requiring only tawbah and istighfar, no kaffarah.
D) It is not sinful if no right is usurped.

Key Takeaway
The ghammus oath is a major sin requiring tawbah, not kaffarah.

8 min
The Three Categories of Oaths: Laghw, Ghamus, and Mun'aqida
Commentary defining oath types and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions and rulings of the three categories of oaths (laghw, ghamus, mun'aqida) and distinguish them based on their proofs.

Core Idea
An oath's ruling depends on its object and intent: past/present false oaths may be excused or sinful, but future oaths are binding.

In this passage, the commentator breaks down the matn's statement about 'hoping for forgiveness for laghw' and 'the binding future oath'. He clarifies the threefold classification: laghw (unintentional falsehood), ghamus (deliberate falsehood), and mun'aqida (binding future oath). Each has a distinct legal effect: laghw requires no expiation, ghamus requires repentance but no expiation, and mun'aqida requires expiation if broken. The discussion includes the Quranic basis from Surat al-Baqarah and al-Ma'idah, as well as a cross-madhhab difference with al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the commentary, extract the names of the three types of oaths: laghw (لغو), ghamus (غموس), and mun'aqida (منعقدة). Note that the first two concern past/present events, while the third concerns future actions.
2
Define each category
For each type, write down the definition as given in the passage: laghw is an oath about a past matter while believing it true but being false; ghamus is a deliberate lie about past or present; mun'aqida is an oath to do or leave something in the future.
Pay attention to the condition of intent for ghamus and the future tense for mun'aqida.
3
Determine the ruling
For each type, state the legal consequence: laghw – no sin, no expiation, forgiveness hoped; ghamus – major sin, no expiation, repentance required; mun'aqida – binding, expiation if broken.
The passage emphasizes that laghw is excused because of mistaken belief.
4
Examine the Quranic proofs
The passage quotes Quran 2:225 and 5:89. Explain how each verse applies: 2:225 negates accountability for laghw; 5:89 imposes expiation for 'aqadtum al-ayman' (binding oaths). Identify the key phrase 'aqadtum' that distinguishes mun'aqida.
The commentary notes that the connection to hope is due to disagreement on the interpretation of laghw.
5
Analyze the dispute on laghw
The passage mentions that al-Shafi'i includes expressions like 'La wallah' without intent as laghw, while Hanafis restrict laghw to false belief. Understand the thamarah: Shafi'is still forgive such utterances, Hanafis do not consider them oaths at all.
This difference does not affect the ruling of expiation, only the categorization.

Source Text

كتاب الأيمان/تعريفه وأقسامه
يرجى عفوه، وعلى آتِ منعقدة
ثُمَّ بين حكمه بقوله : (يرجى عفوه ).
ثُمَّ عطف على فعل أو ترك قوله : (وعلى آت" منعقدة)، الأحسن" أن يقال : وآت منعقدة بلا كلمة : على ؛ ليكون معطوفاً على ماض بيته، وفي المنزل لا والله، وبلى والله من غير قصد اليمين)، وبه أخذ الشافعي، وقد بسطت الكلام في هذا الباب في التعليق الممجّد على موطأ محمّد(۲) فطالعه إن شئت. [١] قوله : يرجى عفوه ؛ ويعني الرجاء في هذه اليمين العفو ؛ لكون الحالف معذوراً، حيث حلف على ظنّ الصدق، فلا مؤاخذة فيه ولا كفارة.
قال في «الهداية»: «يمينُ اللغو أن يحلف على أمر ماض وهو يظن أنه كما قال ، والأمر بخلافه، فهذه اليمين نرجو أن لا يؤاخذ الله الله بها صاحبها، ومن اللغو أن يقول : والله إنه لزيد، وهو يظنّه زيداً ، وإنما هو عمرو، والأصل فيه قوله : لا يُؤَاخِذُكُمُ اللَّهُ بِاللَّغْوِ فِي أَيْمَنِكُمْ ﴾ الآية، إلا أنه علقه بالرجاء للاختلاف في تفسيره». انتهى قال في «العناية»: «أي صورة يمين اللغو مختلف فيها، وإنما علق بالرجاء نفي المؤاخذة بالصورة التي ذكرها، وذلك غير معلوم بالنص) (4) [٢] قوله : وعلي آت ؛ هو اسم الفاعل من الإيتان ؛ أي حلفه على فعل أو تركه مستقبل يسمَّى منعقداً ؛ لأنه عقد قلبه وربط عزمه به ، وهو المراد بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في سورة البقرة : ( وَلَكِن يُؤَاخِذُكُم بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ ) (٥) وبقوله ما في سورة المائدة : لَا يُؤَاخِذُكُمُ اللهُ بِاللَّغْوِ فِي أَيْمَنِكُمْ وَلَكِن يُؤَاخِذُكُم بِمَا عقدتُمُ الْأَيْمَنَ فَكَفَّرَتُهُ إطْعَامُ عَشَرَةِ مَسَكِينَ مِنْ أَوْسَطِ مَا تُطْعِمُونَ أَهْلِيكُمْ أَوْ كِسْوَتُهُمْ أَوْ تَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ ثَلَثَةِ أَيَّامٍ ذَلِكَ كَفَتَرَةُ أَيْمَنِكُمْ إِذَا حَلَفْتُمْ وَاحْفَظُوا أَيْمَنَكُمْ (1). [٣]قوله : الأحسن.... الخ ؛ أشار به إلى كون كلام المصنف له أيضاً صحيحاً لا ما فإنَّه إذا ذُكِرَ لفظ على، يكون معطوفاً على فعل أو ترك، ثُمَّ لا بُدَّ أن يقدر لقوله : آت ؛ موصوف، وهو فعل أو ترك، فيكون فيه إطناب مع وجوب تقديرِ ليس بمذكور، ولو أسقط لفظة : على ، حتى يكون عطفاً على ماض، ففيه إيجاز" بلا احتياج إلى تقدير شيء غير ملفوظ فإن قلت : الحلفُ كَما يكونُ على الماضي والآتي، يكون على الحالِ أيضاً، فلم لم يذكره، وهو من أي قسم من أقسام الحلف.
قلت : إنما لم يذكره لمعنى دقيق
باطلاً ، إما بحذف موصوف كما سيذكره، وإما بأن يقال : إنّ قوله : «على آتِ» معطوف على قوله : «ماض» بإعادة الجرّ ، والداعي إلى إعادته بعد المعطوف عليه ذكرا. [1] قوله : ثم لا بد...الخ ؛ حاصله أنه لَمّا عطف قوله : «آت» على قوله : «فعل وترك» احتيج إلى موصوف «آت»، وهو فعل أو ترك، فيحكم بحذفه، فيلزم حينئذ التطويلُ مع تكلّف الحذف، ولو قال: «وآت» عطفاً على «ماض»، كان فيه اختصار مع عدم الحاجة إلى حذف شيء. [٢] قوله : إيجاز؛ هو عبارة عن كون الكلام المؤدي للمقصودِ مختصراً، ويقابله الإطناب، وهو أن يكون الكلام زائداً على أصل المراد، فإن ساوى الكلام المعنى سُمِّي بالمساواة، وتفصيلُ هذه الثلاثة في كتب علم المعاني والبيان. [٣] قوله : فإن قلت... الخ ؛ حاصلُ الإيرادِ أنَّ المصنّف ذكر الحلف على مستقبل، وسماه منعقداً، وذكر الحلف على ماض وقسمه على قسمين، فإنّه إن كان كذباً عمداً فهو غموس ، وإن كان كذباً ظانّاً أنّه صدق فهو لغو، فصارت الأقسام التي تصوّر فيها الحنث ثلاثة.
وبقي هاهنا الحلف على الزمان الحال، وهو الحد المشترك بين الماضي والمستقبل، وفي المختار (۳) (۲۸۵ : الغموس وهي الحلف على أمرٍ ماض أو حال يتعمد فيها الكذب فلا كفارة فيها.
Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections. He hopes for His forgiveness, and upon a future [oath] that is binding. Then He explained its ruling in His statement: (He hopes for His forgiveness). Then He connected to an action or abandonment His statement: (and upon a future [oath] that is binding). The best is to say: and a future binding [oath] without the word 'upon'; so that it is coordinated with 'past'. [In a] house, 'No by Allah' and 'Yes by Allah' without intending an oath, and this is what al-Shafi'i held. And I have elaborated on this chapter in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad on Muwatta' Muhammad, so consult it if you wish. [1] His statement: He hopes for His forgiveness; meaning hope in this oath for forgiveness, because the swearer is excused, since he swore based on an assumption of truth, so there is no blame or expiation. He said in al-Hidayah: 'The laghw oath is that one swears about a past matter while thinking it is as he said, but the matter is contrary; this oath we hope Allah does not hold its possessor accountable. And from laghw is that one says: By Allah, it is Zayd, while thinking it is Zayd, but it is actually 'Amr. The basis for this is His statement: 'Allah does not hold you accountable for laghw in your oaths' [Quran 2:225] except that He attached it to hope due to disagreement in its interpretation.' End quote. He said in al-'Inayah: 'That is, the form of the laghw oath is disputed; and He attached it to hope negating accountability in the form He mentioned, and that is not known by text.' [2] His statement: 'and upon a future'; it is the active participle from al-Ityan; meaning his swearing about a future action or abandonment is called 'binding' (mun'aqid) because he ties his heart and binds his resolve to it. And it is what is meant by His statement in Surat al-Baqarah: 'But He will hold you accountable for what your hearts have earned' [2:225] and by His statement in Surat al-Ma'idah: 'Allah does not hold you accountable for laghw in your oaths, but He holds you accountable for what you have bound the oaths, so its expiation is feeding ten poor people from the middle of what you feed your families, or clothing them, or freeing a slave; and whoever does not find [the means] then fasting three days. That is the expiation of your oaths when you swear, and keep your oaths' [5:89]. [3] His statement: 'the best...etc.'; he indicated by this that the author's speech is also correct. For if the word 'upon' is mentioned, it becomes coordinated with 'action' or 'abandonment', then there must be estimated for His statement 'future' an attribute which is an action or abandonment, so there is verbosity with the necessity of an estimation that is not mentioned. But if the word 'upon' is dropped, so that it is coordinated with 'past', then there is brevity without needing to estimate anything unspoken. If you say: swearing as it occurs regarding the past and future, also occurs regarding the present, so why did he not mention it, and which section of the sections of swearing is it? I say: He only did not mention it for a subtle reason invalid, either by dropping an attribute as he will mention, or by saying that His statement 'upon a future' is coordinated with His statement 'past' by repeating the preposition, and the motive for repeating it after the coordinated is mentioned. [1] His statement: then there must... etc.; its summary is that when he coordinated His statement 'future' with His statement 'action and abandonment', there is need for an attribute of 'future' which is action or abandonment, so it is judged to be dropped, then elongation follows with the difficulty of dropping. If he said: 'and a future' coordinated with 'past', then there is brevity without the need to drop anything. [2] His statement: brevity; it is an expression for speech that conveys the intended meaning concisely, and its opposite is verbosity, which is speech exceeding the original meaning. If speech equals the meaning, it is called equality. The details of these three are found in the books of semantics and rhetoric. [3] His statement: if you say... etc.; the summary of the objection is that the author mentioned swearing about the future and called it binding, and mentioned swearing about the past and divided it into two parts: if it is an intentional lie, it is ghamus, and if it is a lie while thinking it is true, it is laghw. So the sections in which breaking the oath is conceivable become three. And here remains swearing about the present time, which is the common border between past and future. And in al-Mukhtar: 'Al-Ghamus is swearing about a past or present matter intentionally lying, and there is no expiation in it.'
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from the chapter on oaths, defining and explaining the three main types of oaths: laghw (unintentional false oath), ghamus (deliberate false oath), and mun'aqida (binding future oath). The commentator expands on the matn line 'يرجى عفوه، وعلى آتِ منعقدة' (He hopes for His forgiveness, and upon a future binding oath). The commentary begins by explaining the ruling of the laghw oath: because the swearer is excused for believing his statement to be true, no expiation or sin is incurred, and forgiveness is hoped for. This is supported by Quran 2:225 and a quote from al-Hidayah. The commentary then notes a disagreement among scholars regarding the exact definition of laghw, including the Shafi'i view that expressions like 'La wallah' without intent are included. The discussion then turns to the wording of the matn, debating whether 'على آت' is the best phrasing or if 'وآت' is more concise. The commentator explains that 'آت' refers to a future action or abandonment, and this type of oath is called 'mun'aqida' because it binds the heart. The ruling for mun'aqida is derived from Quran 5:89, which prescribes expiation for breaking such oaths. The commentary also touches on the literary concepts of brevity (إيجاز) and verbosity (إطناب). Finally, a question is raised about the present time oath, and the answer clarifies that it is considered ghamus if intentional and false, with no expiation required.

Key Terms
يمين اللغو (yamin al-laghw): An oath about a past matter where the swearer thinks it is true but it is actually false; there is no sin or expiation, and forgiveness is hoped for.
يمين الغموس (yamin al-ghamus): A deliberate false oath about a past or present matter; it is a major sin, but there is no expiation; repentance is required.
يمين منعقدة (yamin mun'aqida): An oath to perform or abandon an action in the future; it is binding and breaking it requires expiation as prescribed in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, what is the ruling on a person who swears 'By Allah, it is Zayd' honestly believing it is Zayd, but it turns out to be 'Amr?
A) It is a major sin requiring expiation.
B) It is a sin but no expiation required.
C) It is not a sin and no expiation required; forgiveness is hoped for.
D) It is a binding oath that must be expiated.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh classifies oaths into three types (laghw, ghamus, mun'aqida) with distinct rulings based on the object and intent, and only the binding future oath (mun'aqida) requires expiation when broken.

intermediate
10 min
Oaths on the Present Moment Subsumed Under Past
Commentator explains why the matn omits oaths on the present moment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain and apply the Hanafi ruling that an oath on the present moment is treated as an oath on the past, using the rational proof based on the flow of time.

Core Idea
An oath on the present is an oath on the past because the present becomes past at the moment the oath is concluded.

In the Hanafi classification of oaths, three categories are usually discussed: oaths on the past, on the future, and laghw (careless oaths). But what about an oath like 'By Allah, I am writing this now'? The author of al-Wiqaya omitted mention of the present, and the commentator in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya provides a subtle justification. In this lesson, we will examine the grammatical and philosophical reasoning behind this omission and learn how the Hanafi school resolves the apparent gap.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing an oath concerning the present moment. The ruling is that it is treated as an oath on the past, falling under the established categories of ghamus or laghw.
This is a classification ruling, not a hukm taklifi like fard or wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the nature of time. The commentator explains that speech occurs first in the soul (kalam nafsi), then expressed verbally; the oath is concluded only after the utterance is complete, so the present moment has already become past.
The dalil is not from Quran or hadith but from logical reasoning (qiyas).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key is the temporal gap: the present moment during speech elapses during the utterance, so the oath's legal conclusion refers to a past event. Hence, no separate category is needed.
This argument is considered valid in Hanafi usul despite being based on a metaphysical concept of time.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars might object that the present is a distinct temporal category, requiring separate treatment. The Hanafi response is that the present cannot be isolated because of the continuous flow of time.
The commentator also notes that the basis of oaths is custom ('urf), not pure philosophy, but the argument holds.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon by later authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin) and is consistent with Zahir al-Riwayah, which does not give separate rules for present oaths.
The mufta bihi is that oaths on the present are subsumed under past oaths.

وهو أن الكلام يحصلُ أوّلاً في النفس، فيعبر عنه باللسان، فالإخبار المتعلق بزمان الحال اذا حصل في النفس، فيعبر عنه باللسان"، فإذا تم التعبير باللسان

وبالجملة : الحلف على الحالِ حلفٌ على الماضي حقيقة، فيكون حكمه حكمه ؛ فلهذا لم يذكره المصنف الله على حدة، ولا يخفى على الفطن أنّ هذا التقرير يقتضي أن لا يكون الحلف على الحالِ شيئاً مغايراً للحلف على الماضي، فلا يصلح وجهاً لعدم
ذكر المصنف.
فإن السؤال كان عن عدم الذكر بعد كونه شيئاً معتبراً، وأيضاً هذه النكتة التي
ذكرها إنّما يعتبرها أهل الحكمة لا أهل العرف ومبنى الأيمان على العرف، وأهل
For if the word 'ala' is mentioned, it is conjoined to a verb or leaving, then it is necessary to estimate for his saying 'coming' a described thing, which is a verb or leaving, so there is verbosity with the necessity of estimating what is not mentioned. And if he dropped the word 'ala' so that it becomes a conjunction to the past, then there is brevity without needing to estimate anything unspoken. For if you say: The oath, just as it occurs on the past and the future, occurs also on the present, so why did he not mention it, and which section of the sections of oaths is it? (1) I say: He did not mention it only for a subtle reason. [1] His saying: 'then it is necessary' etc.; its summary is that when he conjoined his word 'coming' to 'verb and leaving', there was need for a described thing for 'coming', which is a verb or leaving, so it is ruled to be omitted, and then there is lengthiness with the difficulty of omission, and if he said: 'and coming' conjoined to 'past', there would be conciseness without needing to omit anything. [2] His saying: 'brevity'; it is an expression for the speech that conveys the intended meaning concisely, and its opposite is verbosity, which is when the speech exceeds the original meaning; if the speech equals the meaning, it is called equality; the details of these three are in the books of the science of meanings and rhetoric. [3] His saying: 'for if you say' etc.; the summary of the objection is that the author mentioned the oath on the future and called it concluded, and mentioned the oath on the past and divided it into two types: if it is intentional falsehood then it is ghamus, and if it is falsehood thinking it true then it is laghw, so the categories in which breaking is conceived become three. And there remains here the oath on the present time, which is the common boundary between past and future. (1) Mulla Khusraw said after mentioning the commentator's speech at length in Durar al-Hukkam (2): (39): Rather the correct answer is to say: There is no basis for this question after he said first that absolute oath is more than three, so reflect. And in al-Mukhtar (3) (285): Ghamus is the oath on a past or present matter in which intentional falsehood is used, so there is no expiation in it. Oaths: its definition and divisions. And it is that speech occurs first in the soul, then expressed by the tongue; the report concerning the present time when it occurs in the soul, then expressed by the tongue, so when the expression by the tongue is completed, the oath is concluded; thus the present time becomes past relative to the time of conclusion of the oath, so it is not known from which of the mentioned sections of oaths it is, and if it were outside them, it would necessitate that the oaths are not confined to three; so why did the author not mention it and not state its ruling? [1] His saying: 'occurs first'; i.e., before speaking it, and then it is called internal speech, which is meant in the poet's saying: 'Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made a guide to the heart.' And this is different from the meaning obtained after speaking it; so verbal speech indicates internal speech, which is a simple summary form present in the mind, which the tongue details by speaking the words that indicate it; and if you wish a detailed study of this speech, refer to the books of the science of scholastic theology in the topic of speech. [2] His saying: 'then expressed by the tongue' etc.; the summary of the answer according to what is understood from the apparent meaning of the commentator: it is that in every speech, a summary meaning must first occur in the soul, expressed by words, and the time of conclusion of the oath is the time of completion of expression; so when the meaning of the oath speech occurs first in the soul, then expressed by words, such as his saying, 'By Allah, I am writing' intending the present, then his narration time is only the time from the beginning of his speech to its end, which they call present, and it is past relative to the time of conclusion, because time is flowing and not static in existence; thus the oath upon it is an oath upon the past, and it enters into the previous category. In summary: The oath on the present is an oath on the past in reality, so its ruling is its ruling; therefore the author did not mention it separately. And it is not hidden from the intelligent that this explanation implies that the oath on the present is not a thing different from the oath on the past, so it is not suitable as a reason for the author's non-mention. For the question was about non-mention after it was a considered thing; and also this subtlety that he mentioned is only considered by the people of wisdom, not by the people of custom, and the basis of oaths is on custom, and the people of...
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, addresses a subtle point in the classification of oaths (al-ayman). The original matn (al-Wiqaya) divided oaths into those on the past and those on the future, but did not explicitly mention oaths on the present moment. The commentator here explains why. He begins by analyzing the author's wording: the phrase 'on coming' ('ala atin) is conjoined to 'verb or leaving', requiring an omitted described noun, which creates verbosity. A more concise phrasing would be 'and coming' ('wa atin) conjoined directly to 'past'. This grammatical digression highlights the author's style. Then the core question is raised: Oaths can also be on the present, so why did the matn omit it? The commentator answers with a subtle point: oaths on the present are effectively oaths on the past because of the nature of time. At the moment of uttering the oath, the present instantly becomes past. He uses the concept of 'internal speech' (al-kalam al-nafsi): the meaning occurs in the mind first, then is expressed verbally. The time of expression (the present) elapses during the utterance, so by the time the oath is concluded, the event being sworn about is already past. Therefore, all oaths on the present fall under the category of oaths on the past. The author did not need to mention them separately. The commentator also notes that some scholars, like Mulla Khusraw, suggested a simpler answer: the author already indicated that oaths are more than three, so the question is unnecessary. Additionally, the definition of ghamus (false oath) includes both past and present matters, reinforcing that present is not distinct. The passage also explains the literary terms 'brevity' (ijaz) and 'verbosity' (itnab), showing the commentator's concern with rhetorical style. The final note critiques the answer as being based on philosophical time (ahl al-hikma) rather than custom ('urf), which is the basis of oaths in Islamic law. This highlights a potential tension between rational analysis and practical custom.

Key Terms
الحلف على الحال (al-hilf 'ala al-hal): An oath that refers to the present moment, e.g., 'By Allah, I am writing.'
إيجاز (ijaz): Conciseness in speech that conveys the intended meaning with fewer words.
إطناب (itnab): Verbosity, i.e., speech that exceeds the original intended meaning.
غموس (ghamus): An oath on the past or present in which the person intentionally lies; it is a major sin and requires no expiation.
لغو (laghw): An oath on the past in which the person mistakenly believes he is truthful; it is excused and has no expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why did the author of al-Wiqaya not separately mention oaths on the present moment?
A) Because oaths on present are not possible in Islamic law.
B) Because the present becomes past at the moment the oath is concluded, so they fall under past oaths.
C) Because the author only dealt with past and future due to space constraints.
D) Because oaths on present are invalid.

Key Takeaway
An oath on the present is not a separate category; it is subsumed under oaths on the past because the present becomes past at the moment the oath is concluded.

Why Swearing About the Present Is Like Swearing About the Past
Commentary justifying the omission of present-time oaths from the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning that an oath about the present is treated as an oath about the past and state the consequent ruling (binding oath requiring kaffarah).

Core Idea
Swearing about the present is legally identical to swearing about the past because the present moment elapses during the utterance.

In this lesson, we dive into a subtle but important point in Hanafi fiqh of oaths: why the author of the matn did not list swearing about the present as a separate category. The commentary reveals a deep interplay between the nature of time, inner speech, and customary understanding ('urf). You will see that the present is not a stable reference point, so an oath about 'now' actually refers to a time that has already passed when the oath is complete.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafi theory of speech
Recognize that every oath begins as inner speech (kalam nafsi) in the soul, then is expressed verbally (kalam lafzi). The time of contracting the oath is when the verbal expression ends.
Inner speech is a simple mental picture; the tongue details it.
2
Analyze the three time categories in oaths
Identify the three customary time divisions: past, present, future. The matn only explicitly mentions past and future because the present, upon scrutiny, collapses into the past or is a fleeting instant.
Custom recognizes present, but from a realistic (tahqiq) perspective, time is only past and future.
3
Trace the reasoning for omitting the present
When one swears about the present (e.g., 'By Allah, I am writing'), the action reported must have begun before the speech and continues. By the time the oath is concluded, the moment is past. Hence the oath is effectively about the past.
The present moment is the interval between start and end of speech, which relative to the contract moment is past.
4
Identify the ruling and its basis
Because it falls under oaths about the past, it is a binding oath (yamin mun'aqida). Breaking it makes expiation (kaffarah) wajib. The justification is both the nature of time and custom ('urf), though the commentator notes that people of custom do distinguish present from past in principle.
The author's omission is justified because the ruling is identical; separate mention is unnecessary.
5
Apply the ruling to a practice scenario
Given a hypothetical: A says 'By Allah, I am writing this letter now' but actually is not writing. Determine the category, ruling, and consequence.
Use the guided steps above to classify and derive the ruling.

Oaths: Its definition and divisions. It is that speech occurs first in the soul, then it is expressed by the tongue. The report related to the present time, when it occurs in the soul, is expressed by the tongue. When the expression by the tongue is completed, the oath is contracted. So the present time has become past relative to the time of the oath's contract, so it is not known from which of the mentioned divisions of swearing it is. And if it were outside them, it would necessitate that oaths are not limited to three, so the author did not mention it separately and did not state its ruling. [1] His statement: 'occurs first' means before speaking it, and then it is called inner speech (kalam nafsi). This is intended in the poet's verse: 'Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue was made a guide to the heart.' This is different from the meaning obtained by speech after speaking it. So verbal speech indicates inner speech, which is a simple summary picture in the mind that the tongue details by speaking the words that indicate it. If you wish to elaborate on this speech, refer to books on theology (kalam) in the discussion of speech. [2] His statement: 'then it is expressed by the tongue' etc.; the summary of the answer according to what is apparent from the commentator's speech: it is necessary in every speech that a summary meaning first occurs in the soul which is expressed by words, and the time of contracting the oath is the time of concluding the expression. So if the meaning of the oath speech occurs first in the soul and is expressed by words, like his saying: 'By Allah, I am writing' intending the present, then the time of its narration is the time from the beginning of his speech to its end, which they call the present. This is past relative to the time of contracting because time flows and is not fixed in existence. So swearing on it is swearing on the past and it enters into the previous category. In summary: swearing on the present is truly swearing on the past, so its ruling is its ruling. For this reason, the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not mention it separately. This does not escape the intelligent person that this reasoning requires that swearing on the present is not something different from swearing on the past, so it is not suitable as a reason for the author not mentioning it. Indeed, the question was about not mentioning it after it was something considered. Also, this subtle point that he mentioned is only considered by the people of philosophy (ahl al-hikma), not the people of custom (ahl al-'urf), and the basis of oaths is on custom. The people of custom judge that the necessary consequence is three, and swearing on the present is different from swearing on the past, and legal rulings in this chapter proceed according to custom. So it is better to say: The author did not mention it because it is like swearing on the past in terms of division and ruling. You may interpret the commentator's speech as this and make the subtle point a cause for not mentioning it separately, even though it is considered legally and customarily. Then the meaning of his statement: 'so swearing on it is swearing on the past' is that swearing on the present is like swearing on the past. So remember this for it is from the inspirations of the moment. [1] His statement: 'necessary' because it is a narration of a writing that occurred before this speech, and for the truthfulness of the proposition, there must be correspondence of the narrator to what is narrated about; i.e., correspondence of the perfect predicative relation that occurs in the proposition between the subject and the predicate to the external reality that occurred at a time indicated by the narration's word, whether past or future. [2] His statement: 'according to custom' meaning what is narrated with the present tense form. He indicated by this that the division of time into past, future, and present is only according to custom and the apparent, but in reality it is two divisions: past and future, and between them is a shared instant. [3] His statement: 'to the conclusion' (ila an al-faragh); 'right now' (al-an) with elongated alif is the indivisible boundary separating the two times. It is the beginning for one of its parts and the end for the other, like the point between two lines. [4] His statement: 'and expiate in it' (wa kaffir fihi) is past tense of takfir, meaning to perform expiation; i.e., upon the one who swears, in a binding oath, it is obligatory to perform expiation if he breaks it. [0] His statement: 'only' (faqat) has two possible meanings:
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition and divisions of oaths (al-ayman) from the matn. The commentator begins by establishing the Hanafi theory of speech: every oath originates as inner speech (kalam nafsi) in the mind, then is expressed verbally (kalam lafzi). The poet's verse is cited as evidence that speech in the heart is different from uttered words. The commentator then addresses a subtle question: why did the author of the matn not list swearing about the present moment as a separate category? The answer involves the nature of time. When a person swears about the present (e.g., 'I am writing'), the moment is already past by the time the oath is completed. Therefore, in Hanafi fiqh, an oath about the present is effectively an oath about the past. However, the commentator notes that from a customary perspective, people distinguish between present and past, and legal rulings in oaths are based on custom ('urf). So, the better explanation is that the author omitted the present case because it is similar to the past case in terms of division (both are binding) and ruling (expiation required upon breaking). The passage then clarifies the temporal reasoning with examples: 'I wrote' requires past writing, 'I will write' requires future writing, and the time between speech beginning and ending is considered the present. The rule is that the present moment is a fleeting instant, so swearing about it is past-oriented. Finally, the commentator interprets the matn's phrase 'and only expiate in it if he breaks it' (wa kaffir fihi faqat in hanath). This means that for a binding oath (yamin mun'aqida), expiation is wajib upon breaking, and the word 'only' (faqat) may indicate that expiation is limited to that specific case or that no other action is required.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي (al-kalam al-nafsi): The internal speech that occurs first in the soul/mind before verbal expression.
الحلف على الحال (al-hilf 'ala al-hal): Swearing about the present moment, e.g., saying 'By Allah, I am doing something now.'
اليمين المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqida): A binding oath that is validly contracted and requires expiation if broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why did the author of the matn not list swearing about the present as a separate category among the three divisions of oaths?
A) Because swearing about the present is invalid in Hanafi law.
B) Because swearing about the present is considered swearing about the past in terms of its ruling and division.
C) Because the matn only deals with oaths about the past and future.
D) Because swearing about the present is rare and thus omitted.

Key Takeaway
Swearing about the present is legally subsumed under swearing about the past, making it a binding oath with expiation upon breach.

Kaffarah Only in Mun'aqidah Oaths
Explaining that kaffarah is limited to concluded oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kaffarah for oaths and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position on yamīn al-ghamūs.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is only wajib in mun'aqidah oaths; ghamees requires repentance, not expiation.

This passage clarifies that, according to the Hanafī school, expiation (kaffārah) is only obligatory upon violating a concluded oath (al-yamīn al-mun'aqidah). It addresses a key dispute with the Shāfi'ī school regarding oaths that are knowingly false about the past (al-yamīn al-ghamūs). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of oaths in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify clearly what the Hanafī school holds about kaffārah for different types of oaths.
Kaffārah is wājib only in mun'aqidah (concluded) oath.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize which school or scholar disagrees and what they claim.
Al-Shāfi'ī holds that kaffārah is wājib in yamīn al-ghamūs as well.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the reasoning behind the opposing view.
Shāfi'ī argues that ghamees shares deliberate lying (kadhib 'amdī) with mun'aqidah.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafī school refutes the opponent's analogy.
The text references an earlier refutation (qad marra sābiqan mā yadfa'uh) that defends the distinction; ghamees is a sin requiring repentance, not kaffārah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of the disagreement.
In Hanafī, one who swears a false oath about the past does not expiate; in Shāfi'ī, they do.

Source Text

العرف يحكمون بكون اللازمة ثلاثة، ويكون الحلف على الحال مغايراً للحلف على الماضي، والحكم الشرعي في هذا الباب يجري حسب العرف.

من سوانح الوقت. بالنظر إلى التحقيق فهو قسمان : ماض ومستقبل، وبينهما آن مشترك. [٣] قوله : إلى أن الفراغ ؛ الآن بمد الألف : هو الحد الغير المتجزئ الفاصل بين الزمانين، وهو مبدأ لواحد من جزئيه ، ومنتهي للآخر، كالنقطة بين الخطين.

الحالف في المنعقدة أداء الكفاءة إن حنث
[0] قوله : فقط ؛ يحتمل محملين :
إنما قال : فقط ؛ احتزازاً عن مذهب الشافعي له من الكفارة في الغموس.
أحدهما : أن يكون متعلقاً بقوله : «كفّر»، ويكون إشارة إلى أنَّ في الحلف المنعقد الواجب الكفارة فقط ، لا الإثم عند الحنث، فقد يكون الحنث مستحباً أو واجباً فلا يأثم به إنما تجب الكفارة جزاءً لهتك حرمة اسم الله ، يدل عليه قوله : «إذا حلفت على يمين فرأيت غيرها خيراً منها فآت بالذي هو خير، وكفّر عن يمينك»(٢)، وله ألفاظ مُخَرَّجة في الصحاح الستة وغيرها.
وأنت تعلم ضعف هذا المحمل ، ولأن مال إليه بعض الناظرين، فإن كثيراً ما يكون الحنث ممنوعاً شرعاً في المنعقدة، وهو ما إذا لم يكن غير المحلوف عليه خيراً ، فحينئذ يجب البرّ ويحرم أو يكره الحنث، وعند ذلك يلحقه الإثم بلا شبهة عند الحنث،
بل لقائل أن يقول : الأصل في الحنث هو الإثم، وإنما تخلّف فيما تخلّف لعارض. وثانيهما : أنه مرتبط بالضمير في قوله : «فيه»، وهذا هو الذي اختاره الشارح ه ، وعلى هذا يكون المعنى أنَّ وجوب الكفّارة إنّما هو في المنعقدة فقط لا في القسمين الآخرين ؛ أي الغموس واللغو، وإنّما اكتفى الشارحه على ذكـر الـغـمـوس ؛ وجوب الكفّارة فيه مذهب الشافعي له ، وأما وجوبه في اللغو مجرد احتمال لم يذهب إليه أحد. [١]قوله : عن مذهب الشافعي له ؛ فإنّه قال بوجوب الكفّارة في الغموس أيضاً ؛
لاشتراكه بالمنعقد في الكذب العمدي، وقد مرّ سابقاً ما يدفعه.
("And he expiates in it only if he violates") Customary practice judges that the consequence is threefold, and swearing about the present is different from swearing about the past, and the legal ruling in this chapter follows custom. So it is better to say: The author did not mention it [separately] because it is like swearing about the past in division and ruling, but you may interpret the commentator's words accordingly, and make the point that it was not mentioned separately although it is valid legally and customarily. Then the meaning of his statement "so swearing about it is swearing about the past" is that swearing about the present is like swearing about the past. So remember this, for it is a timely insight. [1] His statement: "must" because it is a narration of writing that occurred before this speech, and for the truth of the proposition, the narration must match the narrated; i.e., the complete informative relation that occurred in the proposition between the subject and predicate in relation to something external to this speech, occurring in a time indicated by the word of narration, whether past or future. [2] His statement: "according to custom"; that which is narrated about in the present tense, and he indicated that the division of time into past, future, and present is only according to custom and appearance. As for reality, [time] is two types: past and future, and between them is a shared instant. [3] His statement: "until completion"; 'al-ān' is the indivisible boundary separating the two times, the beginning of one and the end of the other, like the point between two lines. [4] His statement: "and he expiates in it"; past tense of takfīr, meaning performing kaffārah; i.e., the one who swears a concluded oath must perform kaffārah if he violates it. [0] His statement: "only" has two possible interpretations: He only said "only" to exclude the Shāfi'ī school's view of kaffārah in the ghamees oath. One: It is attached to his saying "expiates", and is an indication that in the concluded oath, only kaffārah is obligatory, not sin upon violation; violation may be recommended or obligatory, so he does not sin by it, but kaffārah is obligatory as a penalty for violating the sanctity of Allah's name. This is supported by his saying: "If you swear an oath and see something better, then do what is better and expiate for your oath," with variations in the six authentic collections. And you know the weakness of this interpretation, although some observers inclined to it; for often violation is prohibited legally in the concluded oath, when there is nothing better than the oath, then fulfilling is obligatory and violation is forbidden or disliked, and then he is definitely sinful when violating. Rather, one can say the default in violation is sin, and it is only removed due to a countervailing factor. The second: It is connected to the pronoun in his saying "in it", and this is what the commentator chose. According to this, the meaning is that the obligation of kaffārah is only in the concluded oath, not in the other two types: the ghamees and the laghw. The commentator only mentioned the ghamees because obligation of kaffārah in it is the view of al-Shāfi'ī, while obligation in laghw is a mere possibility that no one held. [1] His statement: "to exclude the Shāfi'ī school"; for he said that kaffārah is obligatory in ghamees as well, because it shares with the concluded oath in deliberate lying, and [the refutation of that] has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the scope of kaffārah (expiation) for oaths. The text clarifies that according to the Hanafī school, kaffārah is only obligatory (wājib) upon violating a 'mun'aqidah' (concluded) oath. It is not required for 'yamīn al-ghamūs' (false oath about the past) or 'yamīn al-laghw' (unintentional oath). The commentator explains two possible interpretations of the word 'faqaṭ' (only). One interpretation, which the commentator deems weak, is that 'only' means 'only kaffārah is due, not sin'—i.e., though kaffārah is wājib, one does not commit a sin when the violation is beneficial. This is supported by a ḥadīth reported in the Ṣaḥīḥayn: 'If you swear and see something better, do that and expiate.' However, the commentator notes that this is weak, because sometimes violation is prohibited, and then sin is incurred. The preferred interpretation is that 'only' restricts the obligation of kaffārah to the concluded oath, excluding the other types. This contrasts with the Shāfi'ī school, which holds kaffārah wājib in yamīn al-ghamūs as well, arguing that it involves deliberate lying like a concluded oath. The Hanafī refutation of this view is referenced as having been provided earlier in the text.

Key Terms
اليمين المنعقدة (al-yamīn al-mun'aqidah): An oath that is concluded and binding, sworn about a future action with intent and seriousness.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamīn al-ghamūs): An oath about a past event that the swearer knows to be false; a sinful lie.
اليمين اللغو (al-yamīn al-laghw): An unintentional oath made without deliberate intent, e.g., by mistake or in casual speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, in which type of oath is kaffārah wājib upon violation?
A) Al-yamīn al-ghamūs
B) Al-yamīn al-mun'aqidah
C) Al-yamīn al-laghw
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts kaffarah to concluded oaths, while Shafi'is extend it to false past oaths, making the distinction crucial for proper fatwa.

Kaffarah is Due Even in Forgetfulness or Coercion
Ruling that kaffarah is due even when oath or violation is forgetful or coerced

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that kaffarah is wajib upon violation of a mun'aqidah oath regardless of forgetfulness or coercion, and identify the three scenarios covered by this ruling.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is wajib even if oath or violation is forgetful or coerced.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a key principle in Hanafi oath law: the obligation of kaffarah (expiation) is not removed by forgetfulness (sahw) or coercion (ikrah). Whether one swears inadvertently or under duress, or violates the oath in such states, the expiation remains due. The commentator breaks down three possible combinations, all yielding the same ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling expressed
The text emphatically states that kaffarah is obligatory in all cases of violation of a mun'aqidah oath, even if forgetful or coerced.
2
Understand the two factors: forgetfulness and coercion
These are the two circumstances that some might think would remove liability, but the ruling rejects that notion.
3
Enumerate the three scenarios
The commentator lists: (a) both oath and violation are forgetful/coerced, (b) oath forgetful/coerced but violation intentional, (c) oath intentional but violation forgetful/coerced.
All three lead to the same ruling.
4
Grasp the principle
The underlying reason: the sanctity of Allah's name is compromised by the violation itself, regardless of the mental state or volition in the act.
5
Apply to a novel scenario
If someone is forced to swear not to eat, then forgets and eats, kaffarah is still due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولو سهواً أو كرهاً : حلف أو حنث ) ، يعني تجب الكفارة وإن كان الحلف بطريق السهو، أو بالإكراه.
وقوله: حلف أو حنث ; يعني تجب الكفارة في كل حال عند الحنث، سواء كان كل واحد من الحلف أو الحنث عمداً أو سهواً، طوعاً أو كرهاً، بأن أكرهه رجل على أن يحلف أو على أن يحنث، فهذا مشتمل على صور ثلاثة:
أحدها أن يكون كلّ من الحلف والحنث سهواً أو كرهاً.
وثانيها : أن يكون الحلف سهواً أو كرهاً، والحنث عمداً وطوعاً.
وثالثها : عكسه.
("And even if forgetfully or coercively: he swears or violates") means kaffārah is obligatory even if the swearing was by way of forgetfulness or coercion. And his statement: "he swears or violates" means kaffārah is obligatory in every case upon violation, whether each of the swearing or the violation was intentional or forgetful, voluntary or involuntary, such as a man compelling him to swear or to violate. This includes three scenarios: One: both the swearing and the violation are forgetful or coerced. Two: the swearing is forgetful or coerced, while the violation is intentional and voluntary. Three: the opposite [i.e., swearing intentional, violation forgetful or coerced].
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that establishes the obligation of kaffārah (expiation) in a concluded oath regardless of the circumstances of the oath or its violation. The text clarifies that kaffārah is wājib even if the swearing was done forgetfully (sahwan) or under coercion (ikrāhan). Similarly, the violation itself may be forgetful or coerced, yet kaffārah remains due. The commentator provides three illustrative scenarios: (1) both the swearing and the violation are forgetful or coerced; (2) the swearing is forgetful or coerced but the violation is intentional and voluntary; (3) the opposite—the swearing is intentional and voluntary but the violation is forgetful or coerced. In all cases, kaffārah must be performed. This ruling underscores that the harm to the sanctity of Allah's name occurs irrespective of the mental state or volition in the act, so compensation is required.

Key Terms
السهو (al-sahw): Unintentional forgetfulness; the act is done without conscious intent.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Coercion or duress that overcomes one's will; in this context, being forced to swear or violate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, is kaffārah obligatory if a person is forced at knifepoint to swear an oath and then also forced to violate it?
A) No, because coercion removes legal responsibility
B) Yes, kaffārah is obligatory
C) Only if the violation was voluntary
D) Only if the oath was intentional

Key Takeaway
Kaffārah is wājib upon any violation of a mun'aqidah oath, regardless of forgetfulness or coercion.

Expiation for Oaths Under Coercion or Forgetfulness: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute with al-Shafi'i on kaffarah for coerced/forgetful oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on expiation for coerced/forgetful oaths, identify the Shafi'i counterargument, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on dalil.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that coercion or forgetfulness does not remove the obligation of expiation for breaking an oath.

This lesson examines a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether an oath made under coercion or forgetfulness still requires expiation (kaffarah) if broken. The Hanafi position, based on the hadith 'Three things are serious in jest and serious' and qiyas, treats all such oaths as binding. The Shafi'i school, relying on intention-based hadiths, exempts the coerced and forgetful. We will trace the proof structures of both sides and see how the Hanafi response defends its reliance on stronger evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules: expiation is wajib for breaking any valid oath, regardless of whether the oath was sworn or broken intentionally, under coercion, or forgetfully.
The key phrase is 'the intentional, coerced, and forgetful are equal' (from al-Hidayah).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i holds that expiation is not required for the forgetful or coerced at the time of swearing or breaking, because intention is a prerequisite.
The Shafi'i proof includes the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and a hadith about the coerced.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the intention hadith is general, and the coerced hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni with a weak chain.
The Hanafis argue the intention hadith pertains to reward, not legal obligation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the coerced hadith is not authoritative due to weakness, and the intention hadith is misinterpreted. They rely on the hadith 'Three things are serious in jest and serious' to show that even jest (which lacks full intent) is binding, so coercion is even more binding.
Two objections to the Hanafi hadith are mentioned: authenticity and applicability. The passage cuts off during the response.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Muslim who breaks an oath under coercion must pay expiation according to Hanafis, but not according to Shafi'is.
This affects actual practice for those following each school.

Source Text

Book of Oaths / Its definition and divisions. 'And if unintentionally or under coercion: he swore or broke the oath.' (1) He only said: 'only' to avoid the school of al-Shafi'i, (may Allah have mercy on him), regarding expiation in the false oath. 11. ('And if unintentionally or under coercion: he swore or broke the oath') means expiation is obligatory even if the oath was made unintentionally or under coercion. One of them: that it relates to his saying 'expiate,' and it indicates that in the bound oath, only expiation is obligatory, not sin upon breaking it, for breaking may be recommended or obligatory so he does not sin by it; only expiation is obligatory as a penalty for violating the sanctity of Allah's name. This is indicated by his saying: 'If you swear an oath and then see something better, then do that which is better and expiate for your oath.' (2) There are different wordings in the six authentic collections and others. You know the weakness of this interpretation, though some of the researchers inclined toward it. For often breaking the oath is prohibited by law in the bound oath, i.e., when the alternative is not better; then fulfilling it is obligatory and breaking it is prohibited or disliked, and at that point, breaking it incurs sin without doubt. Rather, one could say: the original rule is that breaking an oath is sinful, and it only deviates (from sin) due to an intervening factor. The second interpretation: it is connected to the pronoun in his saying 'in it,' and this is what the commentator chose. According to this, the meaning is that obligation of expiation is only in the bound oath, not in the other two types, i.e., the false oath and the vain oath. The commentator only mentioned the false oath because al-Shafi'i held expiation obligatory in it, while its obligation in the vain oath is merely a possibility that no one held. [1] His statement: 'to avoid the school of al-Shafi'i' means he held expiation obligatory in the false oath as well, because it shares with the bound oath in intentional falsehood, and what refutes this has passed previously. [2] His statement: 'he swore or broke' means expiation is obligatory in every case upon breaking, regardless of whether each of the swearing and the breaking was intentional or unintentional, willingly or under coercion, such as if a man forced him to swear or to break the oath. This includes three scenarios: First: both swearing and breaking are unintentional or under coercion. Second: swearing is unintentional or under coercion, but breaking is intentional and willing. (1) See: Asna al-Matalib (4:240), Mughni al-Muhtaj (4:325), etc. (2) In Sahih Muslim (3:1268) and Sahih al-Bukhari (6:2444), etc. Contrary to al-Shafi'i. Third: the opposite. And he said in al-Hidayah: 'The intentional in the oath, the coerced, and the forgetful are equal.' [2] [1] His statement: 'Contrary to al-Shafi'i' means in his school, expiation is not obligatory for the forgetful and the coerced at the time of swearing or breaking, due to the hadith: 'Actions are by intentions' (3) and the hadith: 'There is no oath for the coerced' (4) narrated by al-Daraqutni with a very weak chain, as mentioned by Ibn Hajar in Talkhis al-Habir. (5) The response to this is that the hadith of the coerced is not authoritative due to weakness in its chain, and the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' is understood to pertain to the dependence of reward on intention, as discussed previously in the Book of Purification. [2] His statement: 'equal' - they relied for equality on the hadith: 'Three things are serious in jest and serious: marriage, divorce, and oath' (6), which establishes that jest is like seriousness in the matter of oaths. So if an oath is contracted through jest, then it is more likely to be contracted under coercion because the one jesting does not intend to execute the ruling of what he utters, while the coerced does intend it, even if due to others' coercion. As for forgetfulness, it is like jest. It was objected to in two ways: First, this hadith mentioning oath is not established; rather, only what the compilers of the four Sunan narrated, and al-Tirmidhi deemed good, and al-Hakim authenticated: 'Marriage, divorce, and return.' In Ibn 'Adi's narration, 'emancipation' instead of 'return.' This is in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Wiqayah by 'Ali al-Qari. Second, as in al-Fath: 'Even if the hadith of oath were established, it would not be evidence because what is mentioned is making jest in oath serious, and the one jesting intends the oath but is not content with its ruling, so the lack of consent is not considered...'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the obligation of expiation (kaffarah) for oaths made under coercion or forgetfulness. The author begins by defining the types of oaths and then focuses on the ruling that expiation is obligatory even if the oath was made unintentionally or under coercion. The commentator explains that this is the Hanafi position, distinguishing it from al-Shafi'i who requires expiation for the false oath (al-yamin al-ghamus) as well. The core of the dispute is whether intention (niyyah) is a condition for the obligation of expiation. Al-Shafi'i relies on the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and a weak hadith 'There is no oath for the coerced' to exempt the forgetful and coerced from expiation. The Hanafis respond that the first hadith relates to reward, not legal obligation, and the second is too weak to be authoritative. Instead, they rely on the hadith 'Three things are serious in jest and serious: marriage, divorce, and oath' to establish that jest (which lacks full intent) is binding in oaths, so coercion (which has more intent) is even more binding. The commentary then presents two objections to this hadith: first, that the wording including 'oath' is not authentic; second, that even if authentic, it does not prove the point because the one jesting intends the oath but does not consent to its legal effect, while the coerced does not consent either. The passage cuts off mid-response, but the Hanafi position is clear: expiation is obligatory in all cases of breaking a valid oath, regardless of intent or coercion.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath or vow invoking Allah's name to do or refrain from something.
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking or violating an oath by doing what one swore not to do, or vice versa.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath, typically feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.
المنعقدة (al-mun'aqidah): The bound oath, i.e., a valid oath where one swears to do or not do something in the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on expiation (kaffarah) for breaking an oath made under coercion?
A) Expiation is not required because the oath was made involuntarily.
B) Expiation is required, just as for an oath made intentionally.
C) Expiation is recommended but not obligatory.
D) Expiation is required only if the coercion was severe.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, coercion or forgetfulness does not remove the obligation of expiation for breaking a valid oath.

Equality of Qasid, Mukrah, and Nasi in Oath Rulings
Establishes equality of intent, compulsion, and forgetfulness in oath rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kaffarah for a broken oath made under compulsion or forgetfulness, and explain the proof and the difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, kaffarah is due even if the oath was made under compulsion or forgetfully — the utterance alone suffices.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle in the law of oaths: that the obligation of kaffarah depends on the utterance of the oath, not the mental state of the swearer. The passage from al-Hidayah states that the qasid (intending person), mukrah (compelled), and nasi (forgetful) are all equal. We will explore the Hanafi proof, the Shafi'i objection, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its scope
Identify the ruling: kaffarah is wajib for anyone who breaks an oath, regardless of whether they intended it, were compelled, or forgot. The ruling applies to all three categories equally.
The passage opens with 'القاصد في اليمين والمُكْرَهُ والنَّاسى سواء'.
2
Identify the primary proof
The main Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'ثلاث جدهن جد، وهزلهن جد: النكاح والطلاق واليمين' (Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and the oath). This establishes that jest in oaths is as effective as serious intent.
The Hanafi scholars use qiyas evlevi (superior analogy): if jest is binding, then compulsion is even more so.
3
Analyze the qiyas and its extension
Apply the qiyas: the jester (hazil) does not intend to execute the ruling but intended the utterance. The compelled person (mukrah) also intends the utterance, though not freely. Since the hazil case is binding, the mukrah case is even stronger. Similarly, forgetfulness (nasi) is like jest—no real intent, but utterance binds.
The commentator addresses two objections: (1) the hadith's chain is weak, (2) the forgetful never intended the cause. But the Hanafi response upholds the ruling based on other principles.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i view and understand the response
The Shafi'i school argues that kaffarah is not due for the compelled or forgetful because of the hadith 'لا يمين لمقهور' and the principle 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات'. The Hanafi response: the first hadith is weak; the second concerns reward, not legal obligation.
This is a genuine ikhtilaf with practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).
