خلافاً للشَّافِعِي وثالثها : عكسه. وقال في «الهداية»: القاصد في اليمين والمُكْرَهُ والنَّاسى سواء.

[١] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ فإنّ عنده لا يجب الكفارة في الناسي والمكره عند الحلف، أو عند الحنث ؛ لحديث : «إنّما الأعمال بالنيات»، وحديث : «ليس على مقهور يمين»، أخرجه الدارقطني بسند ضعيف جداً، كما ذكره ابنُ حَجَر في «تلخيص الحبير». والجواب عنه : أنَّ حديث المقهور مما لا يحتج به ، فإنّ في سنده ضعفاً، وحديث : «إنما الأعمال بالنيات محمول على توقف الثواب على النية، كما مر بحثه في «كتاب الطهارة».

[٢]قوله : سواء ؛ استدلوا للتسوية بحديث : «ثلاث جدهن جد، وهزلهن جد: النكاح والطلاق واليمين»، فإنّه يثبتُ كونُ الهزل مثل الجدّ في باب اليمين، فإذا انعقد اليمين بالهزل فلأن ينعقد بالإكراه أولى ؛ لأنَّ الهازل لا يقصد إجراء حكم ما يتلفظ به، والمكره يقصده، وإن كان بإكراه الغير، وأمّا السهو فهو مثل الهزل.
واعترض عليه بوجهين : أحدهما : إنّ هذا الحديث بذكر اليمين غير ثابت، وإنما الثابت ما أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة، وحسّنه الترمذي، وصححه الحاكم : «النكاح والطلاق والرجعة»، وفي رواية ابن عدي : «العتاق» بدل «الرجعة». كذا في «شرح مختصر الوقاية» لعلي القاري.
وثانيهما : ما في «الفتح»: «لو ثبت حديث اليمين لم يكن فيه دليل ؛ لأنّ المذكور فيه جعل الهزل باليمين جداً، والهازلُ قاصد لليمين غير راض بحكمه، فلا يعتبر عدم رضاه به شرعاً بعد مباشرة السبب مختاراً، والناسي لم يقصد شيئاً أصلاً، ولم يدر ما صنع، وكذا المخطيء، لم يقصد قط التلفظ به، بل بشيء آخر، فلا يكون الوارد في الهازل، وارد في الناسي الذي لم يقصد قط مباشرة السبب، فلا يثبت في حقه نصاً ولا قياساً». انتهى.

[1] قوله : الساهي ؛ هاهنا ثلاثة ألفاظ السهو، والنسيان، والخطأ. فالخطأ : عبارة عن أن يجري اليمين على لسانه من غير قصد، كما إذا أراد أن يقول: سبحان الله، فجرى على لسانه : والله لا أُصلي. والخطأ في الحنث أن يقع منه الحنث خطاً ، كما إذا حلف لا يشرب الماء، فدخل الماء في حلقه من غير قصد عند المضمضة.
والأولان متحدان لغة، ويفرّق بينهما اصطلاحاً بأنّ السهو زوال الصورة عن المدركة ، بقائها في الخزانة، فيحصل للساهي التذكّر بأدنى تنبيه، والنسيان زوالها عن مع كليهما، فيحتاج حينئذ في حصولها إلى سبب جديد.
وقيل : النسيان عدم ذكر ما كان مذكوراً ، والسهو غفلة عما كان مذكوراً، وما لم يكن مذكوراً، فعلى هذا النسيان أخص من السهو، وقيل : النسيان والغفلة عبارة عن زوالِ إدراكِ سابق إذا قصر زمان ،زواله، فإن طال فهو نسيان، كذا في «شرح تحرير الأصول» الشمس الدين محمد المعروف بابن أمير حاج الحلبي، مؤلف «حلبة المجلي شرح منية المصلي» وغيره. إذا عرفت هذا كله فاعلم أنهم صرّحوا بأن الحلف والحنت خاطئاً وساهياً وناسياً كله سواء في وجوب الكفّارة، بناءً على أنَّ الحكم يدار على السبب، وهو التكلّم بلفظ اليمين.
Contrary to al-Shafi'i. And a third view: its opposite. He said in al-Hidayah: The one who intends in the oath, the one compelled, and the forgetful are equal. [1] His statement: contrary to al-Shafi'i; because according to him, kaffarah is not obligatory for the forgetful and compelled when swearing or when breaking the oath, due to the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and the hadith 'There is no oath upon one who is coerced,' reported by al-Daraqutni with a very weak chain, as mentioned by Ibn Hajar in Talkhis al-Habir. The response: The hadith of the coerced is not authoritative because its chain is weak, and the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' is understood to mean that reward depends on intention, as previously discussed in the Book of Purification. [2] His statement: equal; they used as proof the hadith: 'Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and the oath.' Thus it establishes that jest is like seriousness in the chapter of oaths. If an oath is contracted by jest, then it is even more so by compulsion, because the jester does not intend to execute the ruling of what he utters, while the compelled one does intend it, albeit by another's compulsion. As for forgetfulness, it is like jest. It was objected on two grounds: First, this hadith mentioning the oath is not established; rather, what is established is what the authors of the four Sunan reported, which al-Tirmidhi hasan and al-Hakim sahih: 'Marriage, divorce, and return.' In a narration of Ibn 'Adi, 'emancipation' is in place of 'return.' Thus in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Wiqaya by 'Ali al-Qari. Second, what is in al-Fath: 'If the hadith of the oath were established, it would not be a proof, because what is mentioned in it is making jest in an oath as serious. The jester intends the oath but is not pleased with its ruling, so his lack of pleasure is not considered legally after directly undertaking the cause while choosing. The forgetful did not intend anything at all and did not know what he did. Likewise the mistaken one never intended to utter it but rather something else. Thus what applies to the jester does not apply to the forgetful who never intended to directly undertake the cause, so it is not established in his case by text or analogy.' End. [1] His statement: the forgetful; here there are three terms: sahw, nisyan, and khata. Khata is that the oath runs on his tongue unintentionally, as if he wanted to say 'Subhan Allah' but his tongue ran to 'By Allah, I will not pray.' Khata in the breaking is that breaking occurs mistakenly, as if he swore not to drink water, then water entered his throat unintentionally during rinsing. The first two are synonymous linguistically, but distinguished terminologically: sahw is the removal of the image from perception while it remains in the memory, so the sahw person can recall with the slightest reminder; nisyan is the removal from both, so it requires a new cause to retrieve it. It is also said: nisyan is the absence of what was remembered, and sahw is heedlessness of what was remembered and what was not. According to this, nisyan is more specific than sahw. It is also said: nisyan and heedlessness are the removal of a previous perception if the time of its removal is short; if it is long, it is nisyan. Thus in Sharh Tahrir al-Usul by Shams al-Din Muhammad known as Ibn Amir Haj al-Halabi, author of Halbat al-Mujalli Sharh Munyat al-Musalli and others. Once you understand all this, know that they have stated clearly that swearing and breaking mistakenly, forgetfully, and negligently are all equal in the obligation of kaffarah, based on the principle that the ruling revolves around the cause, which is the utterance of the oath word.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding oaths. The opening states the Hanafi position: the one who intends (qasid), the one compelled (mukrah), and the forgetful (nasi) are all equal in the obligation of kaffarah when the oath is broken. It then addresses the Shafi'i opposing view, which holds that no kaffarah is due in cases of compulsion or forgetfulness. The Shafi'i proofs are two hadiths: "Actions are by intentions" and "No oath upon one coerced." The Hanafi response explains that the second hadith is too weak to be authoritative, and the first pertains only to reward, not to legal obligation. The main Hanafi proof is the hadith: "Three things, their seriousness is serious, and their jest is serious: marriage, divorce, and the oath." This establishes that jest in oaths is as effective as serious intent. The commentator then applies qiyas awwala (superior analogy): if an oath is binding in jest, it is even more so under compulsion, because the coerced person actually intends the utterance (unlike the jester who does not). However, two objections are raised against this hadith: first, the wording including 'oath' is not reliably transmitted; the authentic version mentions only marriage, divorce, and return. Second, even if proven, the hadith would not cover forgetfulness or error, because the jester at least intends the utterance, whereas the forgetful person intends nothing. Despite these objections, the commentator concludes that the Hanafi ruling stands, reinforced by the principle that the legal effect depends on the cause—the utterance of the oath wording. The passage then defines the three terms: khata' (error) occurs when the oath slips unintentionally, sahw (heedlessness) is temporary loss of the image, and nisyan (forgetfulness) is permanent loss. Terminological distinctions are given from various sources, but the final fatwa reaffirms that all three are equally liable for kaffarah because the ruling is tied to the utterance itself.

Key Terms
القاصد (al-qāṣid): The one who intentionally swears an oath, fully intending both the utterance and its legal effect.
المُكْرَهُ (al-mukrah): One who is compelled by another to swear an oath against his will.
النَّاسى (al-nāsī): One who forgets; in this context, one who swears or breaks an oath without being aware of it.
السَّهو (al-sahw): Heedlessness or temporary forgetfulness where the image leaves perception but remains in memory.
الخَطَأ (al-khaṭa’): Error: when the oath or its break occurs unintentionally, e.g., a slip of the tongue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is kaffarah obligatory for a person who breaks an oath made under compulsion (mukrah)?
A) Yes, because the ruling depends on the utterance, not the intention.
B) No, because compulsion negates the validity of the oath.
C) Only if the person intended the oath inwardly.
D) It depends on the severity of the compulsion.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation of kaffarah for a broken oath is triggered by the mere utterance of the oath, regardless of compulsion, forgetfulness, or mistake.

Effect of Mistake and Forgetfulness on Oaths: The Hanafi Position on Kaffārah
Defines nāsī, sāhī, khāṭiʼ and disputes Shāfi'ī on kaffārah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires kaffārah for oaths made by mistake or forgetfulness, and evaluate the Shāfi'ī counter-argument based on the hadith of pardon.

Core Idea
Kaffārah for a broken oath is wajib even if the oath was made mistakenly – the ruling is tied to uttering the oath, not intent.

When an oath is uttered inadvertently, forgetfully, or by mistake, does the oath still bind? The Hanafi school answers yes: kaffārah is wajib. But Imam al-Shāfi'ī disagrees, citing a well-known hadith. In this lesson, we will examine the definitions of nāsī, sāhī, and khāṭiʼ, understand the Hanafi ruling and its dalīl, and analyse the inter-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Define the three terms: nāsī, sāhī, khāṭiʼ
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions provided in the passage for these three categories of unintentional oath-making.
The passage distinguishes between inadvertence (sahw) and forgetfulness (nisyān) technically, though both lead to the same ruling.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on kaffārah
State clearly that in the Hanafi school, kaffārah is wajib for all three categories because the ruling revolves around the cause (sabab) – uttering the oath.
3
Identify the Shāfi'ī objection and its dalīl
Imam al-Shāfi'ī uses the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' to argue that oaths made by mistake or forgetfulness do not bind at all.
The hadith is cited to remove not only sin but also legal consequences according to Shāfi'ī.
4
Examine the Hanafi response and its evidence
The majority (jumhūr) respond that the hadith only lifts sin (ithm), not legal consequences. They support this with qiyas: kaffārah for accidental killing and compensation for property damage are not lifted by this hadith.
This response shows that the hadith must be interpreted in light of other sharia evidences.
5
Analyse the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: In Hanafi, a mistaken oath still binds and requires kaffārah; in Shāfi'ī, no kaffārah is due because the oath is not valid.
This difference directly affects the legal obligations of the individual.

[1] قوله : الساهي ؛ هاهنا ثلاثة ألفاظ السهو، والنسيان، والخطأ. فالخطأ : : عبارة عن أن يجري اليمين على لسانه من غير قصد، كما إذا أراد أن يقول: سبحان الله، فجرى على لسانه : والله لا أُصلي. والخطأ في الحنث أن يقع منه الحنث خطاً ، كما إذا حلف لا يشرب الماء، فدخل الماء في حلقه من غير قصد عند المضمضة. والأولان متحدان لغة، ويفرّق بينهما اصطلاحاً بأنّ السهو زوال الصورة عن المدركة ، بقائها في الخزانة، فيحصل للساهي التذكّر بأدنى تنبيه، والنسيان زوالها عن مع كليهما، فيحتاج حينئذ في حصولها إلى سبب جديد. وقيل : النسيان عدم ذكر ما كان مذكوراً ، والسهو غفلة عما كان مذكوراً، وما لم يكن مذكوراً، فعلى هذا النسيان أخص من السهو، وقيل : النسيان والغفلة عبارة عن زوالِ إدراكِ سابق إذا قصر زمان ،زواله، فإن طال فهو نسيان، كذا في «شرح تحرير الأصول» (۳) الشمس الدين محمد المعروف بابن أمير حاج الحلبي، مؤلف «حلبة المجلي شرح منية المصلي» وغيره. إذا عرفت هذا كله فاعلم أنهم صرّحوا بأن الحلف والحنت خاطئاً وساهياً وناسياً كله سواء في وجوب الكفّارة، بناءً على أنَّ الحكم يدار على السبب، وهو التكلّم بلفظ اليمين. Book of Oaths: Its Definition and Divisions. (1) [1] 13 And what is meant by al-nāsī: al-sāhī, which is the one who swore without intention, as it is said: 'Will you not come to us?' and he said: 'Yes, by Allah,' without intending the oath. Accepting it legally after directly performing the cause willingly. And al-nāsī did not intend anything at all and did not know what he did. Likewise, al-mukhtiʼ never intended to utter it, but rather something else. So what is mentioned concerning al-hāzil (the jester) is not applicable to al-nāsī who never intended to directly perform the cause. Thus it is not established in his case by text (naṣṣ) or analogy (qiyās). End (2). [1] His statement: al-sāhī; here are three terms: al-sahw (inadvertence), al-nisyān (forgetfulness), and al-khaṭaʼ (mistake). Al-khaṭaʼ: it is that the oath flows on his tongue without intention, such as when he intends to say 'Subḥān Allāh' but his tongue utters 'By Allah, I will not pray.' And al-khaṭaʼ in al-ḥinth (breaking the oath) is that the ḥinth occurs from him mistakenly, such as when he swears not to drink water, and water enters his throat unintentionally while rinsing the mouth. The first two [al-sahw and al-nisyān] are identical in language, but are distinguished technically: al-sahw is the removal of the image from perception while it remains in the storehouse, so the sāhī can recall with minimal prompting, while al-nisyān is its removal from both, so it requires a new cause for its retrieval. It is said: al-nisyān is failure to remember what was remembered, and al-sahw is heedlessness of what was remembered and what was not remembered. Thus al-nisyān is more specific than al-sahw. And it is said: al-nisyān and al-ghafla are expressions for the removal of a prior perception if the duration of its removal is short; if it is long, it is nisyān. This is in 'Sharḥ Taḥrīr al-Uṣūl' (3) by Shams al-Dīn Muḥammad known as Ibn Amīr Ḥāj al-Ḥalabī, author of 'Ḥulbat al-Mujallī Sharḥ Munyat al-Muṣallī' and others. When you have understood all this, know that they explicitly stated that swearing and breaking an oath while being khāṭiʼ, sāhī, or nāsī are all equal in the obligation of kaffārah (expiation), based on the fact that the ruling revolves around the cause (sabab), which is uttering the wording of the oath. (1) Many [scholars] firmly held the unity of al-sahw and al-nisyān because the language does not differentiate between them, and many scholars differentiated between them, and there is subtle discussion that would take long if mentioned. It is good to refer to al-Taqrīr wa al-Taḥbīr (2:177), Radd al-Mukhtār (3:49), 'Umdat al-Ri'āya (2:223), and others. (2) From Fatḥ al-Qadīr (5:64). (3) al-Taqrīr wa al-Taḥbīr fī Sharḥ al-Taḥrīr (2:117). 'Umdat al-Ri'āya 'alā Sharḥ al-Wiqāya 12 The utmost that can be said is that sin is lifted from al-sāhī, al-nāsī, and al-khāṭiʼ in al-ḥinth, because sin is contingent upon intention. This is supported by the ḥadīth: 'My ummah is pardoned for mistake, forgetfulness, and what they are forced to do' – reported by Ibn Mājah, Ibn Ḥibbān, al-Dāraquṭnī, al-Bayhaqī, al-Ṭabarānī, al-Ḥākim and others with similar wordings, and al-Nawawī in his 'Arba'īn' deemed its chain good. Al-Shāfi'ī used it as evidence for his view that divorce, manumission, and the binding of oaths do not occur when done by mistake, forgetfulness, or coercion. The majority (al-jumhūr) responded that what is meant by it is the lifting of sin, not the lifting of other consequences. Do you not see that Allāh (peace be upon him) obligated kaffārah upon the one who kills by mistake, and the people of knowledge ruled for the obligation of compensation (ḍamān) upon one who destroys another's property forgetfully, inadvertently, or mistakenly. After all that, we say: Many of them interpreted al-nāsī occurring in al-Hidāyah as al-sāhī. This is either because al-sahw and al-nisyān are identical in language, or because forgetfulness is not conceivable in swearing. But it is not hidden that this is problematic, because one can first swear by saying 'By Allah, I will not swear' and then forget it entirely, and thus swear regarding something else, or be heedless of it inadvertently. In summary: each of al-sahw and al-nisyān can be conceived in both swearing and breaking an oath, just as al-khaṭaʼ can be conceived in both. So there is no need to interpret al-nāsī as al-sāhī. And some of them interpreted it as al-khāṭiʼ, based on the fact that in some copies of al-Hidāyah, 'al-khāṭiʼ' occurs in place of 'al-nāsī'. The commentator followed the earlier interpreters, so he interpreted al-nāsī as al-sāhī, and then interpreted it as one who swears without intention. There are objections to this: First, because there is no need to interpret al-nāsī as al-sāhī, as previously mentioned; rather it is better to leave it with its meaning. It cannot be said that his interpretation is based on their linguistic unity, because we say: then there is no need to clarify the intended meaning, unless it is said that al-sahw is used more frequently in its meaning unlike al-nisyān, so he interpreted it accordingly for clarification. Second, because what he interpreted al-sāhī as does not correspond to what they interpreted and exemplified it with. Rather the better [approach] is
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya, a commentary on Sharḥ al-Wiqāya, deals with the definitions and rulings concerning oaths made inadvertently, forgetfully, or mistakenly. The commentator begins by stating that what is meant by 'al-nāsī' (the forgetful one) is 'al-sāhī' (the inadvertent one) – one who swears without intention. He then clarifies that there are three terms: al-sahw (inadvertence), al-nisyān (forgetfulness), and al-khaṭaʼ (mistake). These are defined linguistically and technically. Al-khaṭaʼ is when the oath flows from the tongue unintentionally, or when the oath is broken unintentionally. Al-sahw and al-nisyān are linguistically identical but technically distinguished: in al-sahw the image is removed from immediate perception but remains in the memory, so the person can recall quickly; in al-nisyān it is removed from both, requiring a new cause to recall. After these definitions, the passage states the ruling: swearing and breaking an oath while being khāṭiʼ, sāhī, or nāsī are all equal in the obligation of kaffārah (expiation). The reason is that the ruling revolves around the cause (sabab), which is uttering the oath. Thus, even if done without intent, the oath is still binding and kaffārah is wajib. The passage then addresses an inter-madhhab dispute. Imam al-Shāfi'ī cited the ḥadīth 'My ummah is pardoned for mistake, forgetfulness, and what they are forced to do' to argue that divorce, manumission, and oaths do not occur when done mistakenly or forgetfully. The majority (jumhūr), which includes the Hanafī school, responded that the ḥadīth only lifts sin (ithm), not other consequences like monetary compensation or kaffārah. They provide examples: Allāh obligated kaffārah for accidental killing, and scholars rule that if someone destroys another's property by mistake, they must compensate. So in the Hanafī school, a mistaken oath still requires kaffārah. The passage concludes with a critique of the commentator's interpretation of 'nāsī' in the original text (al-Hidāyah) as 'sāhī'. It argues that both forgetfulness and inadvertence can occur in both swearing and breaking an oath, so there is no need to reinterpret nāsī as sāhī. The commentator's approach is deemed unnecessary; the original term should be left as is.

Key Terms
الناسي (al-nāsī): The one who swears without any intention at all, and does not know what he did; forgetfulness of the oath.
الساهي (al-sāhī): One who swears without intention; inadvertent. In technical terms, the image is removed from immediate perception but remains in memory.
الخاطئ (al-khāṭiʼ): One who makes a mistake: either uttering the oath unintentionally (e.g., intending to say Subḥān Allāh but saying an oath) or breaking the oath unintentionally (e.g., water entering throat unintentionally).
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation required for breaking a valid oath; in this context, it is wajib even if the oath was made mistakenly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, if someone swears by mistake (khāṭiʼ) and later breaks that oath, what is the ruling?
A) No kaffārah is required because the oath was not intended.
B) Kaffārah is wajib, because the ruling revolves around the cause of uttering the oath.
C) The oath is invalid and no action is needed.
D) Only Allah forgives; no legal ruling applies.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation of kaffārah for a broken oath is attached to the act of uttering the oath, regardless of intent, based on the principle that legal effects follow the cause (sabab).

Kafarah for Breaking an Oath: Forgetfulness and Compulsion Do Not Remove Obligation
Ruling that kafarah is due even for unintentional oath-breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that kafarah is obligatory when an oath is broken through forgetfulness or compulsion, and explain the rational proof that the actual act triggers the obligation regardless of intent.

Core Idea
The actual act of breaking the oath triggers kafarah; lack of intent does not cancel the obligation.

In this passage, the commentator on Al-Wiqayah explains a key principle: even if you break an oath unintentionally—through forgetfulness, compulsion, fainting, or insanity—you still owe expiation (kafarah). The reason is that the real, physical act of breaking occurred; your mental state does not erase that act. However, if the act was completely beyond your control, such as someone forcing water down your throat, then no kafarah is due. Let's unpack the legal reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on al-hinth (oath-breaking) occurring through sahw (forgetfulness), ikrah (compulsion), ighma' (fainting), or junun (insanity). The ruling: kafarah is wajib (obligatory) in all these cases.
The key is that kafarah is tied to the existence of the act, not the mental state.
2
Analyze the proof
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): 'because the actual act is not negated by forgetfulness or compulsion.' The existence of the act (al-fil al-haqiqi) is the basis for obligation.
This proof relies on the principle that legal causes are attached to objective realities, not subjective states.
3
Distinguish kafarah from sin
The passage notes that while kafarah is due, sin (ithm) may be absent in cases like compulsion. The obligation of kafarah does not automatically imply sin.
This is important: rulings can have different underlying reasons (ahkam vs. ithm).
4
Understand the exception
If the act is not performed by the swearer at all (e.g., water forcibly poured by another), no kafarah is owed. The act must be attributable to the swearer's own action.
This shows that the 'existence of the act' means the swearer's own act, not just any occurrence.
5
Apply to a subtle example
The passage gives an example: swearing not to swear, then forgetfully swearing. The forgetfulness attaches to the original oath, making the new oath a breaking of it.
This refines the concept of 'actual act' in complex scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا إن كان الحنث بطرق السهو والإكراه تجب ; لأن الفعل الحقيقي لا يعدمه السهو والإكراه، وكذا الإغماء والجنون، فتجب الكفارة بالحنث كيفما كان. والقسم بالله أو باسم من أسمائه : كالرحمن، والرحيم، والحق إدخال الحلف من غير قصد في الخطأ، وصورة السهو في الحلف أن يحلف بأن لا يحلف، فيعرض له السهو عنه، فيحلف بشيء سهوا، إلا أن يقال : تعلق السهو أو النسيان به في هذه الصورة، إنما هو من جهة كونه حنثا للحلف السابق، لا من جهة كونه يمينا، وبهذا تتبين صحة الوجه الثاني الذي ذكرنا من وجهي تفسير الناسي بالساهي، فافهم هذا المقام، فإنه من مزال الأقدام.
[1] قوله : لأن الفعل الحقيقي....الخ ; حاصله : إن وجوب الكفارة مترتب شرعا على وجود الحنث بفعله، وهو لا ينعدم بكونه سهوا، أو إكراها، أو حالة الجنون، أو حالة الإغماء، فكلما يوجد الحنث بفعله يترتب عليه وجوب الكفارة، ولا ينافي ذلك عدم لزوم الإثم في بعض الصور. وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لو لم يوجد الحنث بفعله لا يجب عليه شيء، كما إذا حلف لا يشرب الماء، فصبه رجل في حلقه إكراها، صرح به في النهر.
And similarly, if the oath-breaking occurs through forgetfulness or compulsion, it is obligatory (to pay expiation) because the actual act is not negated by forgetfulness or compulsion. Similarly, fainting and insanity, so expiation is obligatory by the oath-breaking however it occurs. (And the oath by Allah or by one of His names: like Ar-Rahman, Ar-Rahim, and Al-Haqq) Insertion of the oath without intention into error, and the form of forgetfulness in the oath is that he swears not to swear, then forgetfulness occurs to him regarding it, so he swears something forgetfully. Except it may be said: The attachment of forgetfulness or forgetfulness to it in this form is only from the aspect of it being a breaking of the previous oath, not from the aspect of it being an oath. And by this, the validity of the second aspect which we mentioned from the two aspects of interpreting 'al-nasi' as 'al-sahi' becomes clear. So understand this station, for it is a slippery ground. [1] His saying: 'Because the actual act...etc.'; its summary: The obligation of expiation is legally attached to the existence of the oath-breaking by his act, and it is not negated by it being forgetful, or compelled, or a state of insanity, or a state of fainting. So whenever the oath-breaking exists by his act, the obligation of expiation follows, and this does not negate the lack of sin in some forms. And in it is an indication that if the oath-breaking did not occur by his act, nothing is obligatory upon him, such as if he swore not to drink water, and a man poured it into his throat forcibly; this was explicitly stated in 'al-Nahr' (1).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. It addresses the obligation of kafarah (expiation) for breaking an oath, even when the breaking occurs through forgetfulness (sahw), compulsion (ikrah), fainting (ighma'), or insanity (junun). The key principle is that the actual physical act of breaking the oath (al-fil al-haqiqi) is not negated by these conditions; therefore, the legal obligation of kafarah remains. However, the author notes that this does not necessarily entail sin (ithm) in some cases—for example, if someone is forced to break the oath, they may be excused from sin but still owe kafarah. The passage also gives a subtle example: if someone swears not to swear, then forgetfully makes another oath, the breaking is attributed to the earlier oath, not the new one. This clarifies that the ruling depends on the existence of the act itself, not on the mental state of the swearer. The commentary further explains that if the act of breaking did not occur at all (e.g., water forcibly poured into the mouth), no kafarah is owed.

Key Terms
الحنث (al-hinth): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do, or not doing what one swore to do.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness or inadvertence that leads to breaking an oath without deliberate intent.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion or coercion that forces one to break an oath against one's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person breaks an oath due to insanity, what is the ruling?
A) No kafarah is owed.
B) Kafarah is obligatory.
C) Only sin is forgiven, but kafarah is still due.
D) The oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Kafarah is due for breaking an oath even when done forgetfully, under compulsion, or in a state of insanity/fainting, because the actual act exists.

Rulings on Swearing by Divine Attributes and Others
Defines valid and invalid oaths by attributes and other entities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid oaths in Hanafi law, explaining the role of custom in determining oath validity for divine attributes, and state the evidence for prohibition of swearing by other than Allah.

Core Idea
Swearing by Allah's names is always an oath; attributes require custom; other swearing is prohibited and invalid.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies a crucial aspect of Hanafi oath law: what constitutes a valid oath (yamin)? The text explains that while swearing by Allah's names is always binding, swearing by divine attributes depends on common custom. It also firmly prohibits swearing by any created entity, citing the Prophet's command. By studying this, you will learn how Hanafi jurists balanced textual evidence with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on three categories: swearing by Allah's names, by His attributes (essence and action), and by other entities. Determine the legal status (valid or invalid) for each.
Note the hadith prohibits swearing by other than Allah, making it haram.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The hadith from Ibn Umar (Bukhari and Muslim) is cited: 'Indeed Allah forbids you to swear by your fathers, so whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or remain silent.' This is the dalil for prohibition.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (qati' al-dalalah).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition (نهاكم) indicates haram, and the command to swear only by Allah implies that any oath not by Allah is invalid. This is the direct link from text to ruling.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but here the command is after prohibition, so it indicates permissibility of swearing by Allah.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on attributes
Iraqi scholars restricted valid oaths to attributes of essence (e.g., power); Transoxianians included attributes of action (e.g., creation) if customary. Understand both positions.
The commentator sides with the Transoxianian view, making custom the criterion.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and practical outcome
The correct view (al-sahih) is that custom ('urf) determines whether an attribute is commonly used in oaths. This is the mufta bihi position. Apply this to phrases like swearing by the Quran.
Even though the Quran is an attribute of Allah, if someone intends the physical book, it is not an oath; but custom treats it as the speech, so it is valid.

لا بغير الله": كالنبي
[تعليق: قال في «العناية»: «المراد بالاسم هاهنا لفظ دال على الذاتِ الموصوفة، كالرحمن والرحيم، وبالصفة المصادر التي تحصل عن وصف الله، كالرحمة والعلم والعزّة).
[تعليق: ذكر مشايخ العراق أنَّ الحلف بصفاتِ الذات يمين، لا بصفات الفعل، وذكر مشايخ ما وراء النهر : إنّ الاعتبار للعرف، فكل صفة يحلف بها عرفا سواء كانت صفة الذات كالقدرة، أو صفة الفعل كالخلق والترزيق يكون الحلف بها يميناً، وما لا يحلف به عرفاً، سواءً كان صفة الذاتِ أو صفة الفعل لا يكون الحلفُ يميناً، وهذا هو الصحيح.
قوله : لا بغير الله ؛ بل يحرم الحلف بغير الله ؛ لحديث : «إنّ الله ينهاكم أن تحلفوا بآبائكم، فمن كان حالفاً فيحلف بالله أو ليسكت)، أخرجه الشيخان وأصحاب السنن إلا النسائي من حديث ابن عمر.
والقرآن، والكعبة .
ولا بصفة لا يُحْلَفُ بها عرفاً : كرحمته، وعلمه، ورضائه، وغضبه، وسخطه، وعذابه .
وقوله : لعمر الله، وأيم الله .
والحاصل : إن الحلف بأسماء الله لا يمين بلا شبهة، وفي الحلف بصفاته يعتبر العرف، والحلف بغير الله ليس بيمين مطلقاً.
قوله : والقرآن ؛ قال في «فتح القدير»: «لا يخفى أن الحلف بالقرآن الآن متعارف، فيكون يميناً». (انتهى)
و مبناه على أنَّ القرآن بمعنى كلام الله له من صفاته، فيعتبر فيه العرف، كالحلف بكلام الله، فمن مثل غير الأسماء وصفاته بالقرآن، فكأنه أراد به نفس المصحف، فإنّه ليس من صفاته، وأما القرآن سواء أريد به الكلام النفسي القديم، أو الألفاظ والحروف الحادثة، فهو من صفاته حالة.
قوله : وقوله...الخ ؛ هذا مبتدأ خبره قوله : فيما يأتي قسم، وأما قولُ الشارح ه فيما يأتي أن المبتدأ هو قوله : لعمر الله، فلا يخلو عن تسامح، فإن المبتدأ هو قوله : لعمر الله، لا قوله : لعمر الله، والعمر - بفتح العين وضمه – البقاء، ولا يستعمل في القسم إلا – بالفتح - لكونه محل التخفيف، فالمعنى بقاء الله، وهو مبتدأ وخبره محذوف؛ لدلالة جواب القسم عليه؛ أي لبقاء الله قسمي. قد استعمل الله له في كتابه هذا اللفظ في الحلف في سورة الحجر، حيث قال : لعمرُكَ إِنَّهُمْ لَفِي مَكْرَهُمْ يَعْمَهُونَ، وورد الحلف بـ ( أيم الله » في الحديث، حيث قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في شأن بعض أصحابه : «وأيم الله إن كان خليقاً للإمارة»، أخرجه البخاري.
Or by an attribute by which swearing is done from His attributes: like the might of Allah, and His majesty, and His greatness, and His power. Not by other than Allah: like the Prophet. [Commentary: He said in al-Inayah: 'The intended meaning of 'name' here is a word indicating the described essence, like al-Rahman and al-Rahim; and by 'attribute' the verbal nouns that arise from describing Allah, like mercy, knowledge, and might.'] [Commentary: The scholars of Iraq mentioned that swearing by attributes of essence is an oath, not by attributes of action; and the scholars of Transoxiana mentioned that consideration is for custom, so every attribute by which swearing is done customarily, whether it is an attribute of essence like power, or an attribute of action like creation and providing, swearing by it is an oath; and what is not sworn by custom, whether an attribute of essence or action, swearing by it is not an oath; and this is the correct view.] He said: Not by other than Allah; rather, it is forbidden to swear by other than Allah; due to the hadith: 'Indeed Allah forbids you to swear by your fathers, so whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or remain silent,' narrated by the two Shaykhs and the authors of the Sunan except al-Nasa'i from the hadith of Ibn Umar. And the Quran, and the Kaaba. And not by an attribute by which swearing is not done customarily: like His mercy, His knowledge, His pleasure, His anger, His wrath, and His punishment. And his saying: by the life of Allah, and by the oath of Allah. The conclusion: Swearing by the names of Allah is undoubtedly an oath; and in swearing by His attributes, custom is considered; and swearing by other than Allah is not an oath at all. He said: And the Quran; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'It is not hidden that swearing by the Quran nowadays is customary, so it is an oath.' (End) And its basis is that the Quran, meaning the speech of Allah, is from His attributes, so custom is considered in it, like swearing by the speech of Allah. So whoever likens other than names and attributes to the Quran, it is as if he intended the physical mushaf itself, for it is not from His attributes. As for the Quran, whether what is intended is the eternal inner speech, or the created words and letters, it is from His attributes in any case. He said: And his saying... etc.; this is a subject whose predicate is his saying later: an oath. As for the commentator's saying later that the subject is his saying 'by the life of Allah,' it is not free of oversight, for the subject is his saying 'by the life of Allah,' not his saying 'by the life of Allah.' And al-'umr (with fatha and damma on the 'ayn) means duration, and it is not used in oaths except with fatha because it is a place of lightening. So the meaning is 'by the duration of Allah,' and it is a subject with its predicate deleted due to the oath answer indicating it; i.e., 'by the duration of Allah is my oath.' Allah has used this phrase in swearing in His Book in Surah al-Hijr, where He said: 'By your life, indeed they are in their intoxication wandering blindly.' And swearing by 'aym Allah is narrated in the hadith, where the Prophet, peace be upon him, said regarding some of his companions: 'And by the oath of Allah, he was indeed fit for leadership,' narrated by al-Bukhari.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the rulings on oaths made by divine attributes and by entities other than Allah. The text first states the matn: one may swear by an attribute by which swearing is customarily practiced, such as Allah's might, majesty, greatness, and power. The commentary clarifies that 'name' refers to a word denoting the essence, while 'attribute' refers to verbal nouns that describe Allah. There is a disagreement between Iraqi and Transoxianian scholars: the Iraqis hold that only attributes of essence (like power) constitute oaths, while attributes of action (like creating) do not; the Transoxianians hold that custom is the criterion, so any attribute used in common swearing, whether of essence or action, is an oath. The correct view is the latter, relying on custom.

The matn then states that swearing by other than Allah, such as by a prophet, the Quran, or the Kaaba, is not a valid oath and is forbidden. The commentary reinforces this with a hadith from Sahihayn and Sunan (excluding al-Nasa'i) where the Prophet forbids swearing by fathers and commands swearing only by Allah or silence.

Regarding the Quran, the commentary notes that swearing by the Quran is now customary, so it is considered an oath. This is because the Quran, as Allah's speech, is an attribute of Allah; thus, it falls under the rule of attributes. If one intends the physical mushaf (the book), it is not an attribute, but the common custom is to swear by the Quran meaning the speech, so it is valid.

The phrases 'la'amr Allah' (by the life of Allah) and 'aym Allah' (by the oath of Allah) are also oaths. The commentary explains the grammatical construction: 'al-'umr' means duration, and the phrase implies 'by the duration of Allah is my oath.' Evidence is cited from Surah al-Hijr (15:72) where Allah says 'la'amrika' (by your life), and from a hadith where the Prophet said 'aym Allah.'

The overall conclusion: swearing by Allah's names is undoubtedly an oath; swearing by His attributes depends on custom; swearing by other than Allah is not an oath at all and is prohibited.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath that invokes Allah or His attributes to affirm or deny something; binding in Hanafi law.
صفات الذات (sifat al-dhat): Attributes that describe Allah's essence, such as power, might, and knowledge; swearing by them is valid.
صفات الفعل (sifat al-fi'l): Attributes that describe Allah's actions, such as creation and provision; swearing by them is valid if customary.
لعمر الله (la'amr Allah): A phrase meaning 'by the life/belief [؟] of Allah', used as an oath. 'Umr' means duration; the phrase is equivalent to swearing by Allah's attribute of duration.
أيم الله (aym Allah): A phrase meaning 'by the oath of Allah'; also a valid oath form. 'Aym' is derived from yamin (oath).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, what is the ruling on swearing by other than Allah?
A) It is permissible if the person does not mean it seriously.
B) It is forbidden and invalid as an oath.
C) It is valid as an oath but disliked.
D) It is valid if the person intends the oath bindingly.

Key Takeaway
The validity of oaths in Hanafi law hinges on the formula: swearing by Allah's names is always binding, by attributes is custom-dependent, and by other entities is prohibited and null.

Ruling on Oaths by Attributes and the Quran
States the ruling on swearing by Allah's attributes and the Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing by Allah's names, attributes, and other entities, and explain the role of custom (urf) in determining the validity of an oath.

Core Idea
Swearing by Allah's names always binds; by attributes depends on custom; by anything else is void.

This lesson addresses a common point of confusion: what counts as a valid oath? The Hanafi school provides a clear three-part classification based on whom or what you swear by. Understanding this distinction helps you know when an oath is binding and when it is not. The passage from the matn is concise but covers all categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of oaths
The passage mentions swearing by Allah's names, by His attributes, and by other than Allah. List these three.
2
Determine the ruling for each category
Names: valid without doubt. Attributes: validity depends on custom. Other: not an oath at all.
3
Apply custom assessment for attributes
Understand that for attributes, local custom (urf) decides whether the oath is valid. If people in that community commonly swear by that attribute, it is valid; otherwise, it is not.
Example: 'By Allah's anger' may be valid if customary.
4
Note the exception for the Quran
The Quran is considered an attribute of Allah (His speech). Swearing by it is valid because it is customary to do so in Muslim communities. The passage groups it with the names in terms of validity.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i does not require custom for swearing by the Quran because he treats it as an attribute of Allah unconditionally. Hanafis require custom. This affects fatwa in communities where swearing by the Quran is not common.
Not detailed in the passage but important for application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقرآن، والكعبة ولا بصفةٍ لا يُحْلَفُ بها عرفاً : كرحمته، وعلمه، ورضائه، وغضبه، وسخطه، وعذابه. والحاصل : إن الحلف بأسماء الله لا يمين بلا شبهة، وفي الحلف بصفاته يعتبر العرف ، والحلف بغير الله ليس بيمين مطلقاً.
And the Quran, and the Ka'bah, and not by an attribute that is not customarily sworn by: like His mercy, His knowledge, His pleasure, His anger, His displeasure, and His punishment. The conclusion: swearing by the names of Allah is an oath without any doubt, and swearing by His attributes is considered according to custom, and swearing by something other than Allah is not an oath at all.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage from the matn outlines the Hanafi ruling on what constitutes a valid oath. The key distinction is between swearing by Allah's names (which always forms a valid, binding oath) and swearing by His attributes (such as mercy or anger), which depends on whether it is customary in that community to swear by such attributes. Swearing by the Quran is considered valid because the Quran is an attribute of Allah—His speech. Swearing by the Ka'bah or other created entities does not constitute a valid oath. The 'without any doubt' phrase indicates that swearing by Allah's names is unequivocally an oath; there is no need to consider custom. This ruling is based on the principle that oaths must involve a being worthy of veneration (ta'zim), which is exclusively Allah and His attributes.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): In this passage, custom (urf) determines whether swearing by an attribute of Allah constitutes a valid oath.
صفة (sifah): An attribute of Allah (e.g., mercy, knowledge, anger) as distinct from His names. Swearing by attributes is subject to custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The Ka'bah
B) Allah's mercy
C) The Quran
D) A ruler's life

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between swearing by Allah's names (always valid), by His attributes (valid if customary), and by other entities (invalid).

The Validity of Oaths by 'La'amrullah' and 'Aymullah'
Explains the validity of swearing by 'La'amrullah' and 'Aymullah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of oaths using the phrases 'La'amrullah' and 'Aymullah' and explain the evidence from the Quran and Hadith.

Core Idea
Oaths by divine attributes like 'La'amrullah' are valid, just as oaths by Allah's name.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wording of an oath matters. This lesson examines two oath phrases used by the Arabs: 'La'amrullah' (by the life of Allah) and 'Aymullah' (by Allah). The commentary clarifies their validity based on scriptural evidence and grammatical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath phrases
Locate the phrases 'La'amrullah' and 'Aymullah' in the passage. Note that they are the focus of the commentary.
2
Examine the linguistic meaning
Understand that 'La'amrullah' means 'by the existence of Allah' and 'Aymullah' is derived from 'ayman' (oaths). The commentary explains the grammatical structure.
'La'amrullah' is a subject with an implied predicate.
3
Find the Quranic proof for 'La'amrullah'
The commentary cites Surah al-Hijr (15:72) where Allah swears 'la'amruka' (by your life). This establishes that swearing by life is acceptable, and by analogy, 'la'amrullah' is valid.
The verse directly shows the permissibility of oath by life.
4
Find the hadith proof for 'Aymullah'
The Prophet ﷺ said 'Aymullah' concerning a companion. This authentic hadith is evidence for the validity of this phrase.
Recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari.
5
Conclude the Hanafi ruling
Both phrases are valid oaths because they invoke divine attributes (existence/names). The Hanafi school relies on these proofs, and the ruling is established in the school.
The mufta bihi is that both are valid.

Source Text

Commentary
This commentary expands on the matn's mention of 'La'amrullah' and 'Aymullah' as valid oath expressions. The author explains that 'La'amrullah' literally means 'by the life/existence of Allah', which is an attribute of Allah. It is a valid oath because it invokes a divine attribute. The Quranic verse (Q. 15:72) uses the similar expression 'la'amruka' (by your life) addressing the Prophet, indicating that swearing by life is recognized. The phrase 'Aymullah' is derived from 'Ayman' (oaths) and is proven by the hadith where the Prophet used it. The commentary clarifies grammatical points: 'La'amrullah' is a subject with an implied predicate (i.e., 'my oath'), and the variant readings are discussed.

Key Terms
لعمر الله (la'amrullah): An oath phrase meaning 'by the life/existence of Allah', considered a valid oath as it invokes an attribute of Allah.
أيم الله (aymullah): An oath phrase derived from 'ayman' (oaths), meaning 'by Allah', supported by hadith.

Valid Oaths Without Explicit 'Wallahi'
Ruling on valid oath phrases beyond 'Wallahi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which phrases (other than 'Wallahi') constitute valid oaths in the Hanafi school and explain the proof using Quran 16:91 and the principle of custom (urf).

Core Idea
An oath is valid if the expression indicates swearing by Allah, even without His name, relying on language and custom.

This passage expands the concept of oaths beyond the explicit mention of Allah's name to include phrases like the covenant of Allah, verbs of swearing such as 'Aqsamu', and even Persian expressions. The Hanafi school takes a broad view, holding that if the expression is understood in language and custom as an oath by Allah, it is binding. This lesson will walk through the specific phrases and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases discussed
List the phrases: 'Ahdullah' (covenant of Allah), 'Mithaquhu' (His pact), verbs like 'Aqsamu' (I swear), 'Halaftu' (I swore), 'Shahidtu' (I testify), 'Alayya nadhr' (upon me a vow) when conditioned, and the Persian 'Sawgand mikhurram ba Khuda' (I swear by God).
Each phrase is treated as a valid oath in the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the proof for each
For 'Ahdullah', the proof is Quran 16:91 which commands fulfilling the covenant of Allah and mentions oaths, implying that covenant is a form of oath. For verbs like 'Aqsamu', the proof is linguistic custom (urf) – these verbs are used for swearing in the Arabic language, so no need for explicit 'by Allah'.
The Quranic proof is qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalala; the custom proof relies on linguistic usage.
3
Understand the linguistic reasoning
The commentary explains that in the language, verbs like 'aqsama' and 'halafa' are inherently oath expressions. The Shari'ah restricts them to swearing by Allah, but they do not require an explicit mention of Allah's name when used in their customary sense.
This is based on the Hanafi principle that custom is given weight in legal rulings.
4
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) argue that an oath must explicitly mention Allah's name or an attribute, as oaths are by definition by Allah. The Hanafi response is that language and custom are authoritative; the intent to swear by Allah is understood from the phrase.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that a Hanafi would require expiation for breaking such an oath, while a Shafi'i might not consider it an oath at all.
5
Apply to non-Arabic phrases
The same principle extends to Persian and other languages. The Persian phrase 'Sawgand mikhurram ba Khuda' is accepted because it is the customary expression for swearing by God in Persian.
This shows the Hanafi school's flexibility with language, as long as the meaning is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعهد الله ، وميثاقه . [١]قوله : وعهد الله ؛ إنّما صار الحلف به قسماً، وكذا بالميثاق الذي هو بمعناه ؛ لقوله له : وَأَوْفُوا بِعَهْدِ اللَّهِ إِذَا عَهَدتُّمْ وَلَا تَنقُضُوا الْأَيْمَنَ بَعْدَ تَوْكِيدِهَا (1) [٢] قوله : وأقسم ؛ هذه الأفعالُ على صيغة المضارع، وكذا على صيغة الماضي، كحلفت وشهدت وأقسمت مستعملة لليمين بحسب اللغة والعرف ، فلا يحتاج فيها إلى قوله : بالله ونحوه. [٣] قوله : وعلي نذر ؛ إن أراد به قربةً لزمته نذراً، وإن لم ينو به لزوم قربة يكون يميناً إذا علقه بشرط ؛ أي محلوف عليه. [٤]قوله : سوكند ؛ - بفتح السين، وسكون الواو ، ثم كاف فارسية مفتوحة، ثم نون، ثم دال مهملة ساكنتان - هو معنى القسم بالفارسية. [0] قوله : قسم ؛ أي يمين، وهو – بفتحتين - من الإقسام، وذكر أبو البقاء الكفوي في «كليّاته»: إنّ القسم أخص من الحلف واليمين، فإن أهل اللغة يسمون بالقسم واليمين ما يقصد به تعظيم المقسم به، إلا أنهم لا يخصون ذلك بالله ، وأهلُ الشرع يخصونه بالله .
And the covenant of Allah, and His pact. [1] His statement: 'And the covenant of Allah'; it only becomes an oath by it, and similarly by the pact which is its meaning, due to His saying: 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted, and do not break oaths after their confirmation' [Q. 16:91]. [2] His statement: 'And I swear'; these verbs in the present tense, and similarly in the past tense, like 'I swore', 'I testified', 'I took an oath', are used for oath according to language and custom, so they do not require saying 'by Allah' or the like. [3] His statement: 'And upon me a vow'; if he intends by it an act of worship, it becomes binding as a vow; if he does not intend an act of worship, it becomes an oath if he conditions it, i.e., something sworn upon. [4] His statement: 'Sawgand'; with fath on seen, sukun on waw, then a Persian kaf with fath, then noon, then two dal's with sukun; it means 'oath' in Persian. [0] His statement: 'Qasam'; i.e., yamin (oath), and it is from 'aqsama' (to swear). Abu al-Baqa' al-Kafawi mentioned in his 'Kulliyyat' that 'qasam' is more specific than 'halif' and 'yamin', for people of language call 'qasam' and 'yamin' what is intended to magnify the one sworn by, except they do not restrict it to Allah, while people of Shari'ah restrict it to Allah.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, explaining additional phrases that constitute valid oaths. The first is 'Ahdullah' (covenant of Allah), which is made an oath by the Quranic command to fulfill the covenant. Similarly, 'Mithaquhu' (His pact) is treated the same way. Next, verbs like 'Aqsamu' (I swear), 'Halaftu' (I swore), 'Shahidtu' (I testify) are themselves oath expressions in language and custom, so they do not need the explicit mention of Allah's name to be valid. The phrase 'Alayya nadhr' (upon me a vow) can be either a vowed act of worship (if intended as such) or an oath if conditioned on something. Finally, the Persian phrase 'Sawgand mikhurram ba Khuda' (I swear by God) is accepted as a valid oath because it is the customary expression for swearing in Persian. The commentary also notes a linguistic distinction: in common language, 'qasam' and 'yamin' can be used for any solemn affirmation, but in Shari'ah they are restricted to oaths by Allah.

Key Terms
عهد الله (ahdullah): The covenant of Allah; swearing by it is considered a valid oath.
قسم (qasam): An oath; more specific than yamin in that it involves exaltation of the one sworn by. In Shari'a, it is restricted to Allah.
سوكند (sawgand): The Persian word for oath; used in phrases like 'sawgand mikhurram ba Khuda' (I swear by God), considered a valid oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT considered a valid oath?
A) Saying 'I swear by the covenant of Allah'
B) Saying 'I testify' without mentioning Allah
C) Saying 'Upon me a vow' intending a conditional oath
D) Saying 'I swear by the Prophet'

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, an oath is valid if the expression indicates swearing by Allah, even without His explicit name, based on Quranic proof and linguistic custom.

The Legal Definition and Types of Oath (Yamīn) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines legal oath and classifies types (explicit/implicit).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of an oath, distinguish between explicit and implicit oaths, and identify the subtypes of implicit oaths.

Core Idea
An oath is a binding contract that strengthens resolve—both explicit and implicit forms are enforceable.

Oaths permeate daily life, from courtrooms to casual promises. The Hanafi school provides a precise legal framework: an oath (yamīn) is not just an utterance but a contract that obligates the swearer. In this lesson, we unpack the definition and explore the two main types—explicit and implicit—along with the linguistic basis for their validity.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Legal Definition
Identify the sentence in the passage that defines yamīn: 'إنّ اليمين شرعاً : هو عقد يقوى به عزم الحالف على الفعل أو الترك'. Understand why it is called a 'contract' (عقد).
Note that the definition focuses on strengthening resolve, not just invoking Allah.
2
Classify Explicit vs. Implicit
The passage divides oaths into ظاهر (explicit) and مضمر (implicit). Explicit oaths directly mention the verb (e.g., 'I swear by Allah'). Implicit oaths are further split into two types: those indicated by the لام and those indicated by context.
Implicit oaths are valid in Hanafi law even without an explicit verb.
3
Analyze Implicit Oath Subtypes
First subtype: the لام of swearing (e.g., Quran 3:186). Second subtype: context alone (e.g., Quran 19:71, where 'واردها' implies an oath). Recognize that both are binding.
The passage gives specific Quranic examples for each subtype.
4
Examine the Linguistic Discussion
The footnotes discuss the derivation of 'أيم الله' and the grammatical case of 'عهد الله'. Understand that these linguistic points refine the scope of valid oath expressions but do not change the core legal definition.
This step is optional for fiqh focus but enriches understanding of how oaths are identified.

So his saying: 'By the life of Allah' is a subject, and 'an oath' is its predicate, and the intended meaning is the eternity of Allah, its implied form is 'by the life of Allah'. And his saying: 'And by the oath of Allah' – it has been said it is a plural of yamin, its noon was deleted for ease due to its frequent usage; its implied form is 'by the oaths of Allah, my oaths'. And it is said it is among the particles of oath like the waw. The yamin that the language people do not know is the condition and the result; for there is no meaning of glorification in it, and they called it yamin because of the meaning of yamin in it, which is prohibition or obligation. [Footnote 2] His saying: 'an oath': this is a deleted predicate, and its meaning: I swear by the life of Allah for him and by His life. [Footnote 3] His saying: 'it has been said...etc.': He pointed out that there is a difference of opinion regarding it. According to the Kufan grammarians, it is derived from ayman: they committed to deleting its noon in the oath, and aymunu (with fatha on hamza, sukun on ya, dhamma on mim) is plural of yamin. According to the Basrans, the meaning is: by Allah, by Allah, so the waw is for oath, and the word aym is independent, placed for swearing, occurring as a connection. [Footnote 4] His saying: 'for the frequency': meaning due to the frequent usage of this word in oaths, and frequent usage necessitates ease; therefore the hamzat wasl was deleted in the writing of 'bismi Allah' as opposed to 'Read in the name of your Lord'. 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah [1] And by the covenant of Allah: in the genitive case by means of the particle of oath. [Footnote 1] His saying: 'by the particle': by this he pointed out that the waw in the author's saying 'and by the covenant of Allah', and likewise his saying 'and by His compact' are both oath forms, and it is a narration of the oath formula. There is discussion about it: firstly, because the apparent is that the waw in both places is for conjunction, just as the waw in his saying: 'and I swear, and I take an oath, and I bear witness, and upon me is a vow, and if I do such-and-such, and I take an oath' is for conjunction, so all these statements are conjoined to his saying: 'by the life of Allah'. Secondly, because it is possible to raise (nominative) 'the covenant of Allah' and to put it in the accusative by deleting the predicate or assuming a particle of oath.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of oaths (yamin) in Hanafi fiqh. The commentary begins by explaining the grammatical structure of the matn's phrase 'bi-'umr Allah' and then moves to the definition: legally, an oath is a 'contract that strengthens the swearer's resolve to perform or avoid an action'. The author then categorises oaths into explicit (zāhir) and implicit (muḍmar). Implicit oaths are further divided into those indicated by the lām and those indicated by the context. The footnotes provide detailed linguistic analysis, addressing the derivation of 'aym Allah', the nature of 'umr Allah as a divine attribute, and the disagreement between Kufan and Basran grammarians. The discussion of 'ahd Allah and mīthāquhu as oath expressions clarifies that the waw can be either a particle of oath or a conjunction, with implications for the grammatical case of the noun. The mention of the Urdu phrase 'sokand mīkhuram' shows the text's multilingual environment. This lesson establishes the foundational understanding of what constitutes a valid oath in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): A contract that strengthens the resolve of the swearer to do or not do something; legally it prohibits the act if sworn to avoid, or obligates it if sworn to perform.
قسم (qasam): Synonym for yamīn; an oath by which one affirms or denies something, often by invoking Allah or His attributes.
ظاهر (zāhir): An explicit oath where the verb is directly mentioned (e.g., 'I swear by Allah').
مضمر (muḍmar): An implicit oath where the verb is not mentioned but understood from context or particles like lām or waw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A mere utterance that does not create obligation.
B) A contract that strengthens the swearer's resolve to act or abstain.
C) A type of worship requiring purification.
D) A conditional statement that becomes binding only if the condition is met.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of an oath as a contract emphasizes its binding nature; oaths can be explicit or implicit, and both are legally enforceable.

Ruling on Conditional Oaths Involving Kufr
Explains that an oath conditioned on kufr is valid even if kufr does not occur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a conditional statement involving kufr is a valid oath, and identify the Quranic evidence and reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
A conditional statement linking an action to disbelief is a valid oath, even if actual disbelief does not occur.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling: when a person says 'If I do this, I am a kafir,' this statement is treated as a binding oath, not an act of apostasy. The passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies why the non-occurrence of actual disbelief does not invalidate the oath, and provides the Quranic proof from Surah Tahrim (66:1). Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid misapplying rulings of apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act in question is a conditional statement linking an action to disbelief (e.g., 'If I do X, I am a kafir'). The Hanafi ruling is that this constitutes a valid oath (yamin).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the Quranic verse 66:1, where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding what is lawful. This verse is used to prove that forbidding something lawful (taḥrīm al-ḥalāl) is equivalent to an oath.
The verse is quoted in the passage: يَأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand the logical link: by conditioning disbelief on the action, the swearer effectively prohibits the action (since kufr is forbidden). Prohibiting what is lawful is itself an oath, as shown by the verse. Thus, the conditional statement becomes an oath.
The commentary explains that the swearer 'made the condition a sign of disbelief, so he must believe it obligatory to avoid' – this creates the meaning of an oath.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction
Note the objection: if it is a valid conditional, then actual disbelief should occur when the condition is fulfilled. But the Hanafi position states that the swearer does not become a disbeliever. The resolution is that mere utterance or conditional declaration does not constitute actual disbelief; the statement is only an oath.
The author adds 'وإن لم يكفر' to preempt the misunderstanding that non-occurrence of kufr invalidates the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : وإن لم يكفر، إنما قال هذا ؛ لأنه علّق الكفر بالفعل المذكور، فيكون قسماً . بسبب التعليق ، فعدمُ الكفر بذلك الفعلِ دلَّ على عدم صحة التعليق"، فلا يصح القسم، فعدمُ الكفر لما أوهم عدم صحة القسم، فلدفع هذا الوهم، قال : إنَّه قسم وإن لم يكفر، وإنما يكون قسماً ؛ لأنه ما علّق الكفر بذلك الفعل، فقد حرَّمَ الفعل، وتحريم الحلال يمين [0]. وهو أول تصانيفي. [٢]قوله : إنما قال هذا ... الخ ; حاصله : إنّ الحالف لَمَّا عَلَّق أمرا بالكفر فقال : إن فعلت هذا وإن لم أفعل هذا فأنا كافر أو قال : إن كنت فعلت هذا فأنا كافر، فإنّما يكون هو قسماً بسبب التعليق بالكفر ، فيلزم أن يثبت الكفر على تقدير وجود ا رد المعلق عليه، مع أنهم صرّحوا بعدم الكفر ، وهذا يدلّ على عدم صحة التعليق ؛ فلهذا صرح المصنف له عدم الكفر بقوله : وإن لم يكفر. [٣] قوله : دلّ على عدم صحة التعليق ؛ إذ لو صح لثبت الكفر كما في سائر التعليقات حيث ينزل الجزاء بوجود الشرط. 41 ] قوله : لأنه لما علق ...الخ ؛ توضيحه إنّه لَمَّا عَلَّقَ الخَالِفُ الكَفْرَ على ذلك الفعل، فقد جعل الشرط علماً على الكفر ، فلا بد أن يعتقده واجباً ؛ لامتناع، فوجد معنى اليمين فصار قسماً، وإنما لا يكفر ؛ لأنّ مجرّدَ القول بالكفر، ومجرد ارتكاب فعل جعله علماً. على الكفر ليس بكفر حقيقة. [0]قوله : يمين ؛ يدل عليه قول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم خطاباً لنبيه : يَأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ
And his saying: 'And even if he does not become a disbeliever' – he only said this because he conditioned disbelief on the mentioned action, so it becomes an oath due to the conditioning. The non-occurrence of disbelief from that action indicates the invalidity of the conditioning, so the oath would not be valid. But the non-occurrence of disbelief created the misunderstanding that the oath is invalid, so to remove this misunderstanding, he said: 'It is an oath even if he does not become a disbeliever.' It is an oath only because he conditioned disbelief on that action, thus he prohibited the action, and the prohibition of what is lawful is an oath. And it is the first of my compositions. [2] His saying: 'He only said this...etc.' The gist: When the swearer conditioned disbelief on an act, saying: 'If I do this or if I do not do this, then I am a disbeliever,' or said: 'If I did this, then I am a disbeliever,' it is an oath due to the conditioning on disbelief. Consequently, disbelief must occur when the condition is realized, yet they explicitly state that he does not become a disbeliever. This indicates the invalidity of the conditioning; therefore, the author stated his non-disbelief by saying: 'and even if he does not become a disbeliever.' [3] His saying: 'indicates the invalidity of the conditioning' – for if it were valid, disbelief would occur as in other conditionals where the result occurs upon the existence of the condition. [41] His saying: 'because when he conditioned...etc.' Explanation: When the swearer conditioned disbelief on that action, he made the condition a sign of disbelief, so he must believe it obligatory (to avoid), for impossibility, so the meaning of the oath is found and it becomes an oath. And he does not become a disbeliever because mere utterance of disbelief and mere commission of an act which he made a sign for disbelief is not actual disbelief. [0] His saying: 'an oath' – evidence for it is the saying of Allah (upon him be peace) addressing His Prophet: 'O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' (Quran 66:1).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific ruling: when a person makes a conditional statement linking an action to disbelief (e.g., 'If I do this, I am a kafir'), the Hanafi position is that this constitutes a valid oath, even if the condition is not fulfilled and the person does not actually become a disbeliever. The commentary explains the reasoning: by conditioning disbelief on the action, the swearer effectively prohibits the action (since disbelief is forbidden), and prohibiting something lawful is itself an oath. The author, Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah (the matn being commented upon), includes the phrase 'وإن لم يكفر' to preempt the objection that the lack of realized disbelief invalidates the oath. The proof comes from the Quranic verse (66:1) where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding what He made lawful, indicating that such a prohibition is tantamount to an oath. The passage clarifies that the swearer does not become an actual disbeliever, as mere utterance or conditional declaration of disbelief does not constitute actual kufr. This is a nuanced ruling that distinguishes between the legal effect of the oath and the actual occurrence of disbelief.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta‘līq): The act of linking a ruling (here, disbelief) to the occurrence of a condition (the action criticized).
الكفر (al-kufr): Disbelief; here, it is used as the subject of a conditional oath, but actual disbelief does not occur merely by uttering a conditional statement.
تحريم الحلال (taḥrīm al-ḥalāl): Forbidding something that is lawful; this action is considered an oath (yamīn) in Hanafi law, as indicated by the Quranic verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence used in this passage to prove that forbidding something lawful constitutes an oath?
A) A hadith narrated by Abu Hurairah.
B) The Quranic verse: 'O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?'
C) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions.
D) Qiyas on the prohibition of riba.

Key Takeaway
A conditional statement linking an action to disbelief is a valid oath in Hanafi law; actual disbelief does not occur, but the oath carries binding consequences.

Ruling on Suspending Disbelief on Past or Future Acts
Determining whether suspending disbelief on a known past act constitutes immediate kufr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on suspending disbelief (ta'liq al-kufr) on past or future acts, distinguishing between cases where it is treated as an oath and where it constitutes immediate kufr due to satisfaction with disbelief.

Core Idea
Suspending disbelief on a past act is not immediate kufr if understood as an oath; only when accompanied by belief in its kufr-causing effect does it become kufr.

This passage from the Book of Oaths addresses a delicate question: does someone who says 'If I did X, then I am a kafir' become a disbeliever immediately if X is a past act? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the legal nature of such statements—they are oaths (yamin), not affirmations of belief—and the subjective belief of the speaker. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on apostasy and oath expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement and its possible rulings
The person says 'If I did X, I am a kafir.' The question is whether this immediately makes him a kafir if X is known to have occurred. Identify the two possible rulings: immediate kufr (some scholars) or no kufr (majority view).
The act is a conditional statement (ta'liq).
2
Locate the dominant Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Quranic verse (Surah al-Tahrim 66:1-2) equates tahrim al-halal (declaring permissible things prohibited) with an oath (yamin). The hadith about vows also supports treating such statements as oaths.
The proof is from the Quran and hadith; it is qati' al-thubut but the interpretation is key.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah (indication of the proof)
The Quranic verse shows that statements like 'Upon me is a vow' or 'I forbid for myself what is halal' are treated as oaths, not as actual kufr. Hence, suspending disbelief on a known act is also an oath, not immediate kufr.
The verse is used as an analogy (qiyas) by Al-Marghinani.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis (e.g., Muhammad ibn Muqatil) argue that suspending on a known past act is immediate kufr because the condition is already true (it is tanziz, not ta'liq). The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree.
The ikhtilaf revolves around whether ta'liq on a known past act is treated as immediate (tanziz).
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the crucial condition
The relied-upon view: no kufr if the speaker knows it is an oath. If he believes swearing falsely makes him a kafir, then he becomes a kafir due to rida bil-kufr (satisfaction with disbelief). This condition (belief about the nature of the statement) changes the ruling.
The key distinction is the speaker's i'tiqad (belief) about the oath's effect.

Source Text

كتاب الأيمان/تعريفه وأقسامه
وقوله : علقه بماض أو آت ؛ أي لا يَكْفُر بهذا القول سواء علق الكفر بفعل ماض أو مستقبل ، وعند البعض ) : إن علقه بفعل ماض يَكْفُر ؛ لأنَّ التَّعليق بفعل يُعْلَمُ أنَّه قد وقع تنجيز لَكَ تَبْتَغِي مَرْضَاتَ أَزْوَاجِكَ وَاللَّهُ غَفُورٌ رَحِيمٌ ) قَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَنِكُمْ ) (٢)، وقد كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حلف على تركِ أكل العسل (۳) ، وقصته مرويّة في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره. فهذه الآية دلّت على أن تحريم الحلال يمين، فيكونُ كل ما تضمنه يميناً ؛ ولذا كان قوله : علي نذر يميناً ؛ لأنَّ النذر إيجاب المباح ، وهو يستلزم تحريم الحلال ؛ لأنّ إيجاب فعل أو تركه مستلزم لتحريم ضدّه ، ويدلّ عليه أيضاً قوله : «مَن نذر نذراً ولم يسمّ فعليه كفّارة يمين» (٤) ، أخرجه أبو داود وابن ماجة.
[ ١ ] قوله : لأنّ التعليق؛ يعني أنَّ التعليق بما يعلمُ وقوعه تنجيز، فإذا قال : إن كنت فعلت كذا فأنا كافر، فإن كان صادقاً فلا كفارة ولا مؤاخذة، وإن كان كاذباً كفر؛ لأنّه يصيرُ كأنه نجز الكفر وأثبته لنفسه، ويدلّ عليه قوله : «من حلف على يمين بملة غير الإسلام كاذباً متعمدا فهو كما قال» (٥) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم.
لكن الصحيح أنه لا يَكْفُر إن كان يَعْلَمُ أَنَّه يمين، فإن كان عنده أَنَّه يَكْفُرُ بالحلفِ يكفر فيهما".
[١] قوله : لكن الصحيح ... الخ؛ حاصله : إنَّ الصحيح أنه لا يكفر بتعليقه على الكفر، سواءً علقه بماض أو بمستقبل إن كان في اعتقاده أنه يمين؛ لأنه يقصد به مجرد الامتناع عن الشيء، ومجرّد توثيق خبره، ولا يثبت الكفر ما دام اعتقاده راسخاً. فإن كان في اعتقاده أنه يكفر بالحلف به كاذباً، أو يظن ذلك الفعل من موجباتِ الكفر فحلف به كاذباً يكفر حينئذ لا لمجرد التعليق بالكفر، بل لوجودِ الرضى بالكفر، والرضى بالكفر كفر، والظاهرُ أنَّ حديث «الصحيحين» فهو كما قال : خرج مخرج الغالب، فإن الغالب ممن يحلف بمثل هذه الأيمان أن يكون جاهلاً لا يعرفُ إلا لزوم الكفر على تقدير الحنث. كذا في فتح القدير
[٢] قوله : فيهما؛ أي في الغموس والمنعقدة كليهما، أما في الغموس ففي الحال، وأما في المنعقدة فعند مباشرة الشرط في المستقبل، وذلك لأنّ مَن قال : إن فعلت كذا فأنا كافر، مراده الامتناع بالتعليق وعزمه عدم ارتكابه، فليس فيه رضاً بالكفر في الحال، وأما إذا باشر الفعل معتقداً أنه يكفرُ بمباشرته ، فإنّه يكفرُ عند المباشرة ؛ لوجود الرضا بالكفر حينئذ.
Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Its Types. And his statement: 'He suspended it on past or future'; i.e., he does not become a disbeliever by this statement whether he suspends disbelief on a past act or a future act. According to some: if he suspends it on a past act, he becomes a disbeliever, because suspending on an act known to have occurred is immediate... 'Indeed, you sought the pleasure of your spouses. And Allah is Forgiving and Merciful. Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths.' And the Prophet ﷺ swore to leave honey, and his story is narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari and others. This verse indicated that declaring permissible things prohibited is an oath, so everything it contains is an oath; hence his statement 'upon me is a vow' is an oath, because a vow is making permissible obligatory, which entails prohibiting the permissible, because making an act or its abandonment obligatory entails prohibiting its opposite. Also evidence for it is his statement: 'Whoever makes a vow and does not specify it, must expiate it with the expiation of an oath' narrated by Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah. [1] His statement: 'because suspending'; meaning that suspending on something known to occur is immediate. So if he says: 'If I did such and such, then I am a disbeliever,' if he is truthful, there is no expiation and no punishment; if he is lying, he becomes a disbeliever because it is as if he executed disbelief and affirmed it for himself. Evidence for it is his statement: 'Whoever swears an oath by a religion other than Islam, lying deliberately, then he is as he said' narrated by Bukhari and Muslim. But the correct view is that he does not become a disbeliever if he knows it is an oath. If he believes that he becomes a disbeliever by swearing, he becomes a disbeliever in both [types]. [1] His statement: 'But the correct view...' summary: The correct view is that he does not become a disbeliever by suspending it on disbelief, whether he suspends it on past or future, if in his belief it is an oath; because he only intends mere abstention from the thing and mere confirmation of his report, and disbelief is not established as long as his belief is firm. If he believes that he becomes a disbeliever by swearing falsely, or he thinks that act is among the causes of disbelief, then he swears falsely, he becomes a disbeliever then, not merely because of suspending on disbelief, but because of the existence of satisfaction with disbelief, and satisfaction with disbelief is disbelief. Apparently, the hadith of the Two Sahihs 'he is as he said' is interpreted according to the majority, for the majority of those who swear such oaths are ignorant and only know that disbelief is necessary upon violation. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. [2] His statement: 'in both of them'; i.e., in the false oath (al-ghamus) and the valid oath (al-mun'aqidah) both. As for the false oath, it is immediate; as for the valid oath, at the occurrence of the condition in the future. That is because one who says: 'If I do such and such, then I am a disbeliever,' his intention is abstention by suspension and his resolve is not to commit it, so there is no satisfaction with disbelief at present. But if he commits the act believing that he becomes a disbeliever by committing it, then he becomes a disbeliever at the time of committing due to the existence of satisfaction with disbelief then.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussing whether suspending disbelief on a past or future act constitutes immediate kufr. The text begins by stating the general ruling: if someone says, 'If I did such and such, I am a kafir,' and he knows it is an oath, he does not become a kafir. However, some scholars (e.g., Muhammad ibn Muqatil) hold that if the condition is a past act known to have occurred, the suspension is immediate and thus kufr occurs. The author rejects this, citing Quranic evidence (Surah al-Tahrim 66:1-2) that declaring permissible things prohibited is an oath, and hadith that a vow without specification requires expiation. The key proof is that the action is considered an oath (yamin), not actual kufr. The correct Hanafi position is that one does not become a disbeliever if he understands it as an oath; if he believes swearing falsely causes kufr, then he becomes a kafir due to satisfaction with kufr (rida bil-kufr). The hadith 'he is as he said' applies to those ignorant of the oath ruling. The passage also distinguishes between false oaths (al-ghamus) and valid oaths (al-mun'aqidah): in false oaths, kufr is immediate if he believed; in valid oaths, kufr occurs only upon violating the condition with that belief.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a consequence (like kufr) on a condition, as in 'if I do X, then I am a kafir'.
رضا بالكفر (rida bil-kufr): Satisfaction or approval of disbelief, which itself constitutes disbelief.
يمين (yamin): An oath; in this context, any statement that implies a solemn promise or vow, treated as an oath requiring expiation if broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a person says 'If I did X, I am a kafir' knowing it is an oath, and he actually did X, what is the ruling?
A) He becomes a kafir immediately.
B) He does not become a kafir, but must perform kaffarah yamin if he intended an oath.
C) He becomes a kafir only if X is a major sin.
D) The statement is void and he remains a Muslim without any obligation.

Key Takeaway
Suspending disbelief on a known past act is not immediate kufr; it is treated as an oath, and only if the speaker believes it causes kufr does he become a disbeliever due to satisfaction with disbelief.
