The Ruling on Swearing by Haqq and Hurmah
Defining the legal terminology for oaths using 'haqq' and 'hurma' and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid oath formulations in Hanafi fiqh based on the terms used.

Core Idea
Swearing by 'haqq' or 'hurma' is not a valid Islamic oath unless the swearer intends Allah's name.

When a person swears using words like 'by the truth' (haqqan) or 'by the sanctity' (hurma), a question arises: is this a binding oath (yamin) that requires expiation if broken? The Hanafi school differentiates between common speech and legal oaths. In this lesson, we examine the key terms and the school's ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term used in the oath
Examine the exact wording: is it 'haqq', 'haqq Allah', or 'hurma'? Note any additional qualifiers.
The term 'haqq' alone is ambiguous; 'haqq Allah' is more specific.
2
Determine if the term is a name of Allah or something else
Apply the Hanafi principle that only oaths by Allah or His attributes are valid. 'Haqq' can be a name of Allah but is also a common noun. 'Hurma' is never a name of Allah.
The intention of the swearer matters.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on valid oath terms
The default ruling: oaths by other than Allah are not considered valid yamin. Swearing by 'haqq' or 'hurma' falls under this unless the swearer intended Allah's name.
The majority of Hanafi scholars hold this view.
4
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some mashayikh considered 'haqq' a valid oath due to custom ('urf). Recognize that this is a minority opinion.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi ruling
State clearly: according to the majority, such oaths are not yamin and require no kaffarah. Swearing by 'hurma' is qasm bi-ghayr Allah and invalid.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text

[٢] قوله : فيهما؛ أي في الغموس والمنعقدة كليهما، أما في الغموس ففي الحال، وأما في المنعقدة فعند مباشرة الشرط في المستقبل، وذلك لأنّ مَن قال : إن فعلت كذا فأنا كافر، مراده الامتناع بالتعليق وعزمه عدم ارتكابه، فليس فيه رضاً بالكفر في الحال، وأما إذا باشر الفعل معتقداً أنه يكفرُ بمباشرته ، فإنّه يكفرُ عند المباشرة ؛ لوجود الرضا بالكفر حينئذ.
(٣) قوله : وحقاً؛ قال في «المجتبى»: في قوله : وحقاً أو حقاً اختلاف المشايخ، والأكثر على أنه ليس بيمين. [٤] قوله : وحرمته؛ - بالضم - ، اسم من الاحترام، وحرمه الله له ما لا يحل انتهاكه، فهو في الحقيقة قسم بغير الله وكذا لا حلف بحرمة لا إله إلا الله، وبحق الرسول أو الإيمان أو الصلاة ونحو ذلك.
(And by haqq, and by haqq Allah, and by hurmatuhu) [3] His statement: 'but the correct view...' summary: The correct view is that he does not become a disbeliever by suspending it on disbelief... [2] His statement: 'in both of them'... (3) His statement: 'and by haqq' – said in al-Mujtaba: regarding his statement 'wa haqqan' or 'haqqan', there is a difference among the mashayikh, and the majority hold that it is not an oath. [4] His statement: 'and by hurmatuhu' – with dammah, a noun from ihtiram, and what Allah has forbidden is not permissible to violate. It is essentially an oath by other than Allah, and likewise no oath by the sanctity of la ilaha illa Allah, or by the right of the Messenger, or faith, or prayer, and the like.

Commentary
This passage defines the legal terms used in oath formulations: 'haqqan', 'haqq Allah', and 'hurmatuhu'. The matn states these are types of oaths. The commentary clarifies that the majority of scholars (al-mashayikh) hold that swearing by 'haqq' is not a valid oath (yamin) unless one intends it as a name of Allah. Similarly, swearing by 'hurma' (sanctity) is an oath by other than Allah and thus not a valid Islamic oath; it is akin to swearing by the sanctity of 'la ilaha illa Allah' or by the right of the Prophet, faith, prayer, etc. The correct view is that such oaths are not binding in the sense of requiring expiation or incurring sin for breaking them, unless the swearer intends to venerate Allah through these terms. The passage emphasizes that the default ruling is that these are not oaths, based on the opinion of the majority.

Key Terms
حق (haqq): A noun meaning right or truth; when used in oaths, it is ambiguous and not considered a valid oath unless intended as a name of Allah.
حرمة (hurma): Sanctity or inviolability; swearing by it is an oath by other than Allah and thus invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is haram to swear by it.
D) It is a valid oath only if done in court.

Key Takeaway
Swearing by 'haqq' or 'hurma' is not a valid Islamic oath according to the majority of Hanafi scholars.

Swearing by One's Own Disbelief: Intention Overrides Literal Meaning
Commentary on conditional oaths involving disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on conditional statements involving disbelief to determine when a person becomes a disbeliever and when he does not.

Core Idea
Intention determines whether an oath of disbelief is literal or figurative.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced Hanafi ruling: uttering 'I am a disbeliever' as an oath does not automatically make one a disbeliever. The key is the speaker's intent and inner state. If the statement is meant as a mere oath to avoid an action, it remains a valid oath without takfir. However, if the speaker is satisfied with disbelief, it becomes actual kufr. We will trace the proof from the Sahihain and the distinction between two types of oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is conditioning an oath upon disbelief (ta'liq 'ala al-kufr), e.g., 'If I do X, I am a disbeliever.' The ruling: it is a valid oath and does not cause disbelief if intended as an oath.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim as evidence that such oaths are common and not meant literally.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for this ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows that people in the Prophet's time swore such oaths without being considered disbelievers. Therefore, the word 'kufr' is used figuratively as a strong oath, not literal disbelief.
The common practice ('urf) supports this interpretation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (outside Hanafi) hold that any utterance of kufr, even as an oath, renders one a disbeliever immediately. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on intent.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate, but the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Distinguish between ghammus and mun'aqidah
If the oath is false (ghammus, about the past) and the speaker believes he becomes a disbeliever by it, he becomes kafir immediately. If it is a future oath (mun'aqidah), disbelief occurs only at the time of breaking the oath if he still holds that belief.
The difference hinges on the timing of satisfaction with disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : لكن الصحيح ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ الصحيح أنه لا يكفر بتعليقه على الكفر، سواءً علقه بماض أو بمستقبل إن كان في اعتقاده أنه يمين ؛ لأنه يقصد به مجرد الامتناع عن الشيء، ومجرّد توثيق خبره ، ولا يثبت الكفر ما دام اعتقاده راسخاً. فإن كان في اعتقاده أنه يكفر بالحلف به كاذباً ، أو يظن ذلك الفعل من موجباتِ الكفر فحلف به كاذباً يكفر حينئذ لا لمجرد التعليق بالكفر ، بل لوجودِ الرضى بالكفر، والرضى بالكفر كفر، والظاهرُ أنَّ حديث «الصحيحين» فهو كما قال : خرج مخرج الغالب، فإن الغالب ممن يحلف بمثل هذه الأيمان أن يكون جاهلاً لا يعرفُ إلا لزوم الكفر على تقدير الحنث. كذا في فتح القدير [٢] قوله : فيهما ؛ أي في الغموس والمنعقدة كليهما، أما في الغموس ففي الحال، وأما في المنعقدة فعند مباشرة الشرط في المستقبل، وذلك لأنّ مَن قال : إن فعلت كذا فأنا كافر، مراده الامتناع بالتعليق وعزمه عدم ارتكابه، فليس فيه رضاً بالكفر في الحال، وأما إذا باشر الفعل معتقداً أنه يكفرُ بمباشرته ، فإنّه يكفرُ عند المباشرة ؛ لوجود الرضا بالكفر حينئذ.
[1] His statement: 'But the correct view...' etc.; its summary: The correct view is that he does not become a disbeliever by conditioning (his oath) upon disbelief, whether he conditions it upon the past or the future, if in his belief it is an oath; because he intends by it merely to refrain from something and merely to confirm his report, and disbelief is not established as long as his belief is firm. If in his belief he becomes a disbeliever by swearing falsely, or he thinks that act is one of the causes of disbelief, and he swears falsely, then he becomes a disbeliever at that time—not merely due to conditioning upon disbelief, but due to the existence of satisfaction with disbelief, and satisfaction with disbelief is disbelief. It appears that the hadith in the 'Sahihain' is as he said: it came in the context of the common case, for most of those who swear by such oaths are ignorant, knowing only that disbelief is necessary upon the occurrence of the violation. This is from Fath al-Qadir. [2] His statement: 'in both'—i.e., in the ghammus (false oath) and the mun'aqidah (binding oath). As for the ghammus, it is immediately; as for the mun'aqidah, at the occurrence of the condition in the future. That is because the one who says: 'If I do such-and-such, then I am a disbeliever' intends to refrain by conditioning and is resolved not to commit it, so there is no satisfaction with disbelief at present. But if he commits the act believing that he becomes a disbeliever by committing it, then he becomes a disbeliever at the time of committing due to the existence of satisfaction with disbelief at that time.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on a matn statement. The author clarifies the correct Hanafi position on whether a person becomes a disbeliever when he swears by saying 'I am a disbeliever' or similar. The key principle: if the person intends it merely as an oath to emphasize truth or to prevent an action, and he does not actually believe in disbelief, then he does not become a disbeliever. This is because the intended meaning is a figurative oath, not literal disbelief. However, if he believes that swearing falsely leads to disbelief and he does so with satisfaction of that disbelief, he does become a disbeliever—not because of the conditional phrase, but because of the inner state of being pleased with disbelief. The author also distinguishes between two types of oaths: the ghammus (false oath about the past) which causes immediate disbelief if the person held that belief, and the mun'aqidah (oath about the future) wherein disbelief occurs only at the time of breaking the oath if the person still holds that belief. This is a nuanced ruling that guards against over-applying takfir to those who use such expressions casually.

Key Terms
تعليق على الكفر (ta'liq 'ala al-kufr): Conditioning an oath upon disbelief, e.g., 'If I do X, I am a disbeliever.'
الرضا بالكفر (al-rida bi al-kufr): Inner satisfaction or approval with disbelief, which is itself considered disbelief.
يمين غموس (yamin ghammus): A false oath about the past (deliberately lying).
يمين منعقدة (yamin mun'aqidah): A binding oath about the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when does a person become a disbeliever through a conditional oath like 'If I do X, I am a disbeliever'?
A) Immediately upon uttering the conditional statement.
B) Only if he breaks the oath while believing he becomes a disbeliever and is satisfied with that.
C) Only if he uses the word 'Allah' in the oath.
D) Never, because such statements are always considered oaths.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, conditional statements of disbelief are treated as valid oaths and do not cause disbelief unless the speaker is inwardly satisfied with disbelief.

Oaths by Right and Sanctity of Allah
Ruling on oaths by 'haqq Allah' and 'hurmat Allah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing by 'haqq Allah' and 'hurmat Allah', distinguish the majority position from the minority view based on custom, and apply the distinction to similar phrases.

Core Idea
Swearing by 'haqq Allah' or 'hurmat Allah' is not a valid oath unless explicitly intended as a name of Allah.

This lesson examines a concise fiqh passage on the validity of oaths using phrases like 'by the right of Allah' and 'by the sanctity of Allah'. These expressions are common in daily speech but carry distinct implications for the formation of a binding oath. The Hanafi school's majority view holds that such oaths are invalid, while a minority considers them valid due to custom. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to identify the essential components of a valid oath in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Swearing by 'haqq Allah' and 'hurmat Allah'. What is the hukm? The majority holds it is not a valid oath.
The passage lists these as examples of non-oaths, with an exception if intended as a name of Allah.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof supports the ruling? The text states 'فهو في الحقيقة قسم بغير الله' meaning it is in reality an oath by other than Allah. This is based on the principle that valid oaths must be by Allah or His attributes.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? Since 'haqq Allah' and 'hurmat Allah' are not direct names or attributes of Allah, they fall under the category of oaths by other than Allah, which are invalid.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Which school disagrees? The author of al-Ikhtiyar (and some other scholars) consider it a valid oath due to custom ('urf). What is their proof? Customary usage treats these phrases as oaths.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is mufta bihi? The majority view (not an oath) is the relied-upon position, as confirmed by al-Durr al-Mukhtar and other later authorities.
The tarjih authority is the weight of the majority and the explicit statement in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقاً، وحق الله، وحرمته
(۳) قوله : وحقاً ؛ قال في «المجتبى»: في قوله : وحقاً أو حقاً اختلاف المشايخ ، والأكثر على أنه ليس بيمين.
[٤] قوله : وحرمته ؛ - بالضم - ، اسم من الاحترام، وحرمه الله له ما لا يحل انتهاكه، فهو في الحقيقة قسم بغير الله وكذا لا حلف بحرمة لا إله إلا الله، وبحق الرسول أو الإيمان أو الصلاة ونحو ذلك.
(۱) إلا إذا أراد به اسم الله تعالى. ينظر : «الدر المختار (٣: ٥٧).
(۲) واختار صاحب الاختيار (۳) : (۲۹۳) أن حق الله يمين ؛ لأن الحلف به معتاد اعتباراً للعرف.
(۳) اسم بمعنى الاحترام وحرمة الله ما لا يحل انتهاكه فهو في الحقيقة بغيره تعالى. ينظر : رد المحتار»
.(ov : 1)
(٤) «فتح الباري (٥: ٧٨).
And 'haqqan' [by right], and 'haqq Allah' [by the right of Allah], and 'hurmatuhu' [by His sanctity].
(3) His statement: 'haqqan' – He said in al-Mujtaba: There is disagreement among the mashayikh regarding his saying 'haqqan' or 'haqqan', and the majority is that it is not an oath.
[4] His statement: 'hurmatuhu' – with a dammah on the ha, it is a noun meaning respect, and what Allah has forbidden [i.e., His sanctity] is what is not permissible to violate. So in reality it is an oath by other than Allah. Similarly, no oath is made by the sanctity of 'La ilaha illa Allah', nor by the right of the Messenger, or by faith, or by prayer, and so on.
(1) Except if he intends by it the name of Allah, the Exalted. See al-Durr al-Mukhtar (3:57).
(2) The author of al-Ikhtiyar chose (3:293) that 'haqq Allah' is an oath because oaths by it are customary, considering custom.
(3) A noun meaning respect, and the sanctity of Allah is what is not permissible to violate, so in reality it is by other than Him. See Radd al-Muhtar.
(4) Fath al-Bari (5:78).
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the validity of oaths using the phrases 'by the right of Allah' and 'by the sanctity of Allah'. The matn lists these as examples of oaths, and the commentary clarifies their legal status. According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, swearing by 'haqq Allah' (the right of Allah) is not considered a valid oath (yamin) because it is an oath by something other than Allah or His attributes. However, there is ikhtilaf: some scholars, like the author of al-Ikhtiyar, consider it an oath because customarily people use it as such. The preferred view is that it is not an oath unless the speaker intends by it the actual name of Allah. Similarly, swearing by 'hurmat Allah' (the sanctity of Allah) is not an oath, as 'hurma' is a created concept of respect, not an attribute of Allah. This extends to other phrases like 'by the right of the Prophet' or 'by the sanctity of prayer'. The ruling hinges on whether the expression is considered a direct oath by Allah or by something else. The Hanafi school emphasizes that valid oaths are only by Allah, His names, or attributes.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): The right of Allah, used as an oath phrase.
حرمة الله (hurmat Allah): The sanctity of Allah, used as an oath phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, what is the ruling on swearing by 'haqq Allah' (the right of Allah)?
A) It is a valid oath and requires kaffarah if broken.
B) It is not a valid oath because it is an oath by something other than Allah.
C) It is a valid oath only if the person intends divorce.
D) It is always permissible to use as an oath without consequences.

Key Takeaway
Swearing by 'haqq Allah' or 'hurmat Allah' is not a valid Hanafi oath because they refer to Allah's rights or sanctity, not His names or attributes.

Valid Oath Formulations in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on Persian oaths and Arabic oath particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid oath formulations and apply the Hanafi rules for oath particles.

Core Idea
A valid oath requires either a recognized Arabic particle or a customary phrase; Persian expressions are not oaths unless customary.

Oaths in Islamic law must be made in specific ways. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies which Persian phrases count as oaths and which do not, and lists the Arabic particles that create a valid oath. Understanding these rules prevents inadvertent violations and ensures that the legal consequences of oaths (kaffarah) are applied correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on whether certain Persian phrases and conditional statements are valid oaths. Determine the hukm level for each phrase.
The ruling is that Persian phrases like 'sawqand khurram ba-khuda' are not oaths.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the lack of custom ('urf) and the fact that oaths must be by Allah. Phrases that are oaths by other than Allah or not customary are invalid.
The text says 'لعدم التعارف' (due to lack of custom) and 'لأنه حلف بغير الله' (because it is an oath by other than Allah).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Custom is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh; a phrase is an oath only if custom recognizes it as such. Additionally, the object of the oath must be Allah or His attributes.
For Persian phrases, the present tense 'sawqand me-khuram' indicates creation (insha') and is valid, while past tense 'khurda-am' is only informative.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafis consider customary Persian phrases (like common expressions) as valid oaths, but the relied-upon position is that they are not.
This is an intra-madhhab difference; the mufta bihi is the stricter view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author follows the majority view that only Arabic particles and customary phrases recognized by the Shariah are valid. The absence of widespread custom for Persian phrases and the fact that they are oaths by other than Allah lead to the ruling of invalidity.
The tarjih authority is the reliance on the principle that oaths are restricted to Allah's name or recognized custom.

And 'sawqand khurram ba-khuda' i.e., 'I take an oath by God', and 'ya bi-talaq zan' i.e., 'or by divorcing a woman', and 'if he does it, then upon him is His wrath, or His displeasure, or His curse', or 'I am a fornicator, or a thief, or a drinker of wine, or an eater of usury'—no [it is not an oath]. And the letters of oath: al-waw, al-ba, al-ta.
[1] His statement: 'sawqand khurram ba-khuda': it is not an oath because it is a promise; just as his saying 'sawqand khurda-am' is informing about an oath, unlike his saying 'sawqand me-khuram' which is an oath as mentioned, because it indicates creation (insha').
[2] His statement: 'ya bi-talaq zan'—with a fatha on the za, and sukun on the nun in Persian—means 'wife' or 'woman'. And it is not an oath because it is an oath by other than Allah, and there is no custom ('urf). For this very reason, his saying 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me is the wrath of Allah and His curse', or 'I am a fornicator, thief', and so on, is not an oath due to lack of custom.
And al-ta:
[3] His statement: 'al-waw... etc.': He mentioned the frequently used letters of oath, which are al-waw and al-ba.
1. Al-waw enters only upon the manifest noun (muzhar).
2. Al-ba enters upon both the manifest noun and the pronoun (mudmar).
And al-ta enters only upon the word 'Allah'—God.
Among the letters of oath:
1. Al-lam al-maksurah: it enters only upon the name of Allah in great matters, e.g., 'Lillahi' or 'Wallahi' [actually the text has 'الله الله' maybe meaning 'Lillahi' or 'Wallahi'?].
(1) Because it is a promise and not an oath. See al-Idah (q70/b). (2) Mulla Khusraw made a mistake in al-Durar (2:41) about the author of al-Wiqayah using the word 'ya'; he said it should be 'aw' instead. But in Sharh Abi al-Makaram (q296): it is better. However, Abd al-Halim in his Hashiya (1:335) clarified that this is a misunderstanding by Mulla Khusraw because the word 'ya' in Persian means 'or' in Arabic. (3) i.e., it is not an oath because there is no effect of conditioning on the existence of these things. See Sharh Ibn Malik (q130/b).
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on various types of oaths. First, it discusses Persian oaths: phrases like 'sawqand khurram ba-khuda' (I take an oath by God) are not valid oaths but promises; the indicative form 'sawqand khurda-am' is informing about an oath, while the present tense 'sawqand me-khuram' is a valid oath because it creates the oath. Similarly, 'ya bi-talaq zan' (or by divorcing a woman) is not an oath because it is an oath by other than Allah and not customary. The same applies to conditional phrases like 'upon me is the wrath of Allah' or claiming to be a sinner—these are not oaths because they rely on custom, which is absent. The second part discusses the Arabic particles of oath: waw (واو), ba (باء), ta (تاء), and lam (لام). Each has specific rules: waw only enters on manifest nouns, ba on both manifest and pronouns, ta only on the word 'Allah', and lam only on 'Allah' in great matters. The text also includes a correction regarding the Persian word 'ya' meaning 'or', clarifying a misunderstanding by Mulla Khusraw.

Key Terms
حروف القسم (huruf al-qasm): The letters/particles used to make an oath: waw, ba, ta, lam.
تعارف (ta'aruf): Custom or common usage ('urf) that recognizes a phrase as an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Saying 'Wallahi' in Arabic.
D) Saying 'Bismillah' in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A valid Hanafi oath requires an Arabic particle or a customary expression that creates the oath in the present tense; Persian and conditional statements without custom are not binding.

When a Promise is Not an Oath: Understanding Hanafi Criteria for Valid Oaths
Ruling on Persian oath phrases and similar expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which expressions constitute a valid binding oath (yamin) in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish them from non-binding promises and reports.

Core Idea
An oath requires constructive wording (insha) and customary usage; a mere promise or report of swearing is not binding.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies that not every phrase involving God or conditional curses forms a legally binding oath. The Hanafi school requires specific linguistic and customary conditions. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion about when expiation becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key expressions and their classifications
Read the Arabic passage and note each phrase discussed: 'Sawkand khurm bakhudai', 'Sawkandmi khuram', 'Ya bi-talaq zan', and conditional curses. Mark which are deemed oaths and which are not.
Focus on the reasoning given for each classification.
2
Understand the concepts of insha, ikhbar, and wa'd
The commentary distinguishes between constructive speech (insha) such as 'Sawkandmi khuram' (I am swearing), which creates an oath, and reports (ikhbar) or promises (wa'd) like 'Sawkand khurm bakhudai' (I swear by God as a promise). Understand why the latter do not carry legal force.
Insha brings about an immediate legal effect; ikhbar merely informs about a past or future act.
3
Apply the requirement of custom (ta'aruf)
The text states that swearing by other than Allah (e.g., by a wife's divorce) is not an oath because it lacks customary usage. Reflect on how the Hanafi school uses 'urf (custom) to determine which expressions are recognized as oaths.
Custom varies by region and time; but the principle remains that an oath must be in terms that are conventionally understood as binding.

Swearing by God or by the divorce of a wife, and if he does it, then upon him is His anger, or displeasure, or curse, or I am an adulterer, or a thief, or a wine drinker, or an usurer, no. [1] He said: 'Sawkand khurm bakhudai' is not an oath because it is a promise, just as his saying 'Sawkand khurdah am' is a report of an oath, unlike his saying 'Sawkandmi khuram' which is an oath as mentioned, because it indicates construction. [2] He said: 'Ya bi-talaq zan' – with fathah on the za and sukun on the nun in Persian – means the wife and woman, and it is not an oath because it is swearing by other than Allah, and there is no custom; and for this very reason his saying: 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me be Allah's anger, curse, or I am an adulterer or thief' and similar are not oaths due to lack of custom.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. It explains that certain Persian phrases and Arabic expressions do not constitute valid oaths in Hanafi fiqh. The phrase 'Sawkand khurm bakhudai' (I swear by God) is not an oath because it is a promise (wa'd) or a report about an oath, not a constructive (insha') statement. The phrase 'Sawkandmi khuram' (I am swearing) is an oath because it indicates the performance of swearing. Similarly, 'Ya bi-talaq zan' (or by the divorce of a wife) is not an oath because it swears by something other than Allah (the wife's divorce) and there is no customary use of such expressions in the Arab context. Likewise, conditional statements like 'If I do this, Allah's anger be upon me' or claiming to be an adulterer are not oaths, as they lack the custom that would give them legal force. The footnotes clarify a point about the word 'ya' being Persian for 'or', correcting a misunderstanding by Mulla Khusraw.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): A legally binding oath that requires expiation upon breaking.
إنشاء (insha): A constructive speech act that brings about a legal effect, as opposed to ikhbar (report).
وعد (wa'd): A promise that does not constitute an oath and is not legally binding.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a valid oath requires constructive (insha) wording and customary usage; phrases that are promises, reports, or swearing by other than Allah are not binding.

Letters Used in Oaths
Enumeration and rules of letters used in swearing oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the letters of oath recognized by the Hanafi school and explain the grammatical restrictions for each letter's use.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh recognizes a broader set of oath letters than other schools, each with specific usage rules.

Oaths (yamin) are a significant part of Islamic legal practice, used to affirm or deny statements with binding consequences. The Arabic language employs specific particles to form oaths. This lesson examines the letters recognized by the Hanafi school, their grammatical constraints, and the rules for their valid application. Understanding these letters is essential for correctly forming and interpreting oaths in legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary letters
The base text lists waw, ba, and ta as the most frequently used letters. Note that waw enters only on explicit nouns (muzhar), ba on both explicit and implicit (mudmar), and ta only on the name 'Allah'.
These three are the most common in oaths.
2
Examine additional letters from the commentary
The commentary adds lam (kasra), ha, and mim. Lam enters only on 'Allah' for serious matters. Ha and mim have variations in vowel endings and alif usage, but all are valid.
These reflect the broader Hanafi acceptance.
3
Understand the rule of implicit oath letters
The footnote explains that oath letters may be omitted (implicit), and the oath remains valid because Arab custom allows dropping the particle for brevity.
This principle expands the scope of valid oaths.
4
Apply the rules to a sample statement
Given an expression, identify whether it uses a valid letter and whether the grammatical condition is met. For example, 'wallahi' uses waw with explicit noun (valid), 'bika' uses ba with pronoun (valid letter but may be invalid if swearing by other than Allah).
Focus on letter permissibility, not the object of oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

٢ .ومنها : ها محذوف الألف أو مع الألف مع وصل ألف الله له وقطعها. ٣. ومنها : الميم المكسورة أو المضمومة نحو م الله، وجاء الفتح أيضاً. كذا ذكره الدماميني في «شرح التسهيل». [١] قوله : كالله ؛ بالنصب بحذف الجار، وإيصال الفعل إليه، أو بالجر بإبقاء أثر المحذوف، وجاز الرفع أيضاً. [٢] قوله : لأفعله ؛ هذا مجرَّدُ تصوير لإظهار الحرف وإلا فالحلف في الإثبات لا يكون إلا بحرفِ التأكيد، وهو اللام والنون في المقسم عليه نحو : والله لأفعلن كذا. كذا في «تنوير الأبصار».
(1) أي قد تضمر حروف القسم فيكون حلفاً؛ لأن حذف الحرف من عادة العرب إيجازاً. ينظر : مجمع الأنهر(١ : ٥٤٢).
The letters of oath: waw, ba, and ta. [3] He said: 'waw... etc.'; he mentioned the frequently used letters of oath, which are waw and ba. 1. Waw enters only on the explicit noun. 2. Ba enters on both explicit and implicit. And ta (with two dots above) enters only on the word Allah. Among the letters of oath: 1. The kasra lam, and it enters only on the name of Allah for great matters, like 'lillahi la-af'alanna'. 2. Among them: ha with the alif omitted or with the alif, with connection and cutting of the alif of Allah. 3. Among them: the kasra or dhamma mim like 'mi-llah', and fatha is also mentioned. Thus mentioned by al-Damamini in Sharh al-Tashil. [1] He said: 'kallah' is in nasb by dropping the preposition and attaching the verb, or in jar by preserving the effect of the dropped, and also raf is allowed. [2] He said: 'la-af'alahu' is just an illustration to show the letter; otherwise swearing in affirmation only occurs with the emphatic letter, which is lam and nun on the sworn thing, like 'wallahi la-af'alanna'. (1) i.e., the letters of oath may be implicit, then it is an oath, because dropping the letter is the habit of the Arabs for brevity.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the letters used in oaths. The base text mentions three common letters: waw, ba, and ta. The commentary explains each: waw only precedes explicit nouns (e.g., 'wallahi'), ba can precede both explicit and implicit pronouns (e.g., 'bika' is not valid, but 'billahi'), and ta only precedes the word 'Allah'. Additional letters include lam (only for Allah in serious matters), ha (with various alif forms), and mim (with kasra or dhamma). The commentary further discusses the phrase 'kallah' (by Allah) and the expression 'la-af'alahu' as an example of the emphatic lam used in oaths. The footnote explains that letters of oath can be omitted (implicit), and the oath is still valid because Arabs often omit the letter for brevity.

Key Terms
حروف القسم (huruf al-qasm): The particles used to form an oath in Arabic, such as waw, ba, ta, etc.
المظهر (al-muzhar): An explicit noun (not a pronoun).
المضمر (al-mudmar): A pronoun (implicit).
القسم الضمني (al-qasm al-dimni): An implicit oath where the particle is omitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Waw
B) Ba
C) Ta
D) Lam

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh recognizes a wide range of oath letters, each with specific grammatical rules, and permits implicit oaths, reflecting a flexible approach to validating oaths.

The Expiation (Kaffara) for Breaking an Oath
Ruling on expiation of an oath with three options and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three valid options for expiation of an oath and identify what is sufficient for the clothing option according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Expiation for an oath requires freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them; a waist-wrap alone is insufficient.

This lesson examines the ruling on expiation (kaffara) for breaking a valid oath. The text specifies three options: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor persons, or clothing them. The commentary clarifies the conditions for each, emphasizing that the clothing must cover most of the body, so a sarawil (waist-wrap) is insufficient but a shirt suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is breaking a valid oath (hanth). The ruling is that expiation (kaffara) becomes wajib upon the violation.
The cause is hanth, not the oath itself.
2
List the three expiation options
The options are: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor persons, or clothing ten poor persons.
Each option is sufficient; one may choose.
3
Examine the conditions for each option
For freeing a slave: the slave must not be a mahram relative inherited. For feeding: it can be literal ten individuals or one person over ten days, with each meal equivalent to fitr. For clothing: each garment must cover the majority of the body.
The text provides specific qualifications.
4
Identify what is insufficient for clothing
A waist-wrap (sarawil) alone is insufficient because it does not cover most of the body; a shirt is sufficient.
This prevents incomplete fulfillment.
5
Relate to previous rulings
The conditions mirror those for zhihar expiation, ensuring consistency in the madhhab.
Cross-reference strengthens understanding.

This passage is a ruling statement on the expiation (kaffara) for breaking an oath. The text states that the expiation is freeing a slave, or feeding ten poor persons, or clothing them. Consecutive Fasting in Oath Expiation: Hanafi Proof and Shafi'i Dispute
Definition of expiation and Hanafi proof for consecutive fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement of consecutive fasting for oath expiation, explain the proof from the qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud, and distinguish the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Fasting for oath expiation must be three consecutive days; the qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud binds the unqualified Qur'anic text.

Expiation (kaffarah) for a broken oath in Hanafi fiqh follows a sequence: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them. Only when unable to perform any of these financial expiations does fasting become wajib. But must the three days of fasting be consecutive? The Qur'anic verse (5:89) simply says 'three days', but the Hanafi school, based on a well-known qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud, requires consecutiveness. This lesson examines the Hanafi proof, the Shafi'i counterargument, and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is expiation (kaffarah) for a broken oath. It is wajib upon occurrence of hanth (violation). The options are: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, clothing them, or if unable, fasting three days.
Fasting is only wajib when financial expiation is not possible at the time of performance.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary dalil is Qur'an 5:89, which lists the expiation options. The verse is mutawatir (qat'i al-thubut) but its dalalah (meaning) regarding consecutiveness is clarified by a qira'a.
The verse: 'So its expiation is feeding ten poor persons... or freeing a slave. But whoever cannot find [these] then a fast of three days.'
3
Note the ikhtilaf
The dispute is between the Hanafi school (requiring consecutive days) and al-Shafi'i (permitting separation). Each relies on a different reading of the same verse.
Hanafis use the qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud adding 'mutatabi'at' (consecutive); Shafi'i relies on the unqualified mutawatir text.
4
Examine the Hanafi dalil (Ibn Mas'ud qira'a)
The qira'a 'fasiyamu thalathati ayyam mutatabi'at' is mashhura (well-known). In Hanafi usul, a mashhur qira'a can restrict (taqyid) the unqualified mutawatir, because it functions as tafsir (explanation) of the Quran.
This qira'a is not abrogated and is considered an authoritative interpretation.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that the qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud clarifies the intended meaning of the verse. Since it is well-known and transmitted with chains of transmission, it is binding. The Shafi'i reliance on the apparent unqualified wording is overridden by this explanatory reading.
The thamarah (practical consequence): Hanafi mufta bihi requires consecutive fasting; Shafi'i allows non-consecutive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتضمر : كالله لأفعله. وكفارتُهُ : عتق رقبة، أو إطعام عشرة مساكين، كما مر في الظهار، أو كسوتُهم لكل ثوب يستر عامة بدنه ، فلم يجز السراويل فإن عجز عنها وقت الأداء ) : أي عجز عن الأشياء الثلاثة وقت إرادة الأداء . قوله : وقـت إرادة الأداء ؛ أشار به إلى أنَّ المضاف في قوله : في وقت الأداء محذوف، فإنّ المعتبر في وجودِ الصوم هو العجز عن الكفَّارة المالية . عند قصد أداء الكفّارة لا عنده، ولا قبله قبل الحنث أو بعده ، ولا بعد الأداء، فلو حنث موسراً، ثمّ أعسر جاز له الصوم، وفي عكسه لا، وعند الشافعي له على العكس. كذا ذكره الزَّيْلَعِي في «شرح الكنز». ولو وهب ماله لرجل وسلّمه، ثمّ صام ثم رجع إلى هبته أجزأه الصوم. كذا في «المجتبى». والشرط : هو العجز واستمراره إلى الفراغ من الصوم، ولو نسي المال فصام لم يجز على الصحيح، كما في «المجتبى». ولو صام المعسر يومين ثمّ أيسر يسراً اختيارياً أو غير اختياري ، كما إذا مات مورثه موسراً لا يجوز له الصوم، بل يستأنف بالمال، كذا في «الخانية». والأصل في هذا كله قوله حالة : فَكَفَّرَتُهُ إطْعَامُ عَشَرَةِ مَسَكِينَ مِنْ أَوْسَطِ مَا تُطْعِمُونَ أَهْلِيكُمْ أَوْ كِسْوَتُهُمْ أَوْ تَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ ثَلَثَةِ أَيَّامٍ ) ، فإنه يعلمُ منه أنَّ المعتبر في وجوب الصوم هو العجز عن الأشياء الثلاثة لا غيره، وأنَّ المعتبر استمراره، وإنما اعتبر وقت إرادة الأداء ؛ لأنه وقت وجوب الأداء. وبإطلاق قوله : فَصِيَامُ ثَلَثَةِ أَيَّامٍ ) (۳) استند الشافعي في جواز التفريق ، وعندنا يشترط التتابع ؛ لقراءة ابن مسعود ه فصيام ثلاثة أيام متتابعات، وهذه قراءة مشهورة، يجوز تقييد إطلاق القراءة المتواترة بها.
And it is implied: like 'by Allah, I will not do it'. Its expiation is: freeing a slave, or feeding ten poor persons, as passed in zihar, or clothing them with a garment that covers most of his body, so trousers are not sufficient. If he is unable to do them at the time of performance: meaning unable to do the three things at the time of intending performance. His saying 'at the time of intending performance' indicated that the genitive construction in 'at the time of performance' has something omitted, because what is considered for the existence of fasting is inability regarding the financial expiation at the time of intending to perform the expiation, not before it (neither before the violation nor after it), nor after performance. And the basis for all this is His saying, the Most High: 'So its expiation is feeding ten poor persons from the average of what you feed your families or clothing them or freeing a slave. But whoever cannot find [these] then a fast of three days.' For it is understood from it that what is considered for the obligation of fasting is inability regarding the three things, nothing else, and that its continuity is considered; and the time of intending performance is considered only because it is the time of obligation of performance. And by the unqualified nature of His saying 'a fast of three days', al-Shafi'i relied for permissibility of separation, but according to us tawabit (consecutiveness) is required due to the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'a fast of three consecutive days', and this is a well-known reading; it is permissible to restrict the unqualified nature of the mutawatir reading by it.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqaya, dealing with the expiation (kafara) for breaking an oath (yamin). It begins by explaining that the oath can be expressed implicitly by using the letter 'lam' without the explicit word 'qasam'. The expiation is identical to that for zihar: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor persons, or clothing them with a garment that covers most of the body. Trousers alone are insufficient because they do not cover the upper body; a shirt (qamis) is adequate. The passage then addresses the case of inability to perform these financial expiations. The key point is that the ability (yusr) or inability ('usr) is assessed at the time of intending to perform the expiation, not at the time of the broken oath or later. If one was wealthy when he broke the oath but becomes poor before performing expiation, he may fast. Conversely, if he was poor when he broke the oath but becomes wealthy before performing expiation, fasting is not allowed; he must give the financial expiation. This is the Hanafi position; al-Shafi'i holds the opposite view. Further practical scenarios are mentioned: if a person gives away his wealth to avoid expiation and then fasts, it suffices if he later takes it back; if he forgets he has wealth and fasts, it does not count. If a person starts fasting and becomes wealthy during the fast, he must abandon fasting and pay the financial expiation. The legal basis is Qur'an 5:89. The Hanafis deduce from the verse that fasting is only permitted when one is unable to do the three financial expiations, and this inability must continue throughout the fast. The main dispute with al-Shafi'i is about whether the three days must be consecutive (mutatabi'at). Al-Shafi'i relies on the unqualified phrase 'three days' to allow non-consecutive fasting. The Hanafis rely on the qira'a (reading) of Ibn Mas'ud, which adds 'consecutive' (mutatabi'at). This qira'a is mashhura (well-known) and can restrict the unrestricted reading of the mutawatir text, because it clarifies the intended meaning. Thus, for Hanafis, fasting must be three consecutive days.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; consists of freeing a slave, feeding ten poor persons, or clothing them; if unable, fasting three consecutive days.
تتابع (tataabu'): Consecutiveness of fasting days without break.
يمين (yamin): Oath; here it refers to a vow to do or not do something, with the formula 'by Allah' or similar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires three consecutive days for oath expiation fasting based on the mashhur qira'a of Ibn Mas'ud, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i position that permits separation.

Conditions for Substituting Fasting in Oath Expiation
Explains the condition of inability for fasting as substitute expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for substituting fasting in oath expiation and apply them to scenarios involving change in financial status.

Core Idea
Fasting is only a valid substitute for oath expiation if one is unable to perform the three primary expiations at the moment of intending performance.

This lesson examines the precise timing and conditions that allow a person to substitute fasting for the primary forms of oath expiation (feeding, clothing, freeing a slave). The Hanafi school emphasizes that the relevant inability is assessed at the time one intends to perform the expiation, not at the time of the oath or any other point. This seemingly technical detail has significant practical implications, as illustrated by several scenarios in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Fasting as a substitute for the three primary forms of oath expiation. What is the hukm? It is valid (sahih) only if certain conditions are met.
The ruling is conditional on inability.
2
Determine the primary condition
The text states the condition is 'inability' (عجز) at the time of intending performance (وقت إرادة الأداء). This means the person must be unable to feed, clothe, or free a slave at the moment they intend to fulfill the expiation.
Not at the time of the oath or after performance.
3
Understand the timing
Why is the time of intending performance key? The text explains that if someone is wealthy at the time of the oath but becomes poor before intending to perform, they may fast. Conversely, if poor at intending but becomes wealthy later, they cannot fast. This contrasts with the Shafi'i view that considers ability at the time of the oath.
The timing is critical for the ruling.
4
Explore sub-scenarios
The text gives cases: giving away wealth then fasting (valid), forgetting wealth (invalid), becoming wealthy during the fast (invalidates). Each illustrates the condition of continued inability.
These scenarios test the boundaries of the condition.
5
Connect to the proof
The Quranic verse 5:89 is cited: 'فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ' (whoever cannot find [the means], then fasting). The phrase 'لَّمْ يَجِدْ' indicates that the option to fast is conditional on current inability to perform the primary forms.
This is a dalil naqli with clear wajh al-dalalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then if he is unable at the time of performance: meaning unable to do the three things at the time of intending performance. His statement: time of intending performance; he indicated by this that the genitive construction in his statement 'at the time of performance' is deleted. For the consideration in the existence of fasting is inability to pay monetary expiation at the time of intending to perform the expiation, not at the time of the oath, nor before the oath or after it, nor after performance. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran, expanding on the conditions for substituting fasting in oath expiation. The base text states 'if he is unable at the time of performance', referring to the three forms of expiation (feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave). The commentator clarifies that the relevant time is when one intends to perform the expiation, not the time of the oath or after taking it. If someone is wealthy when he breaks his oath but later becomes poor, he may fast; conversely, if he is poor at the time of intending but becomes wealthy later, he must pay with wealth. Al-Shafi'i holds the opposite view. Additionally, if one gives away his wealth as a gift (thus becoming poor), fasts, then retracts the gift, the fast is valid. The condition is that inability must persist until the completion of the fast. Forgetting one's wealth and fasting does not suffice. If a poor person fasts two days then becomes wealthy (e.g., inherits), he must stop fasting and pay with wealth. The proof is Quran 5:89, which makes fasting conditional on not finding the means, showing that the consideration is inability at the time of performance and its continuation.

Key Terms
الْعَجْز (al-ʿajz): Inability to perform the three primary forms of expiation (feeding, clothing, freeing slave) at the time of intending performance.
وَقْتُ إِرَادَةِ الْأَدَاءِ (waqtu irādat al-adāʾ): The moment one intends to perform the expiation, not the time of the oath or after performance.
الْحَنِث (al-ḥanth): The breaking of an oath, which triggers the obligation of expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is wealthy when he breaks his oath, then becomes poor before performing expiation, what is the Hanafi ruling on fasting as expiation?
A) He may fast because he is now unable.
B) He cannot fast; he must pay with wealth.
C) He may fast only if he remains poor for the entire three days.
D) He may fast, but must repeat if he becomes wealthy during the fast.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school conditions the validity of fasting as oath expiation on genuine inability at the time of intending performance, and this inability must persist throughout the fast.

Requirement of Consecutive Fasting for Oath Expiation
Inter-madhhab dispute on consecutive fasting in oath expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires consecutive fasting for oath expiation and identify the usul principle that supports this.

Core Idea
The famous reading of Ibn Mas'ud, though not Quran, restricts the absolute text and makes consecutiveness obligatory.

The Quran orders fasting for three days for oath expiation without mentioning consecutiveness. Al-Shafi'i allows non-consecutive fasts. The Hanafi school requires consecutiveness based on a famous reading of Ibn Mas'ud. This lesson unpacks the usul principle of restricting an absolute with a restricted text and the Hanafi reliance on non-mutawatir but widely transmitted readings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The Quran says 'fasting for three days' (mutlaq). The Shafi'i school holds that non-consecutive fasts are sufficient. The Hanafi school requires consecutive days (tatābuʿ).
This is a dispute over the condition of the fasting act.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school cites the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'three consecutive days' (ثَلَاثَةُ أَيَّامٍ مُتَتَابِعَاتٍ). This reading is mashhūra (well-known) and constitutes a valid evidence for restricting the absolute.
This is a qirāʾa shādhdha but widely transmitted among the Companions.
3
Understand the usul principle
The Hanafi school applies the principle of carrying the absolute (muṭlaq) to the restricted (muqayyad) when both apply to the same ruling. The restriction is based on a reliable source that can add to the Quran.
Al-Taftazani clarifies that this is not an analogy from other expiations; the proof is the qirāʾa itself.
4
Note the exception for menstruation
If a woman menstruates during the three fasts, the consecutiveness is broken. She must start over after purification. This differs from the expiation for breaking Ramadan fast (two months), where menstruation does not break consecutiveness because the period is long.
The exception shows the sensitivity of the ruling to practical hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبإطلاق قوله : فَصِيَامُ ثَلَثَةِ أَيَّامٍ ) استند الشافعي في جواز التفريق ، وعندنا يشترط التتابع ؛ لقراءة ابن مسعود ه فصيام ثلاثة أيام متتابعات، وهذه قراءة مشهورة، يجوز تقييد إطلاق القراءة المتواترة بها. قال الشارح الله في «التنقيح» وشرحه «التوضيح» عند «بحث حمل المطلق على المقيد»: «وإن دخلا ؛ أي المطلق والمقيّد على الحكم ؛ أي صورة اتحاد الحادثة، نحو فصيام ثلاثة أيام مع قراءة ابن مسعود ، وهي ثلاثة أيام متتابعات، فإن الحكم ثلاثة أيام من غير تقييد ؛ لوجوب التتابع، وفي قراءة ابن مسعود وجوب صوم ثلاثة أيام متتابعات يحمل بالاتفاق لامتناع الجمع بينهما، فإنّ المطلق يوجب إجزاء غير المتتابع، والمقيد يوجب عدم إجزائه». انتهى (۲) وقال التفتازاني في «التلويح»: «في المثال إشارة إلى الجواب عما يقال : إنكم حملتم المطلق، وهو كفارة اليمين على المقيد في حادثة أخرى ، وهو كفارة الظهار والقتل، حيث شرطتم التتابع في الصوم يعني إنما حملناه على مقيد. وأراد في هذه الحادثة، وهو قراءة ابن مسعود الله ، فإنّها مشهورة، وبمثلها يزاد على الكتاب ، بخلاف قراءة أبي له فعده من أيام أخر متتابعة في قضاء رمضان، فإنها شادة لا يزادُ بمثلها على النص، والشافعي إنّما لم يشترط التتابع ؛ لأنه لا عمل عنده بالقراءة الغير المتواترة مشهورة كانت أو غير مشهورة». انتهى (۳) [١] قوله : ولاء ؛ - بكسر الواو مع المد بمعنى التتابع والتوالي، فإن حاضت المرأة في أثنائه بطل التتابع، ولزم عليها صوم ثلاثة أيام متتابعة بعد الطهارة، بخلاف كفارة الإفطار من رمضان، فإنّ الحيض لا يبطل تتابع الصوم فيها ؛ لأن مدة الصيام فيها وهو شهران لا تخلو عن الحيض غالباً. [٢] قوله : تجب الكفارة ؛ أي بالحنث، ولا يجوز عنها ما أدّاه قبل الحنث، إلا أنه لا يسترد من المسكين ما دفع إليه قبل الحنث ؛ لوقوعه صدقة. كذا في «الهداية». [٣] قوله : خلافا للشافعي الله ؛ له ظاهر رواية أبي داود والنسائي : «إنَّ رسول الله قال لعبد الرحمن بن سمرة : إذا حلفت على يمين فرأيت غيرها خيراً منها فكفّر عن يمينك، ثم ائت بالذي هو خير وأورد عليه : بأنّ ظاهره يقتضي وجوب تقديم الكفّارة على الحنث، وهو خلافُ
And by the general sense of His statement: 'then fasting for three days', al-Shafi'i relied on the permissibility of discontinuing (non-consecutive fasts), while with us we require consecutiveness, due to the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'fasting for three consecutive days'. This is a well-known reading, and it is permissible to restrict the general sense of the mutawatir reading by it. The commentator said in 'al-Tanqih' and its commentary 'al-Tawdih' under 'Discussion of carrying the absolute upon the restricted': 'And if both, i.e., the absolute and the restricted, enter upon the ruling, i.e., the case of unity of the incident, such as fasting for three days with the reading of Ibn Mas'ud, which is three consecutive days, then the ruling is three days without restriction due to the obligation of consecutiveness, and in the reading of Ibn Mas'ud the obligation of fasting three consecutive days is adopted by consensus because it is impossible to combine them. For the absolute requires the validity of the non-consecutive, while the restricted requires its invalidity.' End. And al-Taftazani said in 'al-Talwih': 'The example indicates the answer to what is said: You have carried the absolute, which is the expiation of the oath, upon the restricted in another incident, i.e., the expiation of zihar and killing, where you required consecutiveness in fasting. Meaning, we only carried it upon a restricted. He intended in this incident, which is the reading of Ibn Mas'ud, for it is well-known, and by such, addition is made to the Book, unlike the reading of Ubayy: 'count from other days consecutive' in making up Ramadan, for it is anomalous; no addition is made by it to the text. And al-Shafi'i did not require consecutiveness because he does not act upon non-mutawatir readings, whether famous or not.' End. [1] His statement: 'consecutive' - with a kasra on the waw and with madd, meaning continuity and succession. So if a woman menstruates in the midst of it, the consecutiveness is nullified, and she must fast three consecutive days after purification, unlike the expiation for breaking the fast of Ramadan, because menstruation does not nullify the consecutiveness of the fast there, since the period of fasting there, which is two months, is rarely free from menstruation. [2] His statement: 'expiation becomes obligatory' - meaning due to the oath, and what was paid before the oath does not suffice for it, but it is not taken back from the poor person what was given before the oath because it occurred as charity. Thus in 'al-Hidayah'. [3] His statement: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' - He has the apparent meaning of the hadith of Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i: 'The Messenger of Allah said to Abd al-Rahman ibn Samra: If you swear an oath and then see something better than it, then expiate your oath and then do that which is better.' And it is objected that its apparent meaning implies the obligation of presenting the expiation before the oath, which is contrary.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i on two issues: the requirement of consecutive fasting in oath expiation, and the timing of expiation (before or after the oath). The Hanafi school requires three consecutive days of fasting for oath expiation, based on the famous reading of Ibn Mas'ud 'three consecutive days' (وهي قراءة مشهورة). Hanafi jurists argue that this reading, though not mutawatir, can restrict the absolute text of the Quran because it is well-known and widely transmitted. Al-Shafi'i rejects this, as he does not consider non-mutawatir readings to have legislative force. The commentators explain the usul principle of restricting an absolute (مطلق) by a qualified (مقيد) when both apply to the same ruling. Al-Taftazani clarifies that this is not an arbitrary analogy from another expiation; the proof is the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud, which is famous and can add to the Quran, unlike anomalous readings. The passage also discusses that if a woman menstruates during the three fasts, the consecutiveness is broken; she must start over after purification. This differs from the expiation for breaking the Ramadan fast (two consecutive months), where menstruation does not break consecutiveness. Additionally, the text states that expiation before the oath is invalid; if one gives money to a poor person before breaking the oath, it does not count as expiation, but the money is not taken back because it is a valid charity. Al-Shafi'i's position is based on a hadith that seems to suggest expiating before the oath. The Hanafi response is implicit: the apparent meaning is overridden by other evidences.

Key Terms
التَّتَابُع (al-tatābuʿ): Consecutive days of fasting without interruption. If broken (e.g., by menstruation), the fast must be restarted.
الْقِرَاءَةُ الْمَشْهُورَة (al-qirāʾa al-mashhūra): A non-mutawatir reading that is widely transmitted among the Companions and Qira'at scholars. It is considered evidence in Hanafi usul for restricting an absolute text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof for requiring consecutive fasting in oath expiation?
A) A hadith from the Prophet
B) The reading of Ibn Mas'ud 'three consecutive days'
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy from the expiation for breaking Ramadan fast

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi requirement of consecutive fasting for oath expiation is based on the famous qirāʾa of Ibn Mas'ud, which restricts the absolute Quranic command.

Timing of Expiation: Before or After the Oath?
Inter-madhhab dispute on timing of expiation relative to oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing expiation before breaking an oath and explain the basis for the difference with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Expiation is a consequence of breaking the oath; doing it beforehand is invalid and counts as charity.

The hadith 'Expiate your oath' seems to allow expiation before breaking the oath. The Hanafi school, however, holds that expiation is only valid after the oath is broken. This lesson examines the proof and reasoning behind the Hanafi position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Can one give expiation (e.g., feeding a poor person) before breaking the oath, or must it be after?
The dispute affects the validity of the act.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
Al-Shafi'i cites the apparent meaning (ẓāhir) of the hadith of Abd al-Rahman ibn Samra: 'If you swear an oath and see something better, then expiate your oath.' The phrase 'expiaate your oath' seems to permit expiation before breaking it.
This is a sunnah ahad hadith.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith as referring to expiation after the oath is broken. They also rely on the general principle that obligations (wājibāt) are not fulfilled before their cause (sabab) is complete.
The cause of the obligation is the breaking of the oath (ḥanth).
4
Determine the practical consequence
If one gives money to a poor person before breaking the oath, it does not count as expiation. However, the money is not taken back because it is a valid charity.
This protects the poor person's right to the charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الْكَفَّارَةُ قَبْلَ الْحَنِثِ (al-kaffāra qabla al-ḥanth): Performing the act of expiation before breaking the oath. In Hanafi, this is invalid and does not fulfill the obligation.
الْحَنِث (al-ḥanth): Breaking the oath; the act that triggers the obligation of expiation.

Key Takeaway

Timing of Kaffarah and Consecutive Fasting: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on timing and consecutiveness of kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the timing of kaffarah and the requirement of consecutive fasting, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using hadith and usul principles.

Core Idea
Expiation before breaking an oath is invalid; fasting must be consecutive – the Hanafi school relies on well-known non-mutawatir readings.

This passage addresses two related points of contention between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding expiation of oaths: (1) can expiation be performed before the oath is broken? and (2) must the fast of kaffarah be three consecutive days? The Hanafi school holds that expiation before hanth is invalid, and fasting must be consecutive. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees on both counts. The passage provides the Hanafi proofs from the reading of Ibn Mas'ud and reconciliation of hadith narrations, and critiques the Shafi'i reliance on a single narration with 'thumma' (then).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disputed issues
The passage discusses (a) whether kaffarah can be done before breaking the oath (hanth), and (b) whether the three-day fast must be consecutive (mutatabi'at).
Both issues are resolved by the Hanafi school with the same evidence.
2
State the Hanafi position and its primary evidence
Hanafi: expiation before hanth is invalid; fasting must be consecutive. Evidence: the well-known (mashhurah) reading of Ibn Mas'ud adds 'متتابعات' (consecutive) to the Qur'anic verse, restricting the absolute; also, hadith narrations with 'waw' (and) show no order, and any 'thumma' narration is interpreted as 'waw' or abrogated.
The Hanafi school accepts mashhurah readings as additions to the text, unlike Shafi'i.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proof
Shafi'i: expiation is valid before hanth; fasting does not need to be consecutive. Proof: the hadith of Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah with the wording 'then expiate... then do what is better' (thumma), implying order; also, he does not act on non-mutawatir readings, so no restriction on the absolute command.
Shafi'i considers only mutawatir readings authoritative for adding to the text.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i evidence
Hanafi response: (1) The 'thumma' narration is counterbalanced by multiple authentic narrations using 'waw', which indicates conjunction without order; 'thumma' is therefore interpreted as 'waw' to reconcile. (2) The apparent obligation of advancing expiation is abrogated by consensus; for Hanafis, abrogation of obligation also removes permissibility, whereas Shafi'i holds that permissibility remains.
The Hanafi usul principle: when a command is abrogated, its permissibility also ends, unless a new proof establishes it.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In practice: Hanafi requires consecutive fasting and forbids expiation before hanth; Shafi'i allows expiation before hanth and does not require consecutive fasting. This affects how a person should act when they intend to break an oath.
The difference is real and has legal consequences for the validity of expiation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صام ثلاثة أيام ولاء، ولم يجز بلا حنث، التكفير قبل الحنث لا يجوز عندنا حتى لو كفر قبل الحنث، ثُمَّ حنث تَجِبَ الكفارة خلافاً للشافعي (1) الحكم وجوب صوم ثلاثة أيام متتابعات يحمل بالاتفاق لامتناع الجمع بينهما، فإنّ المطلق يوجب إجزاء غير المتتابع، والمقيد يوجب عدم إجزائه». انتهى وقال التفتازاني في «التلويح»: «في المثال إشارة إلى الجواب عما يقال : إنكم حملتم المطلق، وهو كفارة اليمين على المقيد في حادثة أخرى ، وهو كفارة الظهار والقتل، حيث شرطتم التتابع في الصوم يعني إنما حملناه على مقيد. وأراد في هذه الحادثة، وهو قراءة ابن مسعود الله ، فإنّها مشهورة، وبمثلها يزاد على الكتاب ، بخلاف قراءة أبي له فعده من أيام أخر متتابعة في قضاء رمضان، فإنها شادة لا يزادُ بمثلها على النص، والشافعي إنّما لم يشترط التتابع ؛ لأنه لا عمل عنده بالقراءة الغير المتواترة مشهورة كانت أو غير مشهورة». انتهى [١] قوله : ولاء ؛ - بكسر الواو مع المد بمعنى التتابع والتوالي، فإن حاضت المرأة في أثنائه بطل التتابع، ولزم عليها صوم ثلاثة أيام متتابعة بعد الطهارة، بخلاف كفارة الإفطار من رمضان، فإنّ الحيض لا يبطل تتابع الصوم فيها ؛ لأن مدة الصيام فيها وهو شهران لا تخلو عن الحيض غالباً. [٢] قوله : تجب الكفارة ؛ أي بالحنث، ولا يجوز عنها ما أدّاه قبل الحنث، إلا أنه لا يسترد من المسكين ما دفع إليه قبل الحنث ؛ لوقوعه صدقة. كذا في «الهداية». [٣] قوله : خلافا للشافعي الله ؛ له ظاهر رواية أبي داود والنسائي : «إنَّ رسول الله قال لعبد الرحمن بن سمرة : إذا حلفت على يمين فرأيت غيرها خيراً منها فكفّر عن يمينك، ثم ائت بالذي هو خير وأورد عليه : بأنّ ظاهره يقتضي وجوب تقديم الكفّارة على الحنث، وهو خلافُ المذهب، بل لم يقل به أحد ممن يعتد به، فإنّ الخلاف إنما هو في جواز التفكير قبل الحنث، وإجزائه لا في وجوبه. وأجيب عنه : بأن وجوب التقديم منسوخ بالإجماع، وبنسخ وجوب شيء لا ينتفي الجواز وهذا عنده ، وعندنا إذا نسخ وجوب المأمور به بطل جوازه أيضاً، ولا يثبت إلا بدليل آخر ، وليطلب تفصيل هذا البحث من «نور الأنوار شرح المنار» وحواشيه للوالد العلام أدخله الله دار السلام، المسماة بـ«قمر الأقمار». ثمّ إنّ المروي في «الصحيحين عن عبد الرحمن بن سمرة هو لفظ : «فات الذي هو خير، وكفّر عن يمينك» (١) ، وفي رواية لأبي داود: «فكفّر عن يمينك وانت بالذي هو خير(۲) (۱) ، وعند مسلم من حديث أبي هريرة مرفوعاً: «من حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيراً منها فليأت الذي هو خير وليكفّر عن يمينه (۳)، وفي «الصحيحين» مرفوعاً: «لا أحلف على : يمين ، فأرى غيرها خيراً منها إلا أتيت الذي هو خير وتحلّلت عن يميني». فهذه الروايات الواردة بحرف الواو» الموضوع للجمع المطلق من غير تقديم و تأخير، سواء كان ذكر التفكير مقدماً أو مؤخّراً لا دلالة لها على تقديم الكفّارة على الحنث حجّة لنا، وكذا ما ورد في بعض الروايات : «فليأت بالذي هو خير وليكفّر عن يمينه»، فتحمل كلمة ثم في الرواية السابقة على معنى «الواو»، جمعاً بين الروايات المختلفة. [١]قوله : سبب الكفارة ؛ أي سبب وجوبها ؛ فإنّها تضاف إلى اليمين، قال الله ذلك كفّارة أيمانكم إذا حلفتم وهذا آية السبية، فإن الواجبات تضاف إلى أسبابها حقيقة، كما يقال : كفّارةُ القتل وكفّارة الظهار وكفّارة الإفطار وغير ذلك. ونحن نقول : إن السبب ما يكون مفضياً إليه، واليمين لا تكون مفضية إلى الكفّارة، فإنها تعقد للبرّ، وإنما تجب الكفّارة بعد نقض اليمين بالحنث، وإنما أضيفت
He fasted three days consecutively, and it is not valid without breaking the oath. Expiation before breaking the oath is not permissible according to us, even if he expiates before breaking the oath, then he breaks the oath, expiation becomes obligatory, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (1) The ruling: the obligation of fasting three consecutive days is understood by agreement because combining them is impossible, for the absolute renders non-consecutive fasting valid, and the restricted renders it invalid. End. And al-Taftazani said in al-Talwih: 'In the example is an indication of the answer to what is said: You have applied the absolute, which is the expiation of the oath, to the restricted in another case, which is expiation of dhihar and killing, where you stipulated consecutiveness in fasting – meaning we only applied it to the restricted. And he intended in this case the reading of Ibn Mas'ud, for it is well-known, and by such [readings] addition to the Book is made, unlike the reading of Abi [who] counted it as other consecutive days in making up Ramadan, for it is anomalous, and the text is not increased by such [readings]. And al-Shafi'i only did not stipulate consecutiveness because he does not act upon non-mutawatir readings, whether well-known or not.' End. [1] His saying: 'consecutively' – with a kasra on the waw with elongation, meaning succession and sequence. If a woman menstruates during it, the consecutiveness is invalidated, and she must fast three consecutive days after purification, unlike the expiation of breaking fast in Ramadan, because menstruation does not invalidate the consecutiveness of fasting there, because the period of fasting there, which is two months, is rarely free from menstruation. [2] His saying: 'expiation becomes obligatory' – i.e., upon the breaking of the oath, and what he performed before the breaking of the oath is not valid for it, except that he does not take back from the poor person what he gave him before the breaking of the oath, because it occurred as charity. Thus in al-Hidayah. [3] His saying: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' – there is the apparent meaning of the narration of Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i: 'The Messenger of Allah said to Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah: If you swear an oath and see something better, then expiate for your oath, then do what is better.' And it is objected that its apparent meaning necessitates the obligation of advancing expiation before the breaking of the oath, which is contrary to the school. Rather, no one among those considered has said it, for the disagreement is only about the permissibility of expiating before breaking the oath and its validity, not about its obligation. And it was answered: that the obligation of advancement is abrogated by consensus, and by abrogation of the obligation of something, the permissibility is not negated – this is according to him. And according to us, when the obligation of the commanded thing is abrogated, its permissibility is also invalidated, and it is only established by another proof. And let the detail of this investigation be sought from 'Nur al-Anwar Sharh al-Manar' and its glosses by the author, may Allah admit him to the abode of peace, named 'Qamar al-Aqmar'. Then, what is narrated in the two Sahihs from Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah is the wording: 'Do what is better, and expiate for your oath.' And in a narration by Abu Dawud: 'Expiate for your oath and do what is better.' And according to Muslim from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah raised: 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something better, let him do what is better and expiate for his oath.' And in the two Sahihs raised: 'I do not swear an oath and see something better except that I do what is better and expiate for my oath.' So these narrations that occur with the conjunction 'waw', which is for absolute conjunction without precedence or delay, whether the mention of expiation is before or after, have no indication of advancing expiation before breaking the oath – a proof for us. Similarly, what occurs in some narrations: 'Let him do what is better and let him expiate for his oath.' So the word 'thumma' in the previous narration is taken to mean 'waw', to reconcile the different narrations. [1] His saying: 'cause of expiation' – i.e., the cause of its obligation; for it is attributed to the oath. Allah says: 'That is the expiation of your oaths when you have sworn' – this is the verse of expiation. For obligations are attributed to their causes in reality, as it is said: expiation of killing, expiation of dhihar, expiation of breaking fast, and other than that. And we say: The cause is what leads to it, and the oath does not lead to expiation, for it is made for righteousness; expiation only becomes obligatory after breaking the oath by the breaking of the oath, and it is only attributed
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on two related issues of kaffarah al-yamin (expiation of oaths): (1) the timing of expiation—whether it can be performed before breaking the oath (hanth), and (2) the requirement of fasting three consecutive days for those who choose fasting as expiation. The Hanafi position is clear: expiation before hanth is not valid, and fasting must be three consecutive days. The passage begins by stating the ruling that if someone fasts three consecutive days before breaking the oath, it is not valid; expiation before hanth is impermissible. Even if one expiates before hanth, the expiation is not counted, and upon hanth, expiation becomes obligatory anew. This is contrary to al-Shafi'i. The commentator then explains the rationale: the obligation of three consecutive fasts is understood by agreement (ittifaq) because the absolute command to fast is restricted by the evidence (the reading of Ibn Mas'ud that adds 'consecutive days'). The Hanafi school uses the well-known (mashhurah) non-mutawatir reading of Ibn Mas'ud to qualify the absolute, whereas al-Shafi'i does not act upon any non-mutawatir reading. The passage then clarifies the meaning of 'consecutively' (wilaa') with examples: if a menstruating woman breaks consecutiveness, she must start over, unlike in kaffarah of Ramadan breaking, where menstruation does not break consecutiveness because the two-month period is rarely free from it. The key point about the timing dispute is addressed through the hadith of Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah, where the word 'thumma' (then) in some versions seems to order expiation before the better action, implying expiation before hanth. The Hanafi scholars respond by showing that other authentic narrations use 'waw' (and), which gives no order, and that the apparent obligation of advancement is abrogated by consensus. Moreover, even if the obligation were abrogated, permissibility remains according to al-Shafi'i, but according to the Hanafis, abrogation of obligation also invalidates permissibility unless a new proof establishes it. Finally, the passage discusses the linguistic and legal point that the oath is called the 'cause' of kaffarah in the Quranic verse, but Hanafis distinguish between the cause that leads to obligation (the oath alone does not; it is the hanth that triggers it) and a metaphorical attribution.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for violating an oath, consisting of feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.
حنث (ḥanth): Breaking an oath by doing what was sworn against or not doing what was sworn to.
ولاء (wilā'): Consecutiveness, without interruption.
متواتر (mutawātir): A reading or hadith transmitted by so many chains that collusion to lie is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone expiates for an oath before breaking it, then breaks the oath, what is the ruling?
A) The expiation is valid and no further expiation is needed.
B) The expiation is not valid and expiation must be performed again after the break.
C) The expiation is partially valid; only half is required again.
D) The expiation is valid only if done with the intention of delaying.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi school: expiation before breaking an oath is invalid, and fasting for kaffarah must be three consecutive days – due to the well-known reading of Ibn Mas'ud and reconciliation of hadith narrations.

Advancing Kaffarah Before Breaking: The Hanafi View
Dispute on advancing kaffarah before hanth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on advancing kaffarah and explain why it is not permitted, citing the key hadith and the sabab-shart distinction.

Core Idea
Kaffarah cannot precede its cause; the breaking of the oath, not the oath itself, is the cause of obligation.

A common question in oaths is whether one can expiate before breaking the oath. The Hanafi school firmly holds that kaffarah must come after the breaking, while Imam al-Shafi'i permits advancing it for financial expiations. This lesson examines the Hanafi position, its proof from the hadith, and the practical consequence of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Determine the exact point of contention: is it permissible to give kaffarah for an oath before breaking it (hanth)?
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
The Hanafi school says no; kaffarah is wajib only after breaking. The proof is that the breaking (hanth) is the cause (sabab) of obligation, and the oath is only a condition (shart).
Refer to the text: 'the breaking is the cause of kaffarah'.
3
Understand the Shafi'i counterargument
Shafi'is argue that the oath itself is the cause, and breaking is merely a condition for performance. They analogize to zakah, where one can pay before the year ends after possessing nisab.
The text mentions this analogy explicitly.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The key hadith uses 'waw' (and) which implies no order. Some narrations have 'thumma' (then), but Hanafis interpret it as 'waw' to reconcile. Thus, the hadith does not support advancing kaffarah.
Look at the Arabic: 'فات الذي هو خير، وكفّر عن يمينك'.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If someone gives kaffarah before breaking, according to Hanafis it does not count and must be repeated after breaking. According to Shafi'is, it suffices.
This is the real consequence for a layperson.
