كتاب الأيمان/تعريفه وأقسامه
فعنده اليمين سبب الكفارة ، والحنث شرط وجوب الأداء
والجواب عنه : بأن وجوب التقديم منسوخ بالإجماع، وبنسخ وجوب شيء لا ينتفي الجواز وهذا عنده ، وعندنا إذا نسخ وجوب المأمور به بطل جوازه أيضاً، ولا يثبت إلا بدليل آخر.
ثمّ إنّ المروي في «الصحيحين عن عبد الرحمن بن سمرة هو لفظ : «فات الذي هو خير، وكفّر عن يمينك» ، وفي رواية لأبي داود: «فكفّر عن يمينك وانت بالذي هو خير»، وعند مسلم من حديث أبي هريرة مرفوعاً: «من حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيراً منها فليأت الذي هو خير وليكفّر عن يمينه، وفي «الصحيحين» مرفوعاً: «لا أحلف على يمين ، فأرى غيرها خيراً منها إلا أتيت الذي هو خير وتحلّلت عن يميني». فهذه الروايات الواردة بحرف الواو» الموضوع للجمع المطلق من غير تقديم و تأخير، سواء كان ذكر التفكير مقدماً أو مؤخّراً لا دلالة لها على تقديم الكفّارة على الحنث حجّة لنا، وكذا ما ورد في بعض الروايات : «فليأت بالذي هو خير وليكفّر عن يمينه»، فتحمل كلمة ثم في الرواية السابقة على معنى «الواو»، جمعاً بين الروايات المختلفة.
[1] قوله : سبب الكفارة ؛ أي سبب وجوبها ؛ فإنّها تضاف إلى اليمين، قال الله ذلك كفّارة أيمانكم إذا حلفتم وهذا آية السبية، فإن الواجبات تضاف إلى أسبابها حقيقة، كما يقال : كفّارةُ القتل وكفّارة الظهار وكفّارة الإفطار وغير ذلك. ونحن نقول : إن السبب ما يكون مفضياً إليه، واليمين لا تكون مفضية إلى الكفّارة، فإنها تعقد للبرّ، وإنما تجب الكفّارة بعد نقض اليمين بالحنث، وإنما أضيفت إليها لوجوبها بحنث بعد اليمين، كما تضافُ الكفّارة إلى الصوم. كذا في «العناية» و«البناية».
[1] قوله : فيجوز التقديم عليه ؛ أي على الحنث لا على اليمين ؛ لجواز تقديم شيء على وجوب أدائه، بعد وجوب سببه بالاتفاق ؛ ولهذا يجوز تقديم الزكاة على حولان الحولين، لكن بعد ملك النصاب الذي هو سبب للوجوب، ولا يجوز تقديمه قبل ملك النصاب ؛ لأنه تقديم على السبب، وهو غير جائز اتفاقاً.
[2] قوله : للبرّ ؛ - بكسر الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الراء - ، يقال : برأت في القول واليمين، أبر فيهما بروراً ؛ إذا صدقت فيهما. والحاصل : إنّ انعقاد اليمين إنّما يكونُ بقصدِ البرّ وإتيان ما حلف عليه، وصدقه في حلفه، وهو المأمور به شرعاً ؛ لقوله : واحفظوا أيمنكُمْ )، فلا تكون اليمين مفضية إلى الكفّارة، وإنّما المفضي إليها الحنث إذا وجد بعد اليمين، فيكون هو السبب، واليمين تكون شرطاً للوجوب، فلا يجوز تقديمُ الكفّارة على الحنث ؛ لكونه تقديماً على السبب.
[3] قوله : فلا تقدم ؛ أي الكفّارة ؛ وهو إما مضارع مجهول من التقديم، وإما مضارع معروف من التقدم، بحذف إحدى التائين.
[4] قوله : في الكفارة المالية ؛ كالتحرير والإطعام والكسوة ، وأما الكفارة بالعبادات البدنية كالصوم فلا يجزئ تقديمه على الحنث، إذ لا يعقل افتراق الوجوب عن وجوب الأداء في العبادات البدنية.
Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections. According to him, the oath is the cause of expiation, and the breaking (of the oath) is the condition for the obligation of performance. The answer to it: that the obligation of advancing (expiation) is abrogated by consensus, and by the abrogation of the obligation of a commanded thing, its permissibility is also negated—this is according to him. And according to us, when the obligation of the commanded thing is abrogated, its permissibility is also nullified, and it is not established except with another evidence. Then, what is narrated in the Two Sahihs from Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah is the wording: 'Do what is better, and expiate for your oath.' And in a narration by Abu Dawud: 'So expiate for your oath and do what is better.' And according to Muslim, from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah, raised (to the Prophet): 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him do what is better and expiate for his oath.' And in the Two Sahihs, raised: 'I do not swear an oath yet see something better than it except that I do what is better and break my oath.' These narrations that come with the letter waw (and), which is placed for absolute conjunction without precedence or delay—whether the mention of expiation is placed first or last—have no indication for advancing expiation before breaking (the oath) as evidence for us. Similarly, what is narrated in some narrations: 'Let him do what is better and expiate for his oath,' so the word 'thumma' (then) in the previous narration is interpreted to mean 'waw' (and), to reconcile the differing narrations.
[1] His saying: 'the cause of expiation' means the cause of its obligation; for it is attributed to the oath. Allah said: 'That is the expiation of your oaths when you have sworn' (Quran 5:89). This is the verse of obligation, for obligations are attributed to their causes in reality, as it is said: 'expiation of killing,' 'expiation of zihar (injurious divorce),' 'expiation of breaking the fast,' and so on. And we say: the cause is that which leads to it, and the oath does not lead to expiation, for it is made for truthfulness. Rather, expiation becomes obligatory only after breaking the oath by violating it. It is only attributed to the oath because it becomes obligatory due to breaking after the oath, just as expiation is attributed to fasting. So it is in al-Inayah and al-Binayah.
[1] His saying: 'So he allows advancement upon it' means upon the breaking, not upon the oath, because it is permissible to advance something before the obligation of its performance, after the obligation of its cause has been established by consensus. For this reason, it is permissible to advance zakah before the completion of the year (hawl), but only after possessing the nisab which is the cause of obligation. And it is not permissible to advance it before possessing the nisab, because that would be advancing before the cause, which is not permissible by consensus.
[2] His saying: 'for truthfulness' (li al-birr) – with a kasrah under the ba and shaddah on the ra – it is said: 'barartu fi al-qawl wa al-yamin, abarru fihima birran' if one is truthful in them. The conclusion: the conclusion of the oath is only for the intention of truthfulness and doing what one swore upon, and being truthful in his oath. This is what is commanded legally, due to His saying: 'And protect your oaths' (Quran 5:89). Thus, the oath does not lead to expiation. Rather, what leads to it is the breaking when it occurs after the oath, so it (the breaking) is the cause, and the oath is a condition for obligation. So it is not permissible to advance expiation before the breaking, because that would be advancing before the cause.
[3] His saying: 'so it (the expiation) is not advanced' – either a passive imperfect from 'taqdim' (to advance) or an active imperfect from 'taqaddum' (to advance oneself), with one of the two ta's omitted.
[4] His saying: 'regarding financial expiation' like freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing. As for expiation through bodily acts of worship like fasting, it is not valid to advance it before breaking, because the separation of obligation from the obligation of performance is not conceivable in bodily acts of worship.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussion on the permissibility of expiating an oath before breaking it. The text begins by presenting the view of 'him' (likely Abu Hanifa or the author of the base matn) that the oath itself is the cause (sabab) of kaffarah, and the breaking (hanth) is only a condition (shart) for the obligation of performance. According to this view, one may advance the kaffarah before breaking. The commentator then answers this by stating that the obligation of advancing kaffarah is abrogated by consensus, and that abrogation of obligation also nullifies permissibility unless proven otherwise. This is according to the opposing view; according to us (Hanafis), abrogating obligation also nullifies permissibility.

The text then provides hadith evidence. The narrations use the conjunction 'waw' (and), which does not imply order. The well-known hadith: 'Do what is better and expiate for your oath' does not indicate whether expiation comes before or after the better action. The narration with 'thumma' (then) is interpreted as 'waw' to reconcile the narrations.

The commentator then explains that according to the Hanafi school, the breaking is the true cause of kaffarah because the oath is made for truthfulness (birr). The oath does not lead to kaffarah; only the act of breaking leads to it. Therefore, advancing kaffarah before the cause (breaking) is invalid, just as one cannot pay zakah before possessing the nisab. The Shafi'i view differs regarding financial kaffarah because they distinguish between the obligation of the ruling (thubut al-wujub) and the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada). However, for bodily acts of worship like fasting, even they agree that advancement is invalid.

The passage reinforces the Hanafi position that the oath is a condition (shart) and the breaking is the cause (sabab). The key point is that kaffarah cannot precede its cause, which is the act of breaking. This is a core principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh concerning the relationship between causes and obligations.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, a solemn affirmation by Allah's name to do or not do something.
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking the oath by doing what one swore not to do, or not doing what one swore to do.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation required for breaking a valid oath; includes freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them; if unable, fasting three days.
السبب (al-sabab): Cause that directly leads to the obligation of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the operative cause (sabab) for the obligation of kaffarah in an oath?
A) The oath itself
B) The breaking of the oath (hanth)
C) The intention to break the oath
D) The command of the ruler

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, kaffarah for breaking an oath cannot be given before the breaking occurs; the breaking is the cause that triggers the obligation.

Sabab vs Shart: The Causal Structure of Oath Expiation
Usul analysis of sabab and shart in oath expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sabab (cause) and shart (condition) in the context of oath expiation and apply the Hanafi rule that the sabab must precede the obligation.

Core Idea
The breaking of the oath is the cause (sabab) of kaffarah; the oath is only a condition (shart) for its obligation.

A key usul distinction underlies the Hanafi ruling on oath expiation: the difference between cause (sabab) and condition (shart). The Shafi'i school treats the oath as the cause, while Hanafis treat the breaking as the cause. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how it affects the permissibility of advancing kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Define sabab and shart in Hanafi usul
Sabab is that which directly leads to the obligation (e.g., the breaking of the oath). Shart is a prerequisite that must be present for the sabab to be effective (e.g., the oath itself).
Refer to the text: 'the cause is that which leads to it'.
2
Identify the sabab and shart in oath expiation
According to Hanafis, the oath is a shart – it must exist for kaffarah to become due after breaking. The breaking (hanth) is the sabab because it is the actual act that necessitates expiation.
The text says: 'the breaking is the cause, and the oath is a condition for obligation'.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Shafi'is consider the oath itself as the sabab, and the breaking as a shart for performance. This leads to the analogy with zakah, where nisab is the sabab and the year is a shart.
The text presents this view and then refutes it.
4
Apply the distinction to the zakah analogy
In zakah, the cause (nisab ownership) is already present; the year is a condition for payment. In oaths, the cause (breaking) is not present until the oath is broken. Hence, advancing kaffarah is like paying zakah before owning nisab, which is invalid by consensus.
The passage argues this clearly.
5
Derive the ruling on advancing kaffarah
Since the sabab (breaking) has not occurred, advancing kaffarah is a 'taqdim ala al-sabab' (advancing before the cause), which is not permissible. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling is that kaffarah must be given after the hanth.
This is the practical outcome of the usul analysis.

Source Text

فعنده اليمين سبب الكفارة ، والحنث شرط وجوب الأداء
والجواب عنه : بأن وجوب التقديم منسوخ بالإجماع، وبنسخ وجوب شيء لا ينتفي الجواز وهذا عنده ، وعندنا إذا نسخ وجوب المأمور به بطل جوازه أيضاً، ولا يثبت إلا بدليل آخر.
ثمّ إنّ المروي في «الصحيحين عن عبد الرحمن بن سمرة هو لفظ : «فات الذي هو خير، وكفّر عن يمينك» ، وفي رواية لأبي داود: «فكفّر عن يمينك وانت بالذي هو خير»، وعند مسلم من حديث أبي هريرة مرفوعاً: «من حلف على يمين فرأى غيرها خيراً منها فليأت الذي هو خير وليكفّر عن يمينه، وفي «الصحيحين» مرفوعاً: «لا أحلف على يمين ، فأرى غيرها خيراً منها إلا أتيت الذي هو خير وتحلّلت عن يميني». فهذه الروايات الواردة بحرف الواو» الموضوع للجمع المطلق من غير تقديم و تأخير، سواء كان ذكر التفكير مقدماً أو مؤخّراً لا دلالة لها على تقديم الكفّارة على الحنث حجّة لنا، وكذا ما ورد في بعض الروايات : «فليأت بالذي هو خير وليكفّر عن يمينه»، فتحمل كلمة ثم في الرواية السابقة على معنى «الواو»، جمعاً بين الروايات المختلفة.
[1] قوله : سبب الكفارة ؛ أي سبب وجوبها ؛ فإنّها تضاف إلى اليمين، قال الله ذلك كفّارة أيمانكم إذا حلفتم وهذا آية السبية، فإن الواجبات تضاف إلى أسبابها حقيقة، كما يقال : كفّارةُ القتل وكفّارة الظهار وكفّارة الإفطار وغير ذلك. ونحن نقول : إن السبب ما يكون مفضياً إليه، واليمين لا تكون مفضية إلى الكفّارة، فإنها تعقد للبرّ، وإنما تجب الكفّارة بعد نقض اليمين بالحنث، وإنما أضيفت إليها لوجوبها بحنث بعد اليمين، كما تضافُ الكفّارة إلى الصوم. كذا في «العناية» و«البناية».
[1] قوله : فيجوز التقديم عليه ؛ أي على الحنث لا على اليمين ؛ لجواز تقديم شيء على وجوب أدائه، بعد وجوب سببه بالاتفاق ؛ ولهذا يجوز تقديم الزكاة على حولان الحولين، لكن بعد ملك النصاب الذي هو سبب للوجوب، ولا يجوز تقديمه قبل ملك النصاب ؛ لأنه تقديم على السبب، وهو غير جائز اتفاقاً.
[2] قوله : للبرّ ؛ - بكسر الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الراء - ، يقال : برأت في القول واليمين، أبر فيهما بروراً ؛ إذا صدقت فيهما. والحاصل : إنّ انعقاد اليمين إنّما يكونُ بقصدِ البرّ وإتيان ما حلف عليه، وصدقه في حلفه، وهو المأمور به شرعاً ؛ لقوله : واحفظوا أيمنكُمْ )، فلا تكون اليمين مفضية إلى الكفّارة، وإنّما المفضي إليها الحنث إذا وجد بعد اليمين، فيكون هو السبب، واليمين تكون شرطاً للوجوب، فلا يجوز تقديمُ الكفّارة على الحنث ؛ لكونه تقديماً على السبب.
[3] قوله : فلا تقدم ؛ أي الكفّارة ؛ وهو إما مضارع مجهول من التقديم، وإما مضارع معروف من التقدم، بحذف إحدى التائين.
[4] قوله : في الكفارة المالية ؛ كالتحرير والإطعام والكسوة ، وأما الكفارة بالعبادات البدنية كالصوم فلا يجزئ تقديمه على الحنث، إذ لا يعقل افتراق الوجوب عن وجوب الأداء في العبادات البدنية.
Book of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections. According to him, the oath is the cause of expiation, and the breaking (of the oath) is the condition for the obligation of performance. The answer to it: that the obligation of advancing (expiation) is abrogated by consensus, and by the abrogation of the obligation of a commanded thing, its permissibility is also negated—this is according to him. And according to us, when the obligation of the commanded thing is abrogated, its permissibility is also nullified, and it is not established except with another evidence. Then, what is narrated in the Two Sahihs from Abd al-Rahman ibn Samurah is the wording: 'Do what is better, and expiate for your oath.' And in a narration by Abu Dawud: 'So expiate for your oath and do what is better.' And according to Muslim, from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah, raised (to the Prophet): 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him do what is better and expiate for his oath.' And in the Two Sahihs, raised: 'I do not swear an oath yet see something better than it except that I do what is better and break my oath.' These narrations that come with the letter waw (and), which is placed for absolute conjunction without precedence or delay—whether the mention of expiation is placed first or last—have no indication for advancing expiation before breaking (the oath) as evidence for us. Similarly, what is narrated in some narrations: 'Let him do what is better and expiate for his oath,' so the word 'thumma' (then) in the previous narration is interpreted to mean 'waw' (and), to reconcile the differing narrations.
[1] His saying: 'the cause of expiation' means the cause of its obligation; for it is attributed to the oath. Allah said: 'That is the expiation of your oaths when you have sworn' (Quran 5:89). This is the verse of obligation, for obligations are attributed to their causes in reality, as it is said: 'expiation of killing,' 'expiation of zihar (injurious divorce),' 'expiation of breaking the fast,' and so on. And we say: the cause is that which leads to it, and the oath does not lead to expiation, for it is made for truthfulness. Rather, expiation becomes obligatory only after breaking the oath by violating it. It is only attributed to the oath because it becomes obligatory due to breaking after the oath, just as expiation is attributed to fasting. So it is in al-Inayah and al-Binayah.
[1] His saying: 'So he allows advancement upon it' means upon the breaking, not upon the oath, because it is permissible to advance something before the obligation of its performance, after the obligation of its cause has been established by consensus. For this reason, it is permissible to advance zakah before the completion of the year (hawl), but only after possessing the nisab which is the cause of obligation. And it is not permissible to advance it before possessing the nisab, because that would be advancing before the cause, which is not permissible by consensus.
[2] His saying: 'for truthfulness' (li al-birr) – with a kasrah under the ba and shaddah on the ra – it is said: 'barartu fi al-qawl wa al-yamin, abarru fihima birran' if one is truthful in them. The conclusion: the conclusion of the oath is only for the intention of truthfulness and doing what one swore upon, and being truthful in his oath. This is what is commanded legally, due to His saying: 'And protect your oaths' (Quran 5:89). Thus, the oath does not lead to expiation. Rather, what leads to it is the breaking when it occurs after the oath, so it (the breaking) is the cause, and the oath is a condition for obligation. So it is not permissible to advance expiation before the breaking, because that would be advancing before the cause.
[3] His saying: 'so it (the expiation) is not advanced' – either a passive imperfect from 'taqdim' (to advance) or an active imperfect from 'taqaddum' (to advance oneself), with one of the two ta's omitted.
[4] His saying: 'regarding financial expiation' like freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing. As for expiation through bodily acts of worship like fasting, it is not valid to advance it before breaking, because the separation of obligation from the obligation of performance is not conceivable in bodily acts of worship.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussion on the permissibility of expiating an oath before breaking it. The text begins by presenting the view of 'him' (likely Abu Hanifa or the author of the base matn) that the oath itself is the cause (sabab) of kaffarah, and the breaking (hanth) is only a condition (shart) for the obligation of performance. According to this view, one may advance the kaffarah before breaking. The commentator then answers this by stating that the obligation of advancing kaffarah is abrogated by consensus, and that abrogation of obligation also nullifies permissibility unless proven otherwise. This is according to the opposing view; according to us (Hanafis), abrogating obligation also nullifies permissibility.

The text then provides hadith evidence. The narrations use the conjunction 'waw' (and), which does not imply order. The well-known hadith: 'Do what is better and expiate for your oath' does not indicate whether expiation comes before or after the better action. The narration with 'thumma' (then) is interpreted as 'waw' to reconcile the narrations.

The commentator then explains that according to the Hanafi school, the breaking is the true cause of kaffarah because the oath is made for truthfulness (birr). The oath does not lead to kaffarah; only the act of breaking leads to it. Therefore, advancing kaffarah before the cause (breaking) is invalid, just as one cannot pay zakah before possessing the nisab. The Shafi'i view differs regarding financial kaffarah because they distinguish between the obligation of the ruling (thubut al-wujub) and the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada). However, for bodily acts of worship like fasting, even they agree that advancement is invalid.

The passage reinforces the Hanafi position that the oath is a condition (shart) and the breaking is the cause (sabab). The key point is that kaffarah cannot precede its cause, which is the act of breaking. This is a core principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh concerning the relationship between causes and obligations.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): Cause that directly leads to the obligation of a ruling.
الشرط (al-shart): Condition that must be present for the obligation to apply, but is not the direct cause.
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking the oath by doing what one swore not to do, or not doing what one swore to do.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, a solemn affirmation by Allah's name to do or not do something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The oath is the cause, the breaking is the condition
B) The breaking is the cause, the oath is the condition
C) Both are causes together
D) Neither is a cause; the obligation arises from the ruler's command

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the breaking of the oath (hanth) is the cause of kaffarah, while the oath itself is a condition; thus kaffarah cannot be given before the hanth.

Distinguishing Wujub from Wujub al-Ada
Analytical distinction between obligation and its performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between wujub (obligation) and wujub al-ada (obligation of performance) in Hanafi fiqh and apply this distinction to financial and bodily acts of worship.

Core Idea
Obligation attaches to the liability; obligation of performance demands discharge — they are separable.

In Hanafi legal theory, the concepts of wujub (obligation) and wujub al-ada (obligation of performance) are distinct yet often conflated. This lesson unpacks the nuanced difference, showing that a duty can be owed in principle while its execution may be deferred or conditional. Understanding this separation is crucial for correctly analyzing obligations such as prayer, fasting, and financial liabilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Start by recognizing that wujub refers to the initial attachment of a duty to the liability (dhimmah), while wujub al-ada refers to the necessity of actually performing and discharging that duty.
The text calls wujub the 'occupation of the liability' and wujub al-ada the 'necessity of discharging' it.
2
Understand the definitions from the text
Read the passage from Tanqih al-Usul: 'The difference between the obligation itself and the obligation of performance is that the first is the occupation of the accountable person's liability with something, and the second is the necessity of discharging the liability from what has attached to it.'
Note the temporal sequence: wujub must precede wujub al-ada.
3
Apply to financial and bodily examples
Consider the sale example: when a sale occurs with an unspecified price, the price becomes an obligation (wujub) in the buyer's liability, but the obligation to pay (wujub al-ada) arises only upon demand. For bodily acts like prayer, the cause (time) creates wujub of the ritual form, but wujub al-ada is the performance of that form.
The text emphasizes that wealth itself is not intended in Allah's rights; performance is the goal.
4
See the practical consequence
Examine the case of a sick traveler: the obligation to fast (wujub) is established because the cause (Ramadan) makes the form of fasting necessary in principle, but the obligation to perform (wujub al-ada) is not present during illness, allowing postponement.
This demonstrates that wujub can exist without wujub al-ada.

فنفس وجوبه يتعلق بالمال ووجوب الأداء بالفعل. قلنا : المال غير مقصود في حقوق الله تعالى، فالكفارة المالية وغير المالية على السواء، على أنّ نفس الوجوبِ ينفك عن وجوب الأداء في العبادات البدنية، فنفس الوجوبُ يتعلّق بالهيئة الحاصلة للعبادات، ووجوب الأداء يتعلق بإيقاع تلك الهيئة على ما حققناه في «شرح التنقيح). [٣] قوله : على ... الخ ; علاوة على مر ، قال الشارح» في «التوضيح»: «اعلم أن بعض العلماء لا يدركون الفرق بين نفس الوجوب وبين وجوب الأداء، فيقولون : إنّ الوجوب لا ينصرف إلا إلى الفعل، وهو الأداء، فبالضرورة يكون نفس الوجوب هو وجوب الأداء، والله درّ مَن أبدع الفرق بينهما ، وما أدق نظره ، وما أمتن حكمته. وتحقيق ذلك : إن الوقت لَمَّا كان سبباً لوجوب الصلاة كان معناه أنه لما حضر الوقت كان لازماً أن توجد فيه هيئة مخصوصة وضعت لعبادة الله . فلزوم وجود تلك الهيئة عقيب السبب، هو نفس الوجوب. وهي الصلاة، ثم الأداء : هو إيقاع تلك الهيئة، فوجوب الأداء هو لزوم إيقاع تلك الهيئة ، وذلك مبني على الأوّل ; لأنَّ السبب أوجب وجود تلك الهيئة لمناسبة بينهما، فإنّ المراد بالسبب الداعي، ثم بواسطة هذا الوجوب يجب إيقاعُ تلك الهيئة، فالوجوب الأوّل يتعلق بالهيئة وهي الصلاة، والثاني بأدائها. وقد يوجد الوجوب بدون وجوب الأداء، كما في المريض والمسافر، فإنّ لزوم وجود الحالة التي هي الصوم حاصل ; لأنَّ ذلك اللزوم باعتبار أن السبب داع إليه ، والمحل وهو المكلف. صالح لهذا ، فلو لم يحصل اللزوم لم يكن السبب سبباً ، لكن لا يجب إيقاعه، مع أنه يجوز أن يكون واقعاً. وإذا وجد البيع بثمن غير معيّن فالبيع مبادلة المال بالمال، وقد ملك المشتري المبيع، فلابد أن يملك البائع مالاً على المشتري تحقيقاً للمبادلة، فهذا نفس الوجوب، ثمّ لزوم أداء المال الواجب فرع على الأوّل ، فهو وجوب الأداء». انتهى
So the obligation itself relates to the wealth, and the obligation of performance relates to the act. We say: Wealth is not intended in the rights of Allah Almighty, so the financial expiation and the non-financial are equal. Moreover, the obligation itself is separable from the obligation of performance in bodily acts of worship. The obligation itself attaches to the form acquired for the acts of worship, and the obligation of performance attaches to the execution of that form, as we have verified in Sharh al-Tanqih. [1] His statement: 'relates to wealth' – this is an attributive distinction between obligation and obligation of performance in financial matters, for the obligation itself relates to the wealth itself, as it is the obligatory duty in the debt, while the obligation of performance relates to the act, i.e., its performance. The clearest expression of the difference is what the commentator mentioned in Tanqih al-Usul: 'The difference between the obligation itself and the obligation of performance is that the first is the occupation of the accountable person's liability with something, and the second is the necessity of discharging the liability from what has attached to it, so it must be preceded by a right in the liability. So when one buys something, the price is established in the liability, but the necessity of performance is upon demand, based on the original obligation.' [2] His statement: 'wealth...' etc. Summary: The wealth itself is not intended in Allah's rights related to wealth, like zakat and expiation and others; rather, the intention is its performance, just as the intention in bodily acts is also the performance of the act. So the financial and the bodily are equal. If it is not permissible to bring forward the bodily expiation before breaking the oath, based on the fact that its obligation is not separable from the obligation of performance, because the intention there is performance, then it is also not permissible in financial matters for the very same reason. [3] His statement: 'on...' etc. In addition to the above, the commentator said in al-Tawdih: 'Know that some scholars do not perceive the difference between the obligation itself and the obligation of performance. They say: Obligation can only be directed to the act, which is performance, so necessarily the obligation itself is the obligation of performance. Excellent is the one who invented the difference between them, how precise his insight, how firm his wisdom! The verification of this: When time is the cause for the obligation of prayer, its meaning is that when the time arrives, it is necessary that a specific form be performed, established for the worship of Allah. So the necessity of the existence of that form following the cause is the obligation itself. That form is prayer. Then performance is the execution of that form. Thus, the obligation of performance is the necessity of executing that form, and this is built upon the first. For the cause necessitates the existence of that form due to a suitability between them, as the intended meaning of the cause is the motivation. Then through this obligation, the execution of that form becomes obligatory. So the first obligation relates to the form, which is prayer, and the second relates to its performance. And obligation may exist without the obligation of performance, as in the sick and the traveler. For the necessity of the state which is fasting is established, because that necessity is based on the cause motivating it, and the subject, the accountable person, is suitable for it. So if the necessity were not established, the cause would not be a cause, but its execution is not obligatory, although it may occur. And when a sale occurs with an unspecified price, the sale is the exchange of wealth for wealth. The buyer has taken ownership of the sold item, so the seller must own wealth from the buyer to actualize the exchange. This is the obligation itself. Then the necessity of paying the obligatory wealth is a branch of the first, so it is the obligation of performance.' End.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analytical explanation of the distinction between wujub (obligation) and wujub al-ada (obligation of performance) in Hanafi legal theory. It addresses a common confusion among scholars who equate the two. The author clarifies that wujub is the initial attachment of a duty to the liability of the accountable person, while wujub al-ada is the necessity of fulfilling that duty. The distinction is illustrated with examples from financial obligations (e.g., price after a sale) and bodily acts (e.g., prayer and fasting). The key insight is that wujub can exist without wujub al-ada, as in the case of a sick person who must fast but is not obliged to execute it during his illness. This nuanced understanding is crucial for issues such as the timing of expiations and the nature of obligations in financial and non-financial acts of worship.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The initial obligation attaching to the accountable person's liability, meaning that something becomes a duty owed in the abstract.
وجوب الأداء (wujub al-ada): The obligation of performance, i.e., the necessity of executing and discharging the duty from the liability.
الهيئة (al-hay'ah): The specific form or manner of the act of worship, e.g., the movements and recitations of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the difference between wujub and wujub al-ada in the case of a sale with an unspecified price?
A) Wujub applies to the seller's liability for the price, while wujub al-ada applies to the buyer's delivery.
B) Wujub is the establishment of the price in the buyer's liability, and wujub al-ada is the necessity of paying it.
C) Wujub and wujub al-ada are the same in this context.
D) Wujub applies to the buyer's liability, and wujub al-ada applies to the seller's delivery.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi legal theory, wujub is the initial attachment of a duty to the liability, while wujub al-ada is the subsequent obligation to perform it; they can exist independently, as seen in cases of incapacity.

Ruling on Swearing to Commit a Sin and on an Infidel's Oath
States the ruling on oaths to commit sin and the absence of expiation for an infidel's oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing to commit a sin and identify the proof, and explain the distinction regarding the oath of an infidel.

Core Idea
Swearing to sin must be broken with expiation; the oath of an infidel has no expiation.

Oaths are serious matters in Islamic law, but what happens when a person swears to do something sinful? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling: such an oath must be broken and expiated, based on the principle of choosing the lesser of two evils. Additionally, we explore why an infidel's oath carries no expiation obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
In the matn, two distinct rules are stated: (1) If a person swears to commit a sin, he must break his oath (hanth) and pay expiation (kaffarah). (2) There is no expiation for the oath of an infidel, even if he later breaks it as a Muslim. Identify these in the Arabic text.
The Arabic phrase 'ومن حلف على معصيةٍ حنث وكفر' contains both rulings.
2
Trace the underlying principle
The commentary explains that breaking the oath is based on the principle: 'When faced with two trials, choose the lesser.' Breaking the oath (with expiation) is a lesser evil than committing the sin (without compensation). Understand that this makes breaking wajib.
The principle is 'من ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونهما'.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The commentary cites the hadith: 'Let him expiate for his oath and do that which is better.' In Hanafi usul, the imperative (فليكفّر) indicates obligation (wujub). Confirm that this hadith is the primary evidence for the ruling.
The hadith is referenced in Sahih al-Bukhari; the chain is zanni but the meaning is clear.
4
Distinguish the infidel's case
The matn states: 'There is no expiation in the oath of an infidel.' The commentary simply notes that an infidel is not obligated by Shari'ah regarding oaths. Understand that this applies even if he later becomes Muslim and breaks the oath.
This is because the obligation of kaffarah is tied to being a mukallaf (legally responsible) at the time of the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه [1]
ومن حلف على معصيةٍ كعدم الكلام مع أبويه حنث وكفر ، ولا كفارة في حلفِ كافر، وإن حنث مسلماً
[1] قوله : حنث ; هذا من فروع قاعدة : من ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونهما، فإنّ في الحنث تفويت البرّ إلى جابر، وهو الكفّارة، وفي البرّارتكاب للمعصية بلا جابر، والحنث في مثل هذه الصورة يصير واجباً ; لأنّ ارتكاب المعصية حرام، ولو كان غير المحلوف عليه أولى يكون الحنث مستحباً. والأصل في هذا كله حديث : «فليكفّر عن يمينه ، وليأت بالذي هو خير(٢)، كما مر ذكره، فإن كان المحلوفُ عليه أولى من غيره ، كقوله : والله لأصلين الضحى اليوم، وقوله : والله لا آكــل البصل، كان البر أولى، بل واجباً ، وكذا إذا كان المحلوف عليه وغيره متساويين. كذا حققه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير» [٢]قوله : ولا كفارة في حلف الكافر ; يعني إذا حلف كافر على فعل أو ترك ، ثمّ (۳)
Chapter of Oaths / Its Definition and Sections
And whoever swears to commit a sin, such as not speaking to his parents, he breaks his oath and expiates, and there is no expiation in the oath of an infidel, even if he breaks it as a Muslim.
[1] His statement: 'he breaks his oath' – this is a branch of the principle: Whoever is tested with two trials chooses the lesser of them. For in breaking there is loss of piety to a compensator, which is the expiation, and in piety there is commission of disobedience without compensator. So breaking in such a case becomes obligatory, because committing disobedience is forbidden. And if the sworn matter is better than its opposite, then breaking becomes recommended. The foundation of all this is the hadith: 'Let him expiate for his oath and do that which is better' (2), as mentioned. For if the sworn matter is better than other, e.g., saying: 'By Allah, I will pray duha today' or 'By Allah, I will not eat onions', then piety is better, rather obligatory, and similarly if the sworn matter and other are equal. This is what Ibn al-Humam verified in Fath al-Qadir. [2] His statement: 'And there is no expiation in the oath of an infidel' – meaning that if an infidel swears to do or not do something, then (3)
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi school regarding oaths. The main text (matn) states two rules: (1) If a person swears to commit a sin, such as not speaking to his parents (which is a sin of cutting family ties), then he must break his oath and expiate (pay kaffarah). (2) If an infidel swears an oath, there is no expiation upon him even if he later breaks it after becoming Muslim. The commentary explains the first rule in light of the legal principle: when faced with two evils, choose the lesser. Breaking the oath (which requires expiation) is lesser than committing the sin. The hadith 'Let him expiate and do that which is better' is the proof. The commentary also distinguishes cases where the sworn act is better (e.g., praying duha) or equal to its opposite, where keeping the oath is recommended or obligatory. The second ruling is simply explained: an infidel is not obligated by the Shari'ah regarding oaths, so no expiation is due.

Key Terms
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking an oath by doing the opposite of what one swore to do.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): The expiatory act required for breaking a valid oath, such as feeding ten poor persons or fasting three days.
البرّ (al-birr): Keeping the oath by doing what was sworn to do (piety).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He must break the oath and pay expiation.
C) He must break the oath without expiation.
D) The oath is invalid and he should ignore it.

Key Takeaway
An oath to commit a sin must be broken and expiated; an infidel's oath carries no expiation.

Distinguishing Wujub from Wujub al-Ada in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines wujub and ada with causal and capability conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between wujub (obligation of a state) and wujub al-ada (obligation to perform) and identify scenarios where wujub exists without wujub al-ada.

Core Idea
An obligation may be present without the binding duty to perform it immediately.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines two key concepts: wujub, the binding nature of a state like fasting, and ada, the actualization of that state. The author explains how a cause (sabab) triggers wujub, but wujub al-ada is a separate, subsidiary obligation. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping Hanafi rulings on travel, illness, and deferred duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two obligations
Locate the two types of wujub mentioned: one attached to the state itself (e.g., the state of prayer) and one attached to the performance (ada).
Passage says: 'al-wujub al-awwal yata'allaqu bi al-hay'a... wa al-thani bi ada'iha.'
2
Understand the causal trigger
The cause (sabab) is a motive that necessitates the state. Ask: why does the cause create wujub? Because it is suitable (munasaba) for the state.
The cause is not merely a legal trigger but a 'da'i' (motive).
3
Apply to the traveler example
For a traveler or sick person, the wujub of fasting exists, but wujub al-ada is absent. Why? Because the cause is present and the person is capable, but actual performance is not obligatory due to concession.
The passage uses 'marid' and 'musafir' as examples.
4
Analyze the sale example
In a sale with unspecified price, the wujub of exchange exists immediately, but wujub al-ada (payment) is subsidiary. How does this illustrate the hierarchy?
The wujub of the state (ownership transfer) is primary; paying the price is secondary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ الأداء : هو إيقاع تلك الهيئة، فوجوب الأداء هو لزوم إيقاع تلك الهيئة، وذلك مبني على الأوّل؛ لأنَّ السبب أوجب وجود تلك الهيئة لمناسبة بينهما، فإنّ المراد بالسبب الداعي، ثمَّ بواسطة هذا الوجوب يجب إيقاعُ تلك الهيئة، فالوجوب الأوّل يتعلق بالهيئة وهي الصلاة، والثاني بأدائها. وقد يوجد الوجوب بدون وجوب الأداء، كما في المريض والمسافر، فإنّ لزوم وجود الحالة التي هي الصوم حاصل؛ لأنَّ ذلك اللزوم باعتبار أن السبب داع إليه، والمحل وهو المكلف. صالح لهذا، فلو لم يحصل اللزوم لم يكن السبب سبباً، لكن لا يجب إيقاعه، مع أنه يجوز أن يكون واقعاً. وإذا وجد البيع بثمن غير معيّن فالبيع مبادلة المال بالمال، وقد ملك المشتري المبيع، فلابد أن يملك البائع مالاً على المشتري تحقيقاً للمبادلة، فهذا نفس الوجوب، ثمَّ لزوم أداء المال الواجب فرع على الأوّل، فهو وجوب الأداء. انتهى
Then performance: it is the actualization of that state. So the obligation of performance is the necessity of actualizing that state, and that is based on the first [obligation]; because the cause necessitates the existence of that state due to the suitability between them. For what is meant by the cause is the motive. Then, by virtue of this obligation, the actualization of that state becomes obligatory. So the first obligation pertains to the state, which is prayer, and the second pertains to its performance. Obligation may exist without the obligation of performance, as in the case of the sick person and the traveler. For the necessity of the existence of the state, which is fasting, is established; because that necessity is on account that the cause is a motive toward it, and the subject—the legally responsible person—is capable of it. So if the necessity were not established, the cause would not be a cause. But its actualization is not obligatory, although it may occur. And when a sale occurs with an unspecified price, sale is the exchange of property for property. The buyer has ownership of the sold item, so the seller must own money from the buyer to achieve the exchange. This is the essence of obligation. Then the necessity of paying the obligatory money is subsidiary to the first; so it is the obligation of performance. End.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of two key Hanafi legal concepts: wujub (obligation) and ada (performance). The author distinguishes between the obligation that attaches to the state itself (e.g., the state of being in prayer) and the obligation to perform that state. The first is caused by the motive (sabab), while the second is the binding nature of actualizing it. An example is given: for a sick person or traveler, the obligation of fasting exists (wujub of the state) because the cause (like time of Ramadan) is present and the person is capable, but the obligation to actually perform it (wujub al-ada) is not required—they may postpone it. This distinction is unique to Hanafi jurisprudence. Another example: in a sale with unspecified price, the obligation of exchange (wujub) exists immediately, but the obligation to pay the price (wujub al-ada) is subsidiary. The passage is quoted from 'al-Talwih' and ends with 'intaha'.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The binding nature of a state (like prayer or fasting) based on the cause and capability.
الأداء (al-ada): The actualization or performance of that obligatory state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does a traveler have the obligation (wujub) of fasting but not the obligation to perform (wujub al-ada)?
A) Because the cause of fasting is not present for a traveler.
B) Because the traveler is not qualified for the obligation.
C) Because the necessity of the state exists but the binding to actualize it is not required.
D) Because the traveler’s fasting is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Wujub (obligation of a state) can exist without wujub al-ada (obligation to perform), as seen in the traveler’s fasting obligation.

Swearing to Sin: Breaking the Oath is Wajib
Ruling on oath to sin with lesser-evil principle and hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing to commit a sin and apply the principle of choosing the lesser evil to determine whether to break the oath.

Core Idea
Breaking a sinful oath is wajib because committing a sin is a greater evil than breaking an oath with expiation.

Swearing to do something sinful puts you in a dilemma: fulfill the oath and sin, or break the oath and pay expiation. The Hanafi school resolves this with a clear legal maxim: choose the lesser evil. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its proof from the Prophet's hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize when an oath is sworn to commit a sin (e.g., 'By Allah, I will drink alcohol').
The oath itself may be valid, but its object is sinful.
2
Apply the lesser-evil principle
Compare two evils: breaking the oath (requires expiation) vs. committing the sin (no remedy). The sin is the greater evil.
The Hanafi maxim: 'Whoever is afflicted with two afflictions chooses the lesser.'
3
Derive the ruling
Since the sin is worse, breaking the oath becomes wajib to avoid it, and expiation must be paid.
The ruling is based on the hadith: 'Let him expiate his oath and do what is better.'
4
Distinguish when fulfilling is better
If the sworn act is virtuous (e.g., praying Duha), fulfilling is better, even wajib if it's a specific act of worship.
If the sworn act and alternative are equal, fulfilling is better but not compulsory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: حنث؛ هذا من فروع قاعدة: من ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونهما، فإنّ في الحنث تفويت البرّ إلى جابر، وهو الكفّارة، وفي البرّارتكاب للمعصية بلا جابر، والحنث في مثل هذه الصورة يصير واجباً؛ لأنّ ارتكاب المعصية حرام، ولو كان غير المحلوف عليه أولى يكون الحنث مستحباً. والأصل في هذا كله . حديث: «فليكفّر عن يمينه، وليأت بالذي هو خير»، كما مر ذكره، فإن كان المحلوفُ عليه أولى من غيره، كقوله: والله لأصلين الضحى اليوم، وقوله: والله لا آكــل البصل، كان البر أولى، بل واجباً، وكذا إذا كان المحلوف عليه وغيره متساويين. كذا حققه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير
His statement: 'he broke his oath'; this is from the branches of the principle: 'Whoever is afflicted with two afflictions chooses the lesser of them.' For in breaking the oath there is forfeiting the good [of fulfilling the oath] but with a compensator, which is the expiation, while in fulfilling there is committing a sin without a compensator. So in such a situation, breaking the oath becomes obligatory, because committing a sin is forbidden. But if the sworn matter is more virtuous than other acts, breaking the oath is recommended. The basis for all this is the hadith: 'Let him expiate his oath and do what is better,' as previously mentioned. So if the sworn matter is more virtuous than other acts, like saying 'By Allah, I will pray al-Duha today,' or 'By Allah, I will not eat onions,' then fulfilling is better, indeed obligatory. Likewise, if the sworn matter and its alternative are equal, as verified by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the word 'hantha' (he broke his oath) from the matn. It applies the Hanafi legal maxim: 'When faced with two evils, choose the lesser.' Breaking the oath (which requires expiation) is a lesser evil than committing a sin (which has no remedy). Therefore, in the case of swearing to do a sin, breaking the oath becomes wajib (obligatory). The proof is the hadith from Bukhari: 'Let him expiate his oath and then do what is better.' The commentary then distinguishes cases: if the sworn act is better (e.g., praying Duha), fulfilling it is better and even wajib (if it is a specific act of worship). If the sworn act and its alternative are equal, fulfilling is praiseworthy. This is based on Ibn al-Humam's verification in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
حنث (hantha): Breaking an oath by not doing what was sworn or doing what was sworn not to do.
البرّ (al-birr): Fulfilling the oath by doing what was sworn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on breaking an oath sworn to commit a sin?
A) It is haram because it breaks the oath.
B) It is wajib to break the oath and pay expiation.
C) It is mustahab to fulfill the oath.
D) It is makruh to break the oath.

Key Takeaway
When swearing to commit a sin, breaking the oath is wajib and expiation must be paid; this is derived from the principle of choosing the lesser evil and the hadith commanding expiation and doing what is better.

No Expiation for a Disbeliever's Oath
Ruling that disbeliever's oath carries no expiation with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a disbeliever's oath does not incur expiation, explain the two reasons (worship and lack of qualification for oath), and identify the Quranic proof from Surah al-Tawbah 9:12.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's oath is invalid and expiation is worship—so no expiation is due.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational Hanafi ruling: a disbeliever's oath carries no expiation, whether broken before or after embracing Islam. The commentator provides two rationales and a Quranic verse to support this. We will walk through the reasoning, address a common objection, and see its practical application in court and in vows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the clear ruling: no expiation (kafarah) is due for a disbeliever's oath, regardless of when the oath is broken (while disbelieving or after Islam). This is the explicit position of the matn and commentary.
2
Understand the Two Reasons
The commentator gives two reasons: (1) expiation is an act of worship ('ibadah), and a disbeliever lacks the legal capacity (ahliyyah) for worship; (2) a valid oath is made to glorify Allah, which contradicts disbelief. Both reasons show the oath is invalid from the start.
3
Locate the Quranic Dalil
The proof is from Surah al-Tawbah (9:12): '…they have no oaths.' The phrase 'la aymana lahum' indicates that disbelievers' oaths are not legally recognized.
The verse is about treaty-breaking, but the phrase is interpreted broadly.
4
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The verse denies legal validity to the oaths of disbelievers. Since expiation is only due upon breaking a valid oath, no expiation can arise. The dalala is zanni because it is an inference from a general expression, but it is strong.
Hanafi usul: general expressions (amm) are binding until specified.
5
Address the Objection
An objector might argue: the cause of expiation is breaking the oath, not the oath itself. So if a disbeliever breaks the oath after becoming Muslim, expiation should be due. The response: breaking must follow a valid oath; a disbeliever's oath is never valid, so no expiation is triggered.
The cause is 'breaking a valid oath', not just any breaking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولا كفارة في حلف الكافر؛ يعني إذا حلف كافر على فعل أو ترك، ثمّ حنث في حالة كفره، أو أسلم وحنث في إسلامه لا تجب الكفارة عليه. والوجه في ذلك: أنَّ الكفّارة عبادة من وجه؛ ولذا تؤدى بالصوم، والكافر ليس بأهل للعبادة، وأيضاً هو ليس بأهل لليمين؛ لأنها تعقد لتعظيم الله، كما هو أهله، وكفره ينافيه، ويشهد له قوله في سورة براءة: (وَإِن تَكَنُوا أَيْمَنَهُم مِّنْ بَعْدِ عَهْدِهِمْ وَطَعَنُوا فِي دِينِكُمْ فَقَتِلُوا أَيْمَةَ الْكُفْرِ إِنَّهُمْ لَا أَيْمَنَ لَهُمْ). فقوله: لا أَيْمَنَ لَهُمْ يدلّ على عدم اعتبار أيمان الكفّار شرعاً، ووجوب الكفارة موقوف على اعتبارها، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال: إنّ سبب وجوب الكفارة عندنا هو الحنث لا اليمين، فينبغي أن تجب الكفّارة حال إسلامه لوجود السبب في حال كونه صالحاً لأداء الكفّارة، وإن لم تجب عند كون الحنث في حال كفره. وجه الدفع: إنَّ سبب الوجوب إنّما هو الحنتُ بعد اليمين المعتبرة شرعاً، ويمين الكافر غير معتبرة، فلا ينعقد الحنث بعدها سبباً لوجوب الكفارة، وإن وجد حالة إسلامه. فإن قلت: لو كان كذلك لزم أن لا يستحلف الكافر المدعى عليه عند القاضي، ولم يعتبر نكوله. قلت: المقصود من تحليف المدعى عليه، هو ظهور وجـه الـدعـوى بحلفـه أو بنكوله، والكافر وإن لم يثبت في حقه شرعاً اليمين المستعقب لحكمه، لكنه في نفسه يعتقد تعظيم اسم الله حلال وحرمة اليمين به كاذباً، فيمتنع عـ عنه، فيحصل المقصود بإلزامه ما ادعاه المدعي. ومن هاهنا يظهر أنه لو نذر الكافر قربة ثمّ أسلم لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأنه ليس من أهل النذر، كما أنه ليس من أهل اليمين لا يقال: قد ثبت في صحیح البخاري وغيره: إنّ عمر كان نذر حالة كفره باعتكاف يوم أو ليلة في المسجد الحرام، فسأل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال له: أوف بنذرك، لأنا نقول: ذلك لا يدل على الوجوب، بل على
His statement: 'And no expiation for the oath of a disbeliever'; meaning, if a disbeliever swears to do or not do something, then breaks his oath while still a disbeliever, or becomes Muslim and then breaks it, expiation is not obligatory upon him. The reason for this is that expiation is an act of worship in one respect; therefore it is performed by fasting, and the disbeliever is not qualified for worship. Also, he is not qualified for an oath because an oath is made to glorify Allah, as is His due, and his disbelief contradicts that. This is attested by Allah's statement in Surah Bara'ah: 'If they break their oaths after their covenant and attack your religion, then fight the leaders of disbelief—indeed, they have no oaths.' So His statement 'they have no oaths' indicates that the oaths of disbelievers are not considered legally, and the obligation of expiation is contingent upon their legal consideration. Thus, the objection that 'the cause of expiation in our school is breaking the oath, not the oath itself, so expiation should be due upon embracing Islam because the cause exists when he is capable of performing expiation, even if it was not due when he broke it as a disbeliever' is refuted. The refutation: The cause of obligation is breaking the oath after a legally valid oath, and the oath of a disbeliever is not valid, so breaking it does not become a cause for expiation, even if it occurs after embracing Islam. If you say: If so, then a disbeliever defendant should not be sworn by the judge, and his refusal to take an oath should not be considered. I say: The purpose of swearing the defendant is to reveal the truth of the claim by his oath or refusal. The disbeliever, even though the oath that entails a legal effect is not proven for him legally, still in himself considers glorifying Allah's name to be lawful and swearing falsely by it to be forbidden, so he refrains from it, and thus the purpose is achieved by obliging him to what the plaintiff claims. From this, it appears that if a disbeliever vows a supererogatory act and then becomes Muslim, nothing is incumbent upon him, because he is not qualified for vows just as he is not for oaths. It cannot be said: It is established in Sahih al-Bukhari and others that 'Umar had vowed during his disbelief to perform i'tikaf for a day or night in the Sacred Mosque, and he asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: Fulfill your vow. Because we say: That does not indicate obligation, but rather...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn's ruling that there is no expiation for a disbeliever's oath. The commentator provides two main reasons: (1) expiation is an act of worship, and a disbeliever is not qualified (ahl) for worship; (2) a disbeliever's oath is not legally valid because it is based on glorifying Allah, which contradicts his disbelief. The Quranic proof is from Surah al-Tawbah (9:12): 'they have no oaths.' The commentator then addresses an objection: since the cause of expiation is the breaking of the oath, not the oath itself, expiation should be due if the person breaks it after becoming Muslim. The response is that the breaking must be of a valid oath; the disbeliever's oath is invalid, so no expiation arises. Another objection concerns swearing a disbeliever as a defendant in court; the response is that the purpose is to elicit truth, and the disbeliever's own reverence for the oath suffices. Finally, the commentator addresses the case of vows (nadhr) by a disbeliever, citing the hadith of 'Umar, and explains that the Prophet's command to fulfill the vow was a recommendation, not an obligation, because the disbeliever was not legally bound.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kafarah): Expiation required for breaking a valid oath; here ruled not due for disbelievers.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath taken in the name of Allah; disbeliever's oath is not valid legally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a disbeliever's oath not considered valid for the purpose of expiation in the Hanafi school?
A) Because a disbeliever cannot speak truthfully.
B) Because expiation is an act of worship and a disbeliever is not qualified, and the oath is based on glorifying Allah, which his disbelief contradicts.
C) Because a disbeliever's oath is always coerced.
D) Because the Quran does not mention expiation for disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
A disbeliever's oath is not legally recognized, so no expiation is due for breaking it, even after conversion.

Ruling on the Oath of a Disbeliever
Explaining why kaffarah is not due on a disbeliever's broken oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the oath of a disbeliever and explain why kaffarah is not required, citing the Quranic proof and refuting the counterargument.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's oath is invalid, so no kaffarah is due even if broken after Islam.

This lesson examines the legal status of oaths made by disbelievers in Hanafi fiqh. The central question: if a person swears an oath while a disbeliever and later breaks it after embracing Islam, is expiation (kaffarah) required? The Hanafi school rules that it is not, based on a clear Quranic verse and a nuanced understanding of the cause of kaffarah. We will walk through the reasoning, evidence, and responses to objections.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and initial reasoning
Identify the Hanafi position: no kaffarah is due on a disbeliever's broken oath, whether broken before or after Islam. Note the twofold reasoning: kaffarah is worship (disbeliever unfit) and oath requires glorification of Allah (disbeliever contradicts).
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Examine Surah Bara'ah 9:12, specifically the phrase 'la aymana lahum'. Understand how this is interpreted as a declaration that disbelievers' oaths are not legally considered (ghayr mu'tabara).
The dalala is zanni despite qati thubut; the interpretation yields the ruling.
3
Refute the counterargument about the cause
Consider the objection that the cause of kaffarah is the breach (hanth) alone. Explain why the Hanafi school insists the cause is 'breach after a valid oath' – the oath itself must be valid. Since the disbeliever's oath is invalid, the breach does not create obligation.
This addresses the scenario where breach occurs after Islam.
4
Address the practical judicial exception
Respond to the objection: if oaths of disbelievers are invalid, why do judges administer oaths to disbeliever defendants? Clarify that the purpose is to reveal the truth, not to create a legally binding oath; the disbeliever's own reverence for God's name achieves the goal.
This shows that practical utility does not imply legal validity.
5
Extend the ruling to vows (nadhr) and handle the hadith of 'Umar
Note that the same principle applies to vows: a disbeliever's vow is not binding. Then discuss the hadith where the Prophet told 'Umar to fulfill his pre-Islamic vow – explain that Hanafi scholars interpret this as recommendation (istihbab), not obligation.
This distinguishes between legal obligation and moral encouragement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حنث في حالة كفره، أو أسلم وحنث في إسلامه لا تجب الكفارة عليه. والوجه في ذلك : أنَّ الكفّارة عبادة من وجه ؛ ولذا تؤدى بالصوم، والكافر ليس بأهل للعبادة، وأيضاً هو ليس بأهل لليمين ؛ لأنها تعقد لتعظيم الله ، كما هو أهله، وكفره ينافيه، ويشهد له قوله في سورة براءة : ( وَإِن تَكَنُوا أَيْمَنَهُم مِّنْ بَعْدِ عَهْدِهِمْ وَطَعَنُوا فِي دِينِكُمْ فَقَتِلُوا أَيْمَةَ الْكُفْرِ إِنَّهُمْ لَا أَيْمَنَ لَهُمْ ) . فقوله : لا أَيْمَنَ لَهُمْ ) يدلّ على عدم اعتبار أيمان الكفّار شرعاً ، ووجوب الكفارة موقوف على اعتبارها، وبهذا يندفع ما يقال : إنّ سبب وجوب الكفارة عندنا هو الحنث لا اليمين، فينبغي أن تجب الكفّارة حال إسلامه لوجود السبب في حال كونه صالحاً لأداء الكفّارة، وإن لم تجب عند كون الحنث في حال كفره. وجه الدفع : إنَّ سبب الوجوب إنّما هو الحنتُ بعد اليمين المعتبرة شرعاً، ويمين الكافر غير معتبرة، فلا ينعقد الحنث بعدها سبباً لوجوب الكفارة، وإن وجد حالة إسلامه. فإن قلت : لو كان كذلك لزم أن لا يستحلف الكافر المدعى عليه عند القاضي، ولم يعتبر نكوله. قلت : المقصود من تحليف المدعى عليه، هو ظهور وجـه الـدعـوى بحلفـه أو بنكوله، والكافر وإن لم يثبت في حقه شرعاً اليمين المستعقب لحكمه، لكنه في نفسه يعتقد تعظيم اسم الله حلال وحرمة اليمين به كاذباً ، فيمتنع عنه ، فيحصل المقصود بإلزامه ما ادعاه المدعي. ومن هاهنا يظهر أنه لو نذر الكافر قربة ثمّ أسلم لا يلزمه شيء ؛ لأنه ليس من أهل النذر، كما أنه ليس من أهل اليمين لا يقال : قد ثبت في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره : «إنّ عمر ه كان نذر حالة كفره باعتكاف يوم أو ليلة في المسجد الحرام، فسأل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال له : أوف بنذرك»(۳)، لأنا نقول : ذلك لا يدل على الوجوب، بل على الاستحباب، ولا كلام فيه كذا حققه في «الفتح»(۳) و«البناية»(4) وغيرهما.
If he breaks the oath while a disbeliever, or he accepts Islam and breaks it in his Islam, expiation is not obligatory upon him. The reasoning for that is: expiation is an act of worship in one respect; therefore it is performed by fasting, and a disbeliever is not qualified for worship. Also, he is not qualified for the oath because it is made for the glorification of Allah, as He is worthy of it, and his disbelief contradicts that. This is supported by His saying in Surah Bara'ah: 'And if they break their oaths after their covenant and attack your religion, then fight the leaders of disbelief – indeed, there are no oaths for them.' So His saying 'there are no oaths for them' indicates the non-consideration of the oaths of disbelievers in Islamic law, and the obligation of expiation depends on their consideration. And with this is refuted what is said: The cause of the obligation of expiation in our school is the breach, not the oath, so expiation should be obligatory upon his Islam because the cause exists when he is qualified to perform expiation, even if it was not obligatory when the breach occurred in his disbelief. The point of refutation: The cause of obligation is only the breach after an oath considered valid in Islamic law, and the oath of a disbeliever is not considered, so the breach after it does not become a cause for the obligation of expiation, even if it occurs in his state of Islam. If you say: If that is the case, then it would necessitate that the disbeliever defendant should not be made to swear before the judge, and his refusal to swear should not be considered. I say: The purpose of making the defendant swear is to reveal the face of the claim by his swearing or his refusal, and even though the oath that entails its ruling is not established in Islamic law for a disbeliever, he himself believes in the glorification of Allah's name and the sanctity of swearing falsely by Him, so he refrains, and the purpose is achieved by imposing upon him what the plaintiff claims. From this it becomes clear that if a disbeliever vows an act of devotion then accepts Islam, nothing is binding on him; because he is not qualified for vows, just as he is not qualified for oaths. It cannot be said: It is established in Sahih al-Bukhari and others that 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) vowed during his disbelief to observe i'tikaf for a day or night in the Sacred Mosque, and he asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he said to him: Fulfill your vow. We say: That does not indicate obligation, but rather recommendation, and there is no dispute about that. Thus it is verified in al-Fath and al-Binaya and others.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on oaths made by disbelievers. The author states that if a person makes an oath while a disbeliever and then breaks it, or breaks it after embracing Islam, no expiation (kaffarah) is required. The reasoning is twofold: first, kaffarah is a form of worship, and a disbeliever is not qualified for worship; second, an oath is an act of glorifying Allah, which a disbeliever cannot properly perform. The Quranic verse from Surah Bara'ah (9:12) is cited: 'there are no oaths for them,' indicating that the oaths of disbelievers are not legally valid. The author then refutes a potential objection: even though the cause of kaffarah is the breaking of the oath, the oath itself must be valid. Since the disbeliever's oath is invalid, no kaffarah is due even if the breach occurs after Islam. Another objection is raised: if so, why do judges administer oaths to disbeliever defendants? The answer is that the purpose is practical: the disbeliever still fears false oaths due to their own belief in God's sanctity, so it serves the judicial process. Similarly, if a disbeliever makes a vow (nadhr) and later embraces Islam, the vow is not binding. The hadith of 'Umar vowing during Jahiliyyah is explained as recommending fulfillment, not obligating it, as confirmed by later scholars.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): A binding oath invoking Allah's name to confirm or deny something.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath, typically fasting, feeding, or clothing.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do or vice versa.
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship upon fulfillment of a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that a disbeliever's broken oath does not require expiation even if the breach occurs after Islam?
A) Because expiation is only for sins, and disbelief is not a sin.
B) Because the cause of expiation is the breach after a valid oath, and a disbeliever's oath is not legally valid.
C) Because the Quran says 'fight the leaders of disbelief' which implies oaths are irrelevant.
D) Because expiation is only required if the oath was sworn in the name of Allah, which a disbeliever cannot do.

Key Takeaway
A disbeliever's oath is legally invalid; thus, even if broken after embracing Islam, no kaffarah is required, because the obligation of kaffarah depends on a valid oath.

Declaring Property Unlawful: Oath or Prohibition?
Defining 'man harrama milkahu' and its legal consequence as a yamin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on declaring one's property unlawful and distinguish between immediate and suspended declarations with their respective consequences.

Core Idea
Declaring your property haram is an oath, not a legal prohibition.

When a person says 'this is haram for me,' does it actually become unlawful? The Hanafi school clarifies that such a statement is treated as an oath (yamin), not as a change in the item's legal status. This ruling hinges on whether the declaration is immediate or suspended. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between personal vows and divine prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
Read the core statement: 'Whoever declares his property unlawful, it does not become unlawful, and if he takes it as lawful, he must expiate.' Identify the two legal effects: (1) no actual prohibition, (2) an oath requiring kaffarah upon use.
This rule applies only to one's own property.
2
Distinguish Immediate vs. Suspended Declarations
An immediate declaration (e.g., 'This food is haram for me') is binding as an oath. A suspended declaration on the very act (e.g., 'If I eat this, it is haram') does not create an oath because at the moment of the condition, the thing is no longer present to be prohibited.
If the suspension is on a different action (e.g., 'If I speak to Zayd, this food is haram'), it is treated as immediate.
3
Apply to Examples
Consider a wife saying to her husband, 'You are haram for me.' This is an immediate declaration; if they later have intercourse, she must expiate. Contrast with 'If I eat this apple, it is haram' – no expiation needed if she eats it.
If the property is already haram (e.g., pork), the declaration is still an oath but the item remains prohibited by Shari'ah.
4
Understand the Consequence: Kaffarah
Violating the oath requires expiation (kaffarah) similar to a broken oath. The text does not specify the amount, but standard Hanafi rulings prescribe feeding ten poor people or fasting three days.
The declaration does not affect the legal status of the object itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه
ومَن حَرَّمَ ملكه لا يحرم وإن استباحه كفر
[1]- @
ومن حرم ملكه " لا يحرم وإن استباحه كفر (۱)
[١] قوله : ومن حرم؛ ماض من التحريم والمراد به تنجيزه، بأن يقول: هذا الشيء علي حرام، ومنه قولها لزوجها : أنت علي حرام ، أو حرمتك على نفسي، فلو طاوعته في الجماع أو أكرهها كفرت كذا في «المجتبى».
ولو علق الحرمة على فعل المحلوف عليه، نحو أن يقول : إن أكلت هذا الطعام فهو علي حرام، فإنه لا تلزمه الكفّارة ؛ وذلك لأنَّ في المنجز حرم على نفسه طعاماً موجوداً، أما في المعلّق فإنّه ما حرَّمه إلا بعد الأكل ؛ لأنَّ الجزاء إنما ينزلُ عقيب الشرط، وحينئذ لم يكن الطعام موجوداً. كذا في «الخلاصة»، وغيرها، وهذا بخلاف ما لو علق حرمته على غير فعل المحلوف عليه، نحو: إن كلّمت زيداً فهذا الطعام علي حرام، فإنّه كالمنجز. [٢] قوله : ملكه ؛ قيّد به ليترتب عليه قوله : لا يحرم يعني لا يحرم ذلك الشيء، لكنّه يكون يميناً ، فتجب الكفّارة باستباحته ومعنى قوله : لا يحرم ؛ أي بتحريمه هذا، فإن كان ذلك الشيء حراماً فهو حرام عليه كما كان، فإن قال : مال فلان علي حرام يكون يميناً أيضاً ، إلا أن يريد به الإخبار. كذا في «الخانية».
(1) أي من حرم على نفسه شيئاً مما يملكه لم يصرّ حراماً عليه، وإن استباحه بمعاملته معاملة المباح كفر. ينظر : درر الحكام (٢ : ٤٢). (۲) قال الطحاوي في شرح معاني الآثار» (۳ : ۱۳۳): «فيجوز أن يكون قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ليس من طريق أن ذلك كان واجباً عليه، ولكن أنه قد كان سمح في حال ما نذره أن يفعله فهو في معصية الله له فأمره النبي الله أن يفعله الآن على أنه طاعة الله عز وجل، فكـان مـا أمـر بـه خلاف ما إذا كان أوجبه هو على نفسه. وقال أبو الحسن القابسي : لم يأمره الشارع على جهة الإيجاب وإنما على جهة المشورة والاستحباب»، كما في تكملة فتح الملهم»(۲ : ۲۱۹). وقال البدر العَيْني في عمدة القاري (۲۳) : (۲۰۹) : أراد أن يعلمهم أن الوفاء بالنذر من أكد الأمور فغلظ أمره بأن أمر عمر ه بالوفاء». (۳) فتح القدير» (٥ : ٨٧). (٤) (البناية (٥) : ۱۹۱).
The Book of Oaths: Its Definition and Categories. And whoever declares his property unlawful, it does not become unlawful, and if he takes it as lawful, he must expiate. [1] The author says: 'Whoever declares unlawful' – the past tense of tahrim, and what is meant is immediate (munajjaz), such as saying: 'This thing is unlawful for me.' Included in this is a woman's statement to her husband: 'You are unlawful for me,' or 'I have made you unlawful for myself.' So if she obeys him in intercourse or he forces her, she must expiate; thus in al-Mujtaba. And if he suspends the unlawfulness on the action of the thing sworn about, such as saying: 'If I eat this food, it is unlawful for me,' then expiation is not binding on him. That is because in the immediate case, he has declared unlawful a food that exists, while in the suspended case, he only declares it unlawful after eating, because the consequence only occurs after the condition, and at that point the food does not exist. Thus in al-Khulasa and others. This is unlike if he suspends its unlawfulness on an action other than the thing sworn about, e.g., 'If I speak to Zayd, this food is unlawful for me,' then it is like the immediate. [2] The author says: 'his property' – he restricted it so that his saying 'it does not become unlawful' applies, meaning that thing does not become unlawful, but it becomes an oath, so expiation becomes obligatory upon taking it as lawful. And the meaning of his saying 'it does not become unlawful' is: by this declaration of unlawfulness. If that thing is already unlawful (haram), then it remains unlawful for him as it was. If he says 'So-and-so's property is unlawful for me,' that also becomes an oath, unless he intends to inform. Thus in al-Khaniyah. (1) That is, whoever declares unlawful for himself something that he owns, it does not become unlawful for him, and if he takes it as lawful by treating it as permissible, he must expiate. See Durar al-Hukkam (2:42). (2) al-Tahawi said in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar (3:133): 'It is possible that the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was not by way of obligation upon him, but rather he had permitted in the state when he vowed to do it, and he was in disobedience to Allah, so the Prophet ordered him to do it now as an act of obedience to Allah. So what he ordered was contrary to if he had obligated it upon himself.' And Abu al-Hasan al-Qabisi said: 'The Lawgiver did not command him by way of obligation but by way of consultation and recommendation.' As in Takmila Fath al-Mulhim (2:219). And Badr al-Ayni said in 'Umdat al-Qari (23:209): 'He wanted to teach them that fulfilling vows is among the most emphasized matters, so he made the matter severe by commanding 'Umar to fulfill it.' (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:87). (4) al-Binaya (5:191).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the rule: 'Whoever declares his property unlawful, it does not become unlawful, but if he takes it as lawful, he must expiate.' The ruling means that such a statement is treated as an oath (yamin), not an actual legal prohibition. The text distinguishes between immediate (munajjaz) and suspended (mu'allaq) declarations. An immediate declaration (e.g., 'This food is haram for me') creates an oath requiring expiation upon breach. If the declaration is suspended on the very act (e.g., 'If I eat this, it is haram'), then because at the moment of condition, the food no longer exists as owned? Actually, the reasoning is that the prohibition only comes after the act, so the food is not present to be declared unlawful at the start; hence no expiation for breaching? Wait, the text says if he says 'If I eat this food, it is haram for me,' then no kaffarah is due. The reason: in the immediate case, he forbids an existing thing; in the suspended, he only forbids after the condition, and at that point the food is no longer present? The explanation is that the consequence (being haram) only occurs after the condition is met, and at that moment the food is not present (since he already ate it). So effectively, no prohibition is established. However, if the suspension is on a different action (e.g., 'If I speak to Zayd, this food is haram'), it is treated as immediate because the food exists at the time of declaration. The word 'property' is qualified to show that the thing does not actually become haram; it only becomes an oath. If the declared thing is already haram by Shari'ah (like pork), it remains haram, but the statement may still be an oath. The footnotes further discuss the hadith of 'Umar's vow, reaffirming that it indicates recommendation, not obligation.

Key Terms
حرَّم يحرم (harrama yuharrimu): To declare something unlawful upon oneself; in this context, it becomes an oath, not a legal prohibition.
ملك (milk): Property owned by the person; the ruling applies only to one's own property.
منجّز (munajjaz): An immediate declaration of unlawfulness, e.g., 'this is haram for me' without condition.
معلّق (mu'allaq): A suspended declaration, e.g., 'if I do X, then it is haram.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'If I eat this apple, it is haram for me.' According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) The apple becomes haram for him, and he must avoid it.
B) It is an oath; if he eats it, kaffarah is due.
C) It is not an oath, and no kaffarah is due if he eats it.
D) He must divorce his wife.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, declaring your own property haram is an oath, not a prohibition; expiation is due only for immediate declarations or those suspended on a different act.

Declaring Property Haram as an Oath: Immediate vs. Conditional
Ruling on tahrim as oath, with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when someone declares his property as haram, distinguish between immediate and conditional declarations, and apply the rule to determine when expiation (kaffarah) is due.

Core Idea
Declaring halal as haram creates an oath, not actual prohibition; kaffarah is due only for immediate declarations or conditions unrelated to the act.

This lesson examines a common scenario: a person declares something he owns as 'haram' for himself. Is it actually forbidden? Or is it an oath? The Hanafi school treats it as an oath. We explore the difference between immediate (tanjiz) and conditional (ta'liq) declarations, and when expiation (kaffarah) is required. The Quranic rebuke to the Prophet (Surah al-Tahrim:1) provides the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
When someone says 'this is haram for me' about something he owns, the first question is: does it become actually haram? The text states clearly: 'no, it does not become haram.' Instead, it is treated as an oath (yamin).
The object's original halal status remains unchanged.
2
Classify the declaration: immediate or conditional
Determine whether the tahrim is unconditional (munajjaz) or conditional (mu'allaq). Immediate: 'this food is haram for me.' Conditional: 'if I eat this food, it is haram for me.' The type decides the obligation of kaffarah.
Also distinguish between condition being the act itself (eating) or a separate act (speaking to Zayd).
3
Apply the ruling for immediate and conditional
If immediate, using the object intentionally requires kaffarah. If conditional on the act itself, no kaffarah because the object was not 'existing' at the time of the oath. If conditional on a separate act, treat as immediate.
The text gives the example: 'If I speak to Zayd, this food is haram for me' – like immediate.
4
Consider custom (urf) in interpretation
Persian phrases like 'halal barawi haram' and 'harjuhu badast rast kiram barawi haram' are interpreted by custom. Later Hanafi fatwa includes divorce in addition to food/drink, due to prevalent custom.
Early scholars hesitated; later scholars followed custom. The mufta bihi is the later view.
5
Recognize the Quranic proof
The verse 'O Prophet, why do you declare haram what Allah has made halal for you' (66:1) establishes that declaring halal as haram is an oath, not a change in divine law.
The verse rebukes the action, showing it is a sin that requires expiation.
