The Chapter of Oaths: Its Definition and Sections. And whoever declares his property as haram, it does not become haram, and if he treats it as permissible, he becomes a disbeliever. [1] And whoever declares his property as haram, it does not become haram, and if he treats it as permissible, he becomes a disbeliever (1) recommendation, and there is no discussion about it (2) as verified in al-Fath (3) and al-Binaya (4) and others. [1] His saying: 'whoever declares'; past tense of tahrim, and what is meant is immediate (munajjaz), such as saying: this thing is haram for me, and from it is a woman saying to her husband: you are haram for me, or I have declared you haram upon myself. If she complied with him in intercourse or he forced her, she becomes a disbeliever (kafir) as in al-Mujtaba. And if he suspended the tahrim on the action of the thing sworn about, such as saying: if I eat this food, it is haram for me, then no expiation is incumbent upon him; because in the immediate case, he declared haram a food that exists, but in the suspended case, he only declared it haram after eating; because the consequence only occurs after the condition, and at that time the food did not exist. As in al-Khulasa and others. This is contrary to if he suspends its tahrim on an action other than the thing sworn about, such as: if I speak to Zayd, then this food is haram for me, for it is like the immediate. [2] His saying: 'his property'; he restricted it so that his saying 'it does not become haram' follows, meaning that thing does not become haram, but it is an oath, so expiation is obligatory by treating it as permissible. And the meaning of his saying: 'it does not become haram' means by this tahrim of his, but if that thing is already haram, then it remains haram as before. If he says: the property of so-and-so is haram for me, it is also an oath, unless he intends to inform. As in al-Khaniyya. (1) That is, whoever declares haram upon himself something that he owns, it does not become haram upon him, and if he treats it as permissible by dealing with it as permissible, he becomes a disbeliever. And Badr al-Ayni said in Umdat al-Qari (23): (209): 'He wanted to teach them that fulfilling a vow is among the most emphasized matters, so he made his command severe by commanding Umar to fulfill it.' Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Oaths, defining its sections and a key ruling. The author states: 'Whoever declares his property as haram, it does not become haram, and if he treats it as permissible, he becomes a disbeliever.' The commentary expands this ruling. The first point clarifies that this 'tahrim' (declaring haram) is immediate (munajjaz), e.g., saying 'this thing is haram for me'. If a woman says to her husband 'you are haram for me', and then she voluntarily has intercourse with him, she becomes a disbeliever — meaning she is liable for kaffarah or has committed a grave sin. However, if she was coerced, she does not. Second, if the tahrim is conditional (mu'allaq), like 'if I eat this food, it is haram for me', then no kaffarah is required upon eating because the food was not existing at the time of the declaration; the prohibition only attaches after the condition is fulfilled. Exception: if the condition is not the act of eating but something else, e.g., 'if I speak to Zayd, this food is haram for me', it is like immediate tahrim, and kaffarah is due. The word 'his property' restricts the ruling: only property he owns is considered; if he declares someone else's property haram, it is still an oath. The phrase 'it does not become haram' means the object remains halal in itself; if it was already haram, it stays haram. Later, the passage discusses the expression 'every halal is haram for me' and similar Persian phrases. According to custom, such phrases are interpreted as oaths about food and drink, but the later scholars (mutakhireen) have given fatwa that they also include divorce (i.e., the wife becomes divorced). The earlier scholars differed. The proof for the entire ruling is the Quranic verse 'O Prophet, why do you declare haram what Allah has made halal for you' (Surah al-Tahrim:1), which shows that declaring halal as haram is a sin and constitutes an oath. Finally, the commentary defines 'treating it as permissible': if one uses the property in a customary manner (e.g., buying with dirhams he declared haram), he violates the oath; if he gives it away, he does not. The oath is effective regardless of the wording used.

Key Terms
تحريم (tahrim): Declaring something as forbidden (haram). In this context, it is often used as an oath, not actual forbiddance.
يمين (yamin): An oath; a declaration that invokes a consequence if violated. In this passage, declaring halal as haram is considered an oath.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for breaking an oath. In this context, if one treats the 'haram' thing as permissible, kaffarah is due.
تنجيز (tanjiz): Immediate; an unconditional declaration of tahrim. It has immediate effect as an oath.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension; making the tahrim conditional on an event. In conditional tahrim, no kaffarah is due if the condition is the act itself.
عرف (urf): Custom; prevalent practice. In this text, custom determines the meaning of Persian phrases like 'halal barawi haram'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says, 'If I eat this bread, it is haram for me,' and then eats the bread, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The bread becomes haram for him, and he must repent.
B) He is not liable for kaffarah because the tahrim was suspended on the act itself.
C) He must pay kaffarah because he broke his oath.
D) He becomes a disbeliever and must re-enter Islam.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats declaring halal as haram as an oath, not a change in status; kaffarah is due for immediate declarations or when the condition is separate from the sworn act.

intermediate
6 min
Ruling on Declaring Halal as Haram as an Oath
Elaboration of the rule that declaring halal haram is an oath requiring kaffarah, with examples and custom-based interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that declaring halal as haram is an oath in Hanafi fiqh, identify the role of custom in interpreting ambiguous phrases, and apply the ruling to examples.

Core Idea
Declaring halal haram is an oath requiring kaffarah — custom determines if it is divorce or an oath about food.

In this passage, the commentator explains a practical issue: when a person says something like 'halal upon me is haram,' what is the legal consequence? The Hanafi school treats such statements as oaths, but the exact effect depends on local custom. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof from the Quran, and the nuance introduced by custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Default Ruling
The act is the statement 'halal upon me is haram' (or similar phrases). The default ruling in the Hanafi school is that it is an oath (yamin) regarding food and drink, not divorce or a null statement.
This is the foundation for all further analysis.
2
Understand the Proof from the Quran and Sunnah
The Quranic verses (At-Tahrim 1-2) rebuke the Prophet for making honey haram and immediately mention the dissolution of oaths, showing that making halal haram is an oath. The story of the Prophet's wives and the honey provides context for this ruling.
The dalil is decisive (qati al-thubut) but the meaning (dalala) is zanni (interpreted).
3
Analyze the Role of Custom (Urf) in Interpretation
In some communities, phrases like 'halal upon me is haram' are customarily used to mean divorce. Later Hanafi scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) consider this custom (urf) to determine the intended meaning, so the ruling can shift from an oath to divorce.
Custom is a recognized source of interpretation in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Earlier scholars (mutaqaddimūn) did not rely on custom; they held such phrases always as oaths unless the speaker explicitly intended divorce. Imam Bazdawi hesitated to accept custom. The later scholars' view is the mufta bihi (operative fatwa).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Apply the Ruling to Different Scenarios
Apply the principles: if one says 'these dirhams are haram upon me' and then buys with them, he breaks the oath (requires kaffarah). If he gives them in charity, he does not break it. If custom points to divorce, the statement becomes talaq.
The nature of the act (using vs. disposing) matters.

كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه (1) 35 قد فرض الله لكم تحلة أيمانكم ) ، على أن اليمين إن كان على فعل وجودي عائشة. ففي الصحيحين وغيرهما عن رضي الله عنها : «أنه كان يمكث عند زينب ويشرب عسلاً ، فتواطأت أنا وحفصة أنا نقول له : أجد منك ريح مغافیر، فدخل على إحداهما فقالت له ذلك ، فقال : لا بل شربت عسلاً عند زينب، ولن أعود له، وقد حلفت لا تخبري بذلك أحداً (۳) ، وكان يبغي بذلك مرضاة أزواجه، فنزلت، والمغافير شبيه بالصمغ لها رائحة كريهة. تبغى مرضاة أزواجك والله غفور رحيم ﴾ (٣) ، فلم يؤاخذك بما صدر منك، وقد (۳) روي أنه أصاب أم إبراهيم في بيت حفصة رضي الله عنها فعلمت فقالت : أيّ رسول الله في بيتي وعلى فراشي، فحرمها على نفسه ، وقال : والله لا أطؤها ولا تذكري ذلك لأحد، فذكرته لعائشة رضي الله عنها) ، فعوتب في التحريم، وأمر بالكفّارة في اليمين، ذكره كثير من السلف. قدْ فرض - شرع - اللهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَنكُمْ ) () تحليلها بالكفارة، وهي ما ذكر في سورة المائدة». انتهى. And every 'halal upon me is haram' is regarding food and drink, and they said: it divorces his wife, and this is the fatwa. Like 'halal barawiy haram', and 'harjah badast rast kiram barawiy haram' according to custom. That is, if he treats it as permissible, he disbelieves; because making the halal haram is an oath, due to the saying of the Most High: [Quran 66:1]. [Footnote: He said: 'treats it as permissible' means he enjoys it in a customary manner. So if he said to dirhams in his hand: 'these are haram upon me', and he buys with them, he breaks his oath; if he gives them in charity or gifts, he does not break it. Thus in Al-Fath. He said: 'oath' whether the prohibition is with the word 'tahrim' or other. He said: 'due to the saying of the Most High'; Shaykh Mu'in al-Din ibn Safi al-Din said in his tafsir: 'O Prophet, why do you prohibit what Allah has made lawful for you?' of honey. The author of Al-Fath said: the gist is that the consideration in understanding these Arabic or Persian expressions as something without intention is custom. If there is no custom, he is asked about his intention. In what is understood without intention, if he says: 'I intended other,' the judge does not accept him, but between him and Allah he is truthful. End. ... The Book of Oaths / Definition and its Divisions. 'Allah has ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths.' meaning that the oath is concerning an existent action. In the two Sahihs and others from 'Aisha: 'He used to stay with Zaynab and drink honey. I and Hafsa conspired to say to him: 'I smell from you the odor of maghafir.' He said: 'No, I drank honey at Zaynab's, and I will not do it again, and I have sworn not to tell anyone.' And he intended thereby to please his wives. Then the verse was revealed. And maghafir is like gum with a bad smell. ... 'seeking the pleasure of your wives, and Allah is Forgiving, Merciful.' So He did not take you to task for what occurred. And it is narrated that he had relations with Umm Ibrahim in Hafsa's house, and she said: 'O Messenger of Allah, in my house and on my bed?' So he forbade her to himself and said: 'By Allah, I will not approach her, and do not mention this to anyone.' She mentioned it to 'Aisha, and he was rebuked for the prohibition and commanded expiation for the oath. Many of the Salaf mentioned it. 'Allah has indeed ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' meaning its dissolution by expiation, which is mentioned in Surah al-Ma'idah. End. [Footnote: He said: 'upon' meaning 'in addition' or as a genitive connected to his saying 'making halal haram is an oath', i.e., based on that... The gist: if the oath is concerning a permissible act, like saying: 'By Allah, I will give charity today,' it entails making the permissible obligatory; because he had the capacity to leave it before the oath, so he obligated it upon himself by his oath. And if it is concerning a non-existent act, like: 'By Allah, I will not go to a certain place,' it entails...]
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling that declaring something halal as haram constitutes an oath (yamin). The author explains various phrases used by laypeople, such as 'halal upon me is haram' and Persian equivalents like 'halal barawiy haram' and 'harjah badast rast kiram barawiy haram'. According to the Hanafi school, these phrases are normally understood as oaths concerning food and drink, unless custom indicates otherwise. However, the later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) have given fatwa that if such phrases are used with the intent of divorce, or if custom dictates divorce, they are effective as divorce. Imam Bazdawi hesitated regarding custom as an indicator of divorce, and the earlier scholars (mutaqaddimūn) did not take that view. The relied-upon position is that the interpretation depends on custom: if the local custom is to intend divorce by such words, it is divorce; otherwise it is an oath regarding food and drink. The author then provides proofs from the Quran (Surah At-Tahrim, verse 1) and the story of the Prophet (peace be upon him) with honey and his wives, which establishes that making halal haram is treated as an oath requiring expiation (kaffarah). The passage also discusses the detail that if one says 'this is haram upon me' concerning a specific item, and then uses it in a permissible manner (e.g., buying with dirhams), he breaks his oath; but if he gives it away in charity or gift, he does not break it. The evidence is that the Prophet was commanded to perform kaffarah for his oath when he forbade honey upon himself. Thus, the core ruling is that tahrim al-halal is a yamin, and its consequences follow the general rules of oaths.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath legally binding the swearer to perform or refrain from an act, with expiation required upon breach.
تحريم الحلال (tahrim al-halal): The act of declaring something that is inherently permissible (halal) as forbidden (haram) upon oneself.
عرف (urf): Customary practice of the people in a particular time and place that determines the intended meaning of ambiguous statements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person says 'halal upon me is haram' without any custom or intention, what is the ruling?
A) It is considered an oath regarding food and drink only.
B) It automatically results in divorce.
C) It is considered a binding vow requiring immediate expiation.
D) The statement is null and has no effect.

Key Takeaway
Declaring halal haram is an oath (yamin) requiring expiation; custom determines whether it is a divorce or an oath about food and drink.

The First Two Conditions: Taqarrub and Avoidance of Sin
Lists two conditions with proofs and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first two Hanafi conditions for a valid vow and apply them to evaluate whether common vows (e.g., to saints or for sinful acts) are valid.

Core Idea
A valid vow must be exclusively for Allah; a vow to a creature or for a sin is haram and batil.

This lesson covers the first two conditions from the Hanafi list: the vow must be a means of drawing near to Allah (taqarrub) and must not be an act of disobedience (ma'siyah). These conditions are foundational because many common practices – like vowing money to a saint's shrine or vowing to commit a sin – are invalid. The hadith 'La nadhra fi ma'siyat Allah' provides the proof for the second condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage declares that a vow to other than Allah is haram and invalid; similarly, a vow to commit a sin is invalid. Determine whether the vow is directed to Allah alone and whether the vowed act is permissible.
The ruling on vows to creatures is explicitly from Qasim ibn Qutlubugha's commentary.
2
Locate the proof for the sin condition
The hadith 'لا نذر في معصية الله' is referenced. In Hanafi usul, the negation (la) indicates prohibition, and the phrase 'fi ma'siyat Allah' specifies the scope.
Thubut is zanni, but dalala is qati – the meaning is clear.
3
Distinguish between inherently sinful acts and those with a valid aspect
If the vowed act has an aspect of obedience but is temporarily forbidden due to another's right (like fasting on the day of sacrifice), the vow is contracted but one should fulfill on another day to avoid sin. This nuance is critical for correct application.
The author gives the example of fasting on Eid al-Adha.

١ . منها أن يكون النذر تقرباً إلى الله ، فإنّ النظر لغير الله الله حرام، ولا يجب الوفاء به ، بل يحرم أكل المنذور لغيره، وقد نص العلامة قاسم بن قُطُلُوبُغا في «شرح درر البحار»: إنّ النذر الذي يقع من أكثر العوام للأموات، كأن يقول : يا سيدي إن ردَّ غائبي أو شُفي مريضي فلك من الذهب والفضة كذا، أو من الثياب كذا، أو الطعام أو الشمع والزيت كذا ، حرام وباطل ؛ لكونه نذراً لمخلوق.
وما يؤخذ من الشمع والزيت والدراهم والحلوى في ضرائح الأولياء تقرباً إليهم حرام لا يحل أكله لا لغني ولا لفقير، ولا يجوز لخادم القبور أخذه ما لم يقصد الناذر التقرب إلى الله ، وذكر الولي إنّما هو لبيان محل صرف النذر، وهو مستحقوه القاطنون برباط أو مسجد، فحينئذ يحلّ أخذه لخدّام ذلك الرباط والمقبرة الفقراء لا الأغنياء، فإنّ الغني ليس بمصرف للنذر.
٢. ومنها : أن لا يكون النذر بمعصية، إذا كان حراماً لعينه ، وليس فيه وجه قربة، فإن كانت فيها جهة قربة وكانت محرَّمة للغير انعقد النذر به كنذر صوم يوم النحر، فلو صامه خرج عن العهدة وأثم والأحسنُ أن يفي بصوم يوم غيره. كذا في «الفتح»
٣. ومنها : أن لا يكون المنذورُ واجباً عليه قبل النذر، فلو نذر حجة الإسلام أو صلاة الظهر ونحو ذلك لم يلزمه شيء غيرها.
٤. ومنها : أن لا يكون المنذور أكثر مما يملكه أو غير مملوك له ، فلو قال لشاة غيره : لله علي أن أذبحها يوم النحر لم يصح النذر.
٥. ومنها : أن لا يكون مستحيل الوجود، فلو نذر صوم أمس أو اعتكافه لم يصح.
٦. ومنها : أن يكون المنذورُ من جنسه واجب شرعاً، فلا يلزم الناذرُ ما ليس من جنسه واجب بالنذر ، كما إذا نذر تشييع الجنازة ودخول المسجد.
٧. ومنها : أن يكون عبادة مقصودة فلا يلزم الوضوء، وتكفين الميت والغسل ونحو ذلك بالنذر.
ومن هاهنا يظهر أنه لا يلزم وفاءَ نذرِ انعقاد مجلس المولد، كما تعورف من العوام في زماننا، وفي المقام تفصيل ليس هذا موضعه، وقد فرغنا عن قدر منه في رسالتي : «السعي المشكور في بحث زيارة سيّد القبور قبر سيد أهل القبور»، صلّى الله عليه وآله وصحبه إلى يوم النشور.
قوله : وفى أو كَفِّر ؛ يعني هو مخير بين الوفاء بالنذر وإتيان المعلّق، وبين أداء كفارة اليمين، وتفصيله على ما ذكره حَسَن الشَّرنبلالي في رسالته : «تحفة التحرير وإسعاف الناذر والغني والفقير بالتخيير بين الوفاء والتحرير»: إنّ النذر المعلّق بما يقصد (التخيير هو رواية النوادر ، ولكنّه صح رجوع الإمام قبل وفاته بسبعة أيام عما نقل عنه في ظاهر الرواية من وجوب الوفاء، سواء علقه بشرط يريده أو بشرط لا يريده ، وبه كان يفتي إسماعيل الزاهد، وهو اختيار السرخسي في المبسوط (۸): (١٣٦ لكثرة البلوى في زماننا، وقال صاحب الدرر (۲: ٤٣) : وبه يفتى ، وفي التنوير (٣ (٦٩ وهو المذهب. وقال صاحب مجمع الأنهر (١ : ٥٤٨) : وفي أكثر المعتبرات هذا هو المذهب الصحيح المفتى به.)
The Book of Vows / Its Definition and Categories. Conditions are required for the validity of vows and the obligation of fulfilling them, as expanded by the author of al-Bada'i', al-Bahr, al-Nahr, and others: 1. One is that the vow must be a means of drawing near to Allah, for a vow to other than Allah is forbidden, and it is not obligatory to fulfill it; rather, consuming the vowed item for other than Him is forbidden. The scholar Qasim ibn Qutlubugha stated in Sharh Durar al-Bihar: The vow that most common people make to the dead, such as saying: 'O my master, if my absent one returns or my sick one is cured, then for you is such-and-such gold and silver, or such-and-such clothes, or food, or wax and oil' is forbidden and invalid because it is a vow to a creature. And what is taken of wax, oil, money, and sweets at the shrines of saints as a means of drawing near to them is forbidden; its consumption is not permissible for rich or poor. It is not permissible for the caretaker of graves to take it unless the person making the vow intended to draw near to Allah, and mentioning the saint is only to indicate the place of disbursement of the vow, and those entitled to it are the residents of a ribat or mosque. In that case, it is permissible for the poor caretakers of that ribat and cemetery to take it, not the rich, for the rich are not a channel for the vow. 2. And among them: that the vow must not be for disobedience, if it is inherently forbidden and contains no aspect of obedience. If it contains an aspect of obedience but is forbidden due to a right of another, the vow is contracted, like a vow to fast on the day of sacrifice. If he fasts it, he fulfills the obligation but sins, and it is better to fast on another day. Thus in al-Fath. 3. And among them: that the vowed thing must not already be obligatory upon him before the vow. So if he vows the Hajj of Islam or the Zuhr prayer and the like, nothing else is binding upon him. 4. And among them: that the vowed thing must not be more than what he owns or not owned by him. So if he says regarding another's sheep: 'For Allah I must slaughter it on the day of sacrifice,' the vow is invalid. 5. And among them: that it must not be impossible in existence. So if he vows to fast yesterday or to observe i'tikaf yesterday, it is invalid. 6. And among them: that the vowed thing must be of a type that is legally obligatory. So he is not bound by what is not of a type that is obligatory by vow, such as if he vows to accompany a funeral procession or enter a mosque. 7. And among them: that it must be an intended act of worship. So ablution, shrouding the deceased, washing, and the like are not binding by vow. From here it becomes clear that fulfilling the vow of holding a mawlid gathering, as is customary among the common people in our time, is not binding. There is a detail about this but this is not its place. We have completed a portion of it in our treatise: 'al-Sa'y al-Mashkur fi Bahth Ziyarat Sayyid al-Qubur Qabr Sayyid Ahl al-Qubur', may Allah send blessings upon him and his family and companions until the Day of Resurrection. His statement: 'or fulfill or expiate' means he has the choice between fulfilling the vow and doing the conditional act, and paying the expiation of an oath. The detail according to what Hasan al-Shurunbulali mentioned in his treatise: The suspended vow with what is intended... (the choice is a narration of al-Nawadir, but it is established that the Imam retracted seven days before his death from what was transmitted from him in the apparent narration regarding the obligation of fulfillment, whether he suspended it by a condition he desires or not; and this is the fatwa of Isma'il al-Zahid, and it is the choice of al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut due to the prevalence of need in our time. The author of al-Durar said: 'And this is the fatwa.' In al-Tanwir, it is the madhhab. The author of Majma' al-Anhur said: 'In most of the reliable books, this is the correct madhhab for fatwa.')
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the conditions for the validity of vows (nudhur) in the Hanafi school. The author lists seven conditions, drawing from earlier works like al-Bada'i' and al-Bahr. The first and most fundamental condition is that the vow must be an act of drawing near to Allah (taqarrub). Vows made to created beings, such as the common practice of vowing to saints or the dead, are forbidden and invalid. The author quotes Qasim ibn Qutlubugha who explicitly states that such vows are haram and batil because they are directed to a creature. The second condition is that the vowed act must not be inherently sinful (ma'siyah). However, if the act has a potential for obedience but is temporarily forbidden due to another's right (like fasting on the day of sacrifice), the vow is valid but one should fulfill it on another day to avoid sin. The third condition is that the vowed act must not already be obligatory upon the person before the vow (like Hajj or daily prayers), as a vow cannot add obligation to what is already required. The fourth condition is that the vowed item must be owned by the vower; vowing another's property is invalid. The fifth is that the vowed act must be possible; vowing to fast yesterday is impossible and thus invalid. The sixth condition requires that the vowed act be of a type that is legally obligatory in its category (like prayer or fasting); non-obligatory acts like visiting a funeral or entering a mosque are not binding through vows. The seventh condition limits vows to acts that are considered 'intended acts of worship' (ibadat maqsudah); acts like wudu, shrouding, and washing the dead are not binding if vowed. The author then applies these principles to the common practice of vowing to hold mawlid gatherings, stating that such vows are not binding. Finally, the passage discusses the ruling on suspended vows (mu'allaq) and the choice between fulfillment and expiation, citing the nawadir narration that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier opinion, making the choice (takhayyur) the relied-upon position in later fatwa.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow, a commitment to perform an act of worship or give charity as a means of drawing near to Allah.
تقرب (taqarrub): Drawing near to Allah through an act of worship; the primary condition for a valid vow.
معصية (ma'siyah): Disobedience to Allah; a vow to commit a sin is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi conditions, which of the following vows is valid?
A) Vowing to donate money to a saint's shrine if a loved one recovers.
B) Vowing to fast on the day of sacrifice as an act of thanks.
C) Vowing to pray extra tahajjud if a wish is fulfilled.
D) Vowing to give a portion of one's wealth to a temple.

Key Takeaway
A valid vow must be directed exclusively to Allah and must not involve disobedience; vows to creatures or for sin are invalid and must be avoided.

Suspended Vows: The Choice Between Fulfillment and Expiation
Discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute on suspended vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on suspended vows and identify the mufta bihi ruling regarding the choice between fulfillment and expiation.

Core Idea
For a suspended vow, the later Hanafi position allows a choice between fulfillment and expiation, overriding the earlier obligation.

This lesson focuses on the ruling of suspended vows (nadhr mu'allaq), where a person says, 'If Allah cures my sick, I will do such-and-such.' The originally transmitted position (Zahir al-Riwayah) required unconditional fulfillment. However, Imam Abu Hanifa is reported to have retracted this view seven days before his death, adopting a position of choice (takhayyur) between fulfilling the vow or paying the expiation of an oath. This later position is the relied-upon fatwa due to widespread need.

Guided Path
1
Define a suspended vow
A suspended vow is a conditional commitment: if a condition (e.g., recovery from illness) occurs, the vow must be fulfilled. The condition is often something desired.
The passage distinguishes between vows suspended by a condition one desires and those one does not desire.
2
State the original (Zahir al-Riwayah) position
In the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), fulfillment is obligatory whether the condition is desired or not. This is the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
This was the earlier view of Imam Abu Hanifa as well.
3
Note Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction
Seven days before his death, Imam Abu Hanifa retracted, adopting the Nawadir position that the person has a choice: either fulfill the vow or pay kaffarat al-yamin (expiation of an oath).
This change is attributed to the prevalence of need (kathrat balwa).
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The later choice position is the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school, supported by al-Sarakhsi, al-Durar, al-Tanwir, and others.
This is the authoritative ruling for contemporary fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان/ تعريفه وأقسامه
وتشترط لصحة النذور ووجوب وفائه شروط ، بسطها صاحبُ «البدائع» و «البحر» و«النهر» وغيرها :
١ . منها أن يكون النذر تقرباً إلى الله ، فإنّ النظر لغير الله الله حرام، ولا يجب الوفاء به ، بل يحرم أكل المنذور لغيره، وقد نص العلامة قاسم بن قُطُلُوبُغا في «شرح درر البحار»: إنّ النذر الذي يقع من أكثر العوام للأموات، كأن يقول : يا سيدي إن ردَّ غائبي أو شُفي مريضي فلك من الذهب والفضة كذا، أو من الثياب كذا، أو الطعام أو الشمع والزيت كذا ، حرام وباطل ؛ لكونه نذراً لمخلوق.
وما يؤخذ من الشمع والزيت والدراهم والحلوى في ضرائح الأولياء تقرباً إليهم حرام لا يحل أكله لا لغني ولا لفقير، ولا يجوز لخادم القبور أخذه ما لم يقصد الناذر التقرب إلى الله ، وذكر الولي إنّما هو لبيان محل صرف النذر، وهو مستحقوه القاطنون برباط أو مسجد، فحينئذ يحلّ أخذه لخدّام ذلك الرباط والمقبرة الفقراء لا الأغنياء، فإنّ الغني ليس بمصرف للنذر.
٢. ومنها : أن لا يكون النذر بمعصية، إذا كان حراماً لعينه ، وليس فيه وجه قربة، فإن كانت فيها جهة قربة وكانت محرَّمة للغير انعقد النذر به كنذر صوم يوم النحر، فلو صامه خرج عن العهدة وأثم والأحسنُ أن يفي بصوم يوم غيره. كذا في «الفتح»
٣. ومنها : أن لا يكون المنذورُ واجباً عليه قبل النذر، فلو نذر حجة الإسلام أو صلاة الظهر ونحو ذلك لم يلزمه شيء غيرها.
٤. ومنها : أن لا يكون المنذور أكثر مما يملكه أو غير مملوك له ، فلو قال لشاة غيره : لله علي أن أذبحها يوم النحر لم يصح النذر.
٥. ومنها : أن لا يكون مستحيل الوجود، فلو نذر صوم أمس أو اعتكافه لم يصح.
٦. ومنها : أن يكون المنذورُ من جنسه واجب شرعاً، فلا يلزم الناذرُ ما ليس من جنسه واجب بالنذر ، كما إذا نذر تشييع الجنازة ودخول المسجد.
٧. ومنها : أن يكون عبادة مقصودة فلا يلزم الوضوء، وتكفين الميت والغسل ونحو ذلك بالنذر.
ومن هاهنا يظهر أنه لا يلزم وفاءَ نذرِ انعقاد مجلس المولد، كما تعورف من العوام في زماننا، وفي المقام تفصيل ليس هذا موضعه، وقد فرغنا عن قدر منه في رسالتي : «السعي المشكور في بحث زيارة سيّد القبور قبر سيد أهل القبور»، صلّى الله عليه وآله وصحبه إلى يوم النشور.
قوله : وفى أو كَفِّر ؛ يعني هو مخير بين الوفاء بالنذر وإتيان المعلّق، وبين أداء كفارة اليمين، وتفصيله على ما ذكره حَسَن الشَّرنبلالي في رسالته : «تحفة التحرير وإسعاف الناذر والغني والفقير بالتخيير بين الوفاء والتحرير»: إنّ النذر المعلّق بما يقصد (التخيير هو رواية النوادر ، ولكنّه صح رجوع الإمام قبل وفاته بسبعة أيام عما نقل عنه في ظاهر الرواية من وجوب الوفاء، سواء علقه بشرط يريده أو بشرط لا يريده ، وبه كان يفتي إسماعيل الزاهد، وهو اختيار السرخسي في المبسوط (۸): (١٣٦ لكثرة البلوى في زماننا، وقال صاحب الدرر (۲: ٤٣) : وبه يفتى ، وفي التنوير (٣ (٦٩ وهو المذهب. وقال صاحب مجمع الأنهر (١ : ٥٤٨) : وفي أكثر المعتبرات هذا هو المذهب الصحيح المفتى به.)
The Book of Vows / Its Definition and Categories. Conditions are required for the validity of vows and the obligation of fulfilling them, as expanded by the author of al-Bada'i', al-Bahr, al-Nahr, and others: 1. One is that the vow must be a means of drawing near to Allah, for a vow to other than Allah is forbidden, and it is not obligatory to fulfill it; rather, consuming the vowed item for other than Him is forbidden. The scholar Qasim ibn Qutlubugha stated in Sharh Durar al-Bihar: The vow that most common people make to the dead, such as saying: 'O my master, if my absent one returns or my sick one is cured, then for you is such-and-such gold and silver, or such-and-such clothes, or food, or wax and oil' is forbidden and invalid because it is a vow to a creature. And what is taken of wax, oil, money, and sweets at the shrines of saints as a means of drawing near to them is forbidden; its consumption is not permissible for rich or poor. It is not permissible for the caretaker of graves to take it unless the person making the vow intended to draw near to Allah, and mentioning the saint is only to indicate the place of disbursement of the vow, and those entitled to it are the residents of a ribat or mosque. In that case, it is permissible for the poor caretakers of that ribat and cemetery to take it, not the rich, for the rich are not a channel for the vow. 2. And among them: that the vow must not be for disobedience, if it is inherently forbidden and contains no aspect of obedience. If it contains an aspect of obedience but is forbidden due to a right of another, the vow is contracted, like a vow to fast on the day of sacrifice. If he fasts it, he fulfills the obligation but sins, and it is better to fast on another day. Thus in al-Fath. 3. And among them: that the vowed thing must not already be obligatory upon him before the vow. So if he vows the Hajj of Islam or the Zuhr prayer and the like, nothing else is binding upon him. 4. And among them: that the vowed thing must not be more than what he owns or not owned by him. So if he says regarding another's sheep: 'For Allah I must slaughter it on the day of sacrifice,' the vow is invalid. 5. And among them: that it must not be impossible in existence. So if he vows to fast yesterday or to observe i'tikaf yesterday, it is invalid. 6. And among them: that the vowed thing must be of a type that is legally obligatory. So he is not bound by what is not of a type that is obligatory by vow, such as if he vows to accompany a funeral procession or enter a mosque. 7. And among them: that it must be an intended act of worship. So ablution, shrouding the deceased, washing, and the like are not binding by vow. From here it becomes clear that fulfilling the vow of holding a mawlid gathering, as is customary among the common people in our time, is not binding. There is a detail about this but this is not its place. We have completed a portion of it in our treatise: 'al-Sa'y al-Mashkur fi Bahth Ziyarat Sayyid al-Qubur Qabr Sayyid Ahl al-Qubur', may Allah send blessings upon him and his family and companions until the Day of Resurrection. His statement: 'or fulfill or expiate' means he has the choice between fulfilling the vow and doing the conditional act, and paying the expiation of an oath. The detail according to what Hasan al-Shurunbulali mentioned in his treatise: The suspended vow with what is intended... (the choice is a narration of al-Nawadir, but it is established that the Imam retracted seven days before his death from what was transmitted from him in the apparent narration regarding the obligation of fulfillment, whether he suspended it by a condition he desires or not; and this is the fatwa of Isma'il al-Zahid, and it is the choice of al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut due to the prevalence of need in our time. The author of al-Durar said: 'And this is the fatwa.' In al-Tanwir, it is the madhhab. The author of Majma' al-Anhur said: 'In most of the reliable books, this is the correct madhhab for fatwa.')
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the conditions for the validity of vows (nudhur) in the Hanafi school. The author lists seven conditions, drawing from earlier works like al-Bada'i' and al-Bahr. The first and most fundamental condition is that the vow must be an act of drawing near to Allah (taqarrub). Vows made to created beings, such as the common practice of vowing to saints or the dead, are forbidden and invalid. The author quotes Qasim ibn Qutlubugha who explicitly states that such vows are haram and batil because they are directed to a creature. The second condition is that the vowed act must not be inherently sinful (ma'siyah). However, if the act has a potential for obedience but is temporarily forbidden due to another's right (like fasting on the day of sacrifice), the vow is valid but one should fulfill it on another day to avoid sin. The third condition is that the vowed act must not already be obligatory upon the person before the vow (like Hajj or daily prayers), as a vow cannot add obligation to what is already required. The fourth condition is that the vowed item must be owned by the vower; vowing another's property is invalid. The fifth is that the vowed act must be possible; vowing to fast yesterday is impossible and thus invalid. The sixth condition requires that the vowed act be of a type that is legally obligatory in its category (like prayer or fasting); non-obligatory acts like visiting a funeral or entering a mosque are not binding through vows. The seventh condition limits vows to acts that are considered 'intended acts of worship' (ibadat maqsudah); acts like wudu, shrouding, and washing the dead are not binding if vowed. The author then applies these principles to the common practice of vowing to hold mawlid gatherings, stating that such vows are not binding. Finally, the passage discusses the ruling on suspended vows (mu'allaq) and the choice between fulfillment and expiation, citing the nawadir narration that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier opinion, making the choice (takhayyur) the relied-upon position in later fatwa.

Key Terms
نذر معلق (nadhr mu'allaq): A conditional vow, e.g., 'If my sick person recovers, I will do such-and-such.'
تخيير (takhayyur): The choice between two options; in this context, between fulfilling the vow and paying expiation.
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): Expiation for an oath: feeding or clothing ten poor people, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi position, what is the ruling for a suspended vow?
A) The vow is invalid because it is conditional.
B) Fulfillment is the only option.
C) The person has a choice between fulfillment and expiation.
D) Expiation is required regardless of fulfillment.

Key Takeaway
For suspended vows, the relied-upon Hanafi position allows a choice between fulfillment and expiation, providing flexibility due to widespread need.

Conditions for a Valid Nadhr (Vow) in Hanafi Fiqh
Lists and explains six conditions for a valid nadhr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for a valid nadhr in Hanafi fiqh and apply them to evaluate the validity of a given vow.

Core Idea
A valid vow requires the vowed act to be possible, owned, not already wajib, and an intended worship.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every promise to do a good deed constitutes a binding vow (nadhr). The classical jurists established specific conditions that determine whether a vow is valid and must be fulfilled. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lists these conditions clearly, helping you identify which vows are legally binding and which are invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions listed in the text
Read the Arabic text and translation to locate each condition. The passage enumerates five main conditions (though the numbering shows 3,4,5,6,6). List them in order.
Condition 6 has two sub-points: (a) the vowed act must be from a class that is obligatory in the Law, and (b) it must be an intended act of worship.
2
Understand the rationale behind each condition
For each condition, consider why the Hanafi school imposes it. For example, the condition that the act not already be obligatory prevents duplication of obligation; the requirement of ownership prevents vowing what one does not possess.
3
Distinguish between acts that are 'already wajib' and acts that become wajib by vow
The condition excludes acts like the obligatory prayers or Hajj al-Islam because they are already binding. A vow cannot make them more obligatory; it only reaffirms what is already due.
4
Recognize the concept of 'ibadah maqsudah'
Intended acts of worship (like salah, sawm, sadaqah) are ends in themselves. Acts that are mere means (like wudu, ghusl) are not valid subjects of a vow. Compare with the example given in the text.
This distinction is specific to the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the conditions to a contemporary issue
The text mentions vowing a Mawlid gathering. Use the conditions to explain why such a vow is not binding. Then test other scenarios like vowing to attend a lecture or give charity.
The commentator notes that there is further detail elsewhere, but the conditions alone suffice for this analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣. ومنها : أن لا يكون المنذورُ واجباً عليه قبل النذر، فلو نذر حجة الإسلام أو صلاة الظهر ونحو ذلك لم يلزمه شيء غيرها.
٤. ومنها : أن لا يكون المنذور أكثر مما يملكه أو غير مملوك له ، فلو قال لشاة غيره : لله علي أن أذبحها يوم النحر لم يصح النذر.
ه . ومنها : أن لا يكون مستحيل الوجود، فلو نذر صوم أمس أو اعتكافه لم يصح.
ه. ومنها : أن يكون المنذورُ من جنسه واجب شرعاً، فلا يلزم الناذرُ ما ليس من جنسه واجب بالنذر ، كما إذا نذر تشييع الجنازة ودخول المسجد. ٦ . ومنها : أن يكون عبادة مقصودة فلا يلزم الوضوء، وتكفين الميت والغسل ونحو ذلك بالنذر.
ومن هاهنا يظهر أنه لا يلزم وفاءَ نذرِ انعقاد مجلس المولد، كما تعورف من العوام في زماننا، وفي المقام تفصيل ليس هذا موضعه، وقد فرغنا عن قدر منه في رسالتي : «السعي المشكور في بحث زيارة سيد القبور قبر سيد أهل القبور»، صلّى الله عليه وآله وصحبه إلى يوم النشور.
3. Among them: that the vowed thing should not be obligatory upon him before the vow. So if he vows the Hajj of Islam or the Zuhr prayer and the like, nothing other than them becomes obligatory upon him. 4. Among them: that the vowed thing should not be more than what he owns or not owned by him. So if he says regarding someone else's sheep: 'By Allah, I must slaughter it on the day of Nahr', the vow is not valid. 5. Among them: that it should not be impossible of existence. So if he vows fasting yesterday or i'tikaf in it, it is not valid. 6. Among them: that the vowed thing should be from a class that is obligatory in the Law. So the vow does not obligate the vower what is not from its class obligatory by vow, as if he vows attending a funeral procession and entering the mosque. 6. Among them: that it should be an intended act of worship. So wudu, shrouding the dead, ghusl and the like are not obligated by nadhr. And from here it becomes clear that it is not incumbent to fulfill the vow of holding a Mawlid gathering, as has become customary among the common folk in our time. And in this matter there is a detail not appropriate for this place. We have completed a portion of it in my treatise: 'al-Sa'y al-Mashkur fi Bahth Ziyarat Sayyid al-Qubur...'
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage lists conditions for a valid nadhr (vow) in the Hanafi school. The text first states that the vowed act must not already be obligatory (wajib) before the vow. For example, if one vows to perform Hajj al-Islam (the obligatory Hajj) or the obligatory Zuhr prayer, the vow does not make a separate obligation; he only has to perform what was already due. Second, the vowed item must be owned by the vower or within his capacity; vowing to slaughter another's sheep is invalid. Third, the vowed act must be possible; fasting yesterday after the day has passed is impossible. Fourth, the vowed act must be of a type that is considered 'obligatory' in the law in the sense that its class is valid for vowing; acts like attending a funeral or entering a mosque are not from the class of acts that become obligatory by vow. Fifth, the vowed act must be an intended act of worship (ibadah maqsudah); acts that are merely means to worship, like wudu or shrouding, are not valid subjects of a vow. Finally, the commentator notes that based on these conditions, the common practice of vowing to hold a Mawlid gathering is not legally binding, as it is not an intended act of worship in the required sense. He refers to his own treatise for further detail.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow whereby one makes a particular act of worship obligatory upon oneself for the sake of Allah.
عبادة مقصودة (ibadah maqsudah): An act of worship intended in itself, not merely a means to another worship.
مجلس المولد (majlis al-mawlid): A gathering to celebrate the birth of the Prophet Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وسلم).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following vows would be valid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Vowing to perform the Zuhr prayer (obligatory prayer).
B) Vowing to slaughter a sheep owned by someone else.
C) Vowing to fast three days for a desired outcome.
D) Vowing to perform ghusl (ritual bath) for prayer.

Key Takeaway
A valid nadhr requires the vowed act to be possible, owned, not already wajib, and an intended worship.

Suspended Vows with Undesired Conditions: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Hanifa's retraction on suspended vows with undesired conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on a suspended vow with an undesired condition, identify the mufta bihi, and apply it to a new scenario.

Core Idea
A vow tied to a sin you don't intend carries both vow and oath – giving you a choice.

What happens when someone makes a suspended vow but the condition is something they never want to do – like swearing to perform Hajj if they commit adultery? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this. The earlier, widely transmitted view (Zahir al-Riwayah) demands the vow be fulfilled. But Imam Abu Hanifa later retracted, giving the vower a choice: fulfill the vow or pay expiation for an oath. This lesson follows that debate and the reasoning behind the final fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the ruling of Zahir al-Riwayah (obligatory fulfillment regardless of the condition) and the later view of Abu Hanifa (choice between fulfillment and expiation).
2
Analyze the reasoning of Zahir al-Riwayah
Understand that a suspended vow becomes immediate upon occurrence of the condition, and immediate vows cannot be replaced by expiation.
Sahibayn and early Abu Hanifa held this view.
3
Examine the dual nature of the utterance
In an undesired condition, the speaker's phrase contains both a vow (the form of commitment) and an oath (the intent to prevent oneself). This duality justifies the option.
The hadith 'Kaffarat al-nadhr kaffarat al-yamin' supports the oath analogy.
4
Understand the retraction and its acceptance
Abu Hanifa retracted seven days before his death; later scholars like al-Sarakhsi accepted it, and it became the mufta bihi despite being from Nawadir.
The author corrects a mistake: the option only applies to undesired conditions, not both.
5
Apply the mufta bihi to a case
Given 'If I lie, I must fast a month', the vower can either fast or pay kaffarah yamin (feed 10 poor or fast 3 days).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعلق بما لا يقصد كونه المذكور في ظاهر الرواية وجوب الوفاء فيه أيضاً؛ لأنَّ المعلق بالشرط كالمنجز عند وجوده، ولو نجز النذر عند وجود الكفارة لا تجزئه الكفارة، بل يجب الإتيان بالمنذور فكذا هذا. عن أبي حنيفة الله إنه رجع عنه قبل موته بسبعة أيام إلى أنه بخير بين الوفاء وبين التكفير؛ وذلك لأنّ في الشرط الذي لا يريدُ كونه كقوله : إن زنيت فعلى حجة أو صوم سنة ، كلامه يشتمل على معنى النذر واليمين جميعاً. أما معنى النذر فظاهر؛ لوجود صيغة الالتزام. وأما معنى اليمين؛ فلأنه قصد به الامتناع عن إيجاد الشرط، فكان مراده : إني لا أفعل هذا، ولو فعلت فعلي كذا، فيتخير بين أن يميل إلى جهة النذر فيفي، وبين أن يميل إلى جهة اليمين فيكفر، وعليه يحمل حديث: «كفارة النذر كفارة اليمين»، أخرجه أصحاب السنن. وهذا التخيير في شرط لا يريد كونه، وإن كان خلاف ظاهر الرواية، فإنَّ ظاهر الرواية هو وجوب الوفاء بالنذر مطلقاً كان أو معلّقاً بشرط يريده، أو بشرط لا يريده، لكن لما ثبت رجوع الإمام إليه أفتى به جمع من المشايخ، كالصدر الشهيد، وشمس الأئمة السِّرَخْسي، ومشايخ بلخ وبُخارا ، وصححه في «الهداية»، وقال في «البزازية»: عليه الفتوى. [قوله : القول الآخر؛ هذا هو ظاهرُ الرواية، والتفصيل هو رواية النوادر، وقد أخطأ صاحب البحر حيث ظنّ أنّ في رواية النوادر التخيير فيهما، وذلك لما عرفت]
And the suspended [vow] with what is not intended to happen: in Zahir al-Riwayah, fulfillment is obligatory in it as well; because the suspended [vow] upon condition is like an immediate [vow] when the condition occurs. And if an immediate vow occurs while there is expiation [due], the expiation does not suffice him; rather, the vowed act must be performed. So similarly this. From Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) that he retracted from it seven days before his death to the position that he has a choice between fulfillment and expiation. This is because in the condition which he does not desire to happen, like saying 'If I commit adultery, then upon me is Hajj or fasting for a year', his speech contains both the meaning of nadhr and yamin. As for the meaning of nadhr, it is apparent due to the presence of the formula of commitment. As for the meaning of yamin, because he intends by it to abstain from bringing about the condition. So his intent is: I will not do this, and if I do, then such-and-such is upon me. So he can choose between inclining to the aspect of nadhr and fulfilling it, or inclining to the aspect of yamin and expiating. And upon this is carried the hadith: 'The expiation for a vow is the expiation for an oath' reported by the authors of the Sunan. And this choice in the condition he does not desire to happen, although it contradicts Zahir al-Riwayah, for Zahir al-Riwayah is that fulfillment of the vow is obligatory whether unconditional or suspended with a desired condition or with an undesired condition. But when the Imam's retraction was established, a group of scholars gave fatwa accordingly, such as al-Sadr al-Shahid, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, the scholars of Balkh and Bukhara. And it was deemed correct in al-Hidayah, and in al-Bazzaziyya it is said: 'Upon it is the fatwa.' [His saying: 'the other view': this is Zahir al-Riwayah, and the detailed view is the narration of the Nawadir. And the author of al-Bahr erred in thinking that in the Nawadir narration there is choice in both; that is as you have learned.]
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding suspended vows (al-nadhr al-mu'allaq) specifically when the condition is one which the vower does not desire to occur (e.g., 'If I commit adultery, I must perform Hajj'). The text first states the position of Zahir al-Riwayah: regardless of whether the condition is desired or undesired, fulfillment of the vowed act is obligatory. The reasoning is that once the condition occurs, the vow becomes immediate, and expiation does not suffice in immediate vows. However, the passage then narrates a retraction (ruju') from Abu Hanifa seven days before his death: he adopted the view that the vower has a choice (takhayyur) between fulfilling the vow or paying expiation (kaffarah yamin). This is because such a statement carries both the meaning of a nadhr (due to the form of commitment) and a yamin (due to the intent of preventing oneself from the condition). The hadith 'Kaffarat al-nadhr kaffarat al-yamin' is cited in support. Although this contradicts Zahir al-Riwayah (which is the transmission from Muhammad al-Shaybani from Abu Hanifa), the retraction was accepted by later scholars, and many muftis (like al-Sarakhsi and the scholars of Balkh and Bukhara) opined that fatwa is given accordingly. The text also corrects a mistake by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, who thought that the Nawadir narration (rare transmissions) also gave choice in both types of conditions; actually, the choice is only for the undesired condition. The relied-upon position for fatwa is thus the later view of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
النذر المعلق (al-nadhr al-mu'allaq): A vow conditioned on the occurrence of a future event, e.g., 'If Allah cures my sick, I will fast a month.'
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) representing the primary transmission of Abu Hanifa's views.
رواية النوادر (Riwayat al-Nawadir): Rare or less common transmissions from the early Hanafi imams, distinct from Zahir al-Riwayah.
التخيير (al-takhayyur): The option given to the vower to choose between fulfilling the vow or paying expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later view of Abu Hanifa accepted for fatwa, what is the ruling for a person who says: 'If I commit adultery, then I must perform Hajj'?
A) He must fulfill the vow and perform Hajj.
B) He has a choice: either perform Hajj or pay expiation for an oath.
C) He must pay expiation only, no need to perform Hajj.
D) The vow is invalid because the condition is sinful.

Key Takeaway
In a suspended vow with an undesired condition, the mufta bihi position gives the vower a choice between fulfilling the vow or paying expiation for an oath.

The Hanafi Position on Conditional Vows with Undesired Conditions
Explains Hanafi ikhtilaf on nadhr mu'allaq with unwanted conditions and Abu Hanifa's retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional vows with undesired conditions and state the mufta bihi position regarding the choice between fulfillment and expiation.

Core Idea
A conditional vow on an undesired act partakes of both vow and oath, giving the person a choice to fulfill or expiate.

This lesson examines a nuanced issue within Hanafi fiqh: when a person makes a vow conditional upon an act they do not desire to occur, do they have to fulfill the vow, or can they expiate it like an oath? The passage reveals a significant intra-school difference between the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) and Imam Abu Hanifa's later retraction, which became the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the two competing positions
The passage presents the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) which mandates unconditional fulfillment of a conditional vow once the condition occurs, and Abu Hanifa's retraction which grants a choice between fulfillment and oath expiation (kaffarah).
Both positions are from within the Hanafi school; this is an intra-madhhab dispute.
2
2. Understand the reasoning for the apparent transmission
Zahir al-riwayah treats the conditional vow as equivalent to an unconditional vow upon condition occurrence. Since the vowed act is itself a form of worship, expiation cannot substitute.
This position emphasizes the literal nature of the vow.
3
3. Grasp the basis of Abu Hanifa's retraction
Abu Hanifa later recognized that a conditional vow tied to an undesired act contains two meanings: a vow (nadhr) on the surface, and an oath (yamin) in intent (to prevent oneself from doing the act). The hadith 'Kaffarah al-nadhr kaffarat al-yamin' supports this dual nature.
The retraction occurred seven days before his death.
4
4. Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
Despite contradicting zahir al-riwayah, Abu Hanifa's retraction was adopted by later authorities like al-Sarakhsi and al-Hidayah, making the choice the operative ruling for fatwa.
This is an example where later preference (nawadir) overrides the apparent transmission.
5
5. Consider the exception for sinful conditions
The passage discusses a refinement: if the condition is a sinful act, some scholars argued that choice should not apply because it grants leniency for sin. However, the adopted view still allows choice, as expiation is a legal concession similar to that for broken oaths.
This shows the dynamic nature of fiqh reasoning.

Book of Oaths: Definition and Divisions. He only said this to guard against the other statement, which is the obligation of fulfillment whether he ties it to a condition he desires or does not desire. The obligatory matter upon the occurrence of the condition is fulfillment and performing the vowed act itself because the meaning of an oath is absent in it; because he intended to show desire for what he made a condition, like saying: 'If Allah cures my sick one.' And what is tied to a condition he does not intend to occur – mentioned in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) – the obligation of fulfillment is also in it, because the conditional is like the unconditional upon its occurrence. If the vow were made unconditional at the occurrence of the expiation, the expiation would not suffice for him; rather fulfillment is obligatory, and likewise this. From Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that he retracted from it seven days before his death, that he has a choice between fulfillment and expiation. That is because in the condition he does not intend to occur, like saying: 'If I commit adultery, then a Hajj or fasting a year upon me,' his speech contains the meaning of both a vow and an oath. As for the meaning of a vow, it is apparent due to the presence of the formula of commitment. As for the meaning of an oath, it is because he intended by it to refrain from bringing about the condition, so his intent is: 'I will not do this, and if I do, then such-and-such is upon me.' So he may choose between inclining toward the aspect of the vow and fulfilling, or inclining toward the aspect of the oath and expiating. Upon this is carried the hadith: 'The expiation of a vow is the expiation of an oath,' narrated by the authors of the Sunan. And this choice is in a condition he does not intend to occur, even though it is contrary to the apparent transmission, for the apparent transmission is that fulfillment of the vow is obligatory whether it is absolute or tied to a condition he desires or a condition he does not desire. But because the Imam's retraction was established, a group of scholars gave fatwa accordingly, such as al-Sadr al-Shahid, Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi, the scholars of Balkh and Bukhara, and it was corrected in al-Hidayah, and it was said in al-Bazzaziyyah: upon it is the fatwa. [11] His statement: 'The other statement; this is the apparent transmission, and the detail is the narration of the Nawadir. The author of al-Bahr erred where he thought that in the narration of the Nawadir there is choice in both.' And this was correct; because when he ties it to a condition he does not intend, there is the meaning of an oath in it (which is prevention), but in its apparent form it is a vow, so he has a choice. I say: If the condition is a forbidden matter, like 'If I commit adultery,' for example, he should not have a choice; because choice is a leniency, and what is forbidden does not necessitate leniency. And in a condition he desires, there is no choice at all, neither in the Nawadir nor in the principles; rather the obligation of fulfillment is agreed upon in it. The difference of the two narrations is only in the condition he does not intend but rather intends to refrain from. [1] His statement: 'If the condition...' etc. Its summary: If the condition he does not intend to occur is a forbidden matter – i.e., legally prohibited – whether absolutely forbidden or prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi), like 'If I commit adultery,' 'If I drink wine,' 'If I abandon prayer,' or 'If I play chess,' then upon me is fasting for a year, he should not have a choice when the condition occurs between performing the vowed act and paying the expiation of an oath; rather he must fulfill, because in choice there is leniency, and committing a prohibited matter does not necessitate leniency. There is a discussion from several aspects: One of them: sometimes fulfillment may be lighter than paying expiation, so there is no meaning to absolutely obligating fulfillment to achieve severity, like if he says: 'If I commit adultery, then fasting one day or praying two rak'ahs upon me,' for performing such an act is much lighter than paying expiation, especially if the one making the vow is wealthy. And it was answered: Because the original purpose of choice in the law is for leniency, so it does not necessitate committing a forbidden act. But there is consideration: mere choice does not negate committing a forbidden act legally. Do you not see that breaking an oath is legally forbidden, yet the Lawgiver gave the one who breaks an oath a choice between freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing?
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning vows (nadhr) that are conditional upon a situation the person does not genuinely desire, such as swearing to perform a pilgrimage if one falls into sin. The author explains that according to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) of Imam Muhammad, the vow must be fulfilled regardless of whether the condition is desired or not. The reasoning is that a conditional vow becomes like an unconditional one when the condition occurs; since the vowed act itself is due, expiation cannot substitute. However, Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) retracted this position seven days before his death. He ruled that the person has a choice between fulfilling the vow or giving expiation (kaffarah) of an oath. The rationale is that the conditional expression contains two elements: the outward form of a vow (nadhr) and the inner intent of an oath (yamin) meant to deter oneself from the condition. The hadith 'Kaffarah al-nadhr kaffarat al-yamin' (the expiation of a vow is the expiation of an oath) supports this. Despite contrary to the apparent transmission, this retraction became the basis for fatwa by later scholars such as al-Sarakhsi and those of Balkh and Bukhara, and it is affirmed in al-Hidayah. The passage then discusses a refinement: if the condition is a sinful act (e.g., adultery), should the choice still apply? The commentator suggests that it should not, because allowing leniency (takhfif) for committing a sin seems inappropriate. However, this view is debated. A counterargument is that fulfillment might be easier than expiation, so obliging it does not necessarily produce severity. The final response defends the choice principle: the very nature of the law's provisions for expiation (e.g., in breaking an oath) shows that choice does not condone the sin; it is a legal concession. Thus the mufta bihi is the choice between fulfillment and expiation for any conditional vow where the condition is not intended, regardless of whether the condition is sinful.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow whereby a person obligates upon himself an act of worship, conditional or unconditional.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, often by Allah, to do or not do something; sometimes used for a vow that has a preventive intent.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books, representing the majority of the school.
النوادر (al-nawadir): Rare transmissions from Hanafi imams that are less authoritative than zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what must a person do if he says 'If I commit adultery, I must perform Hajj' and then commits adultery?
A) He must perform Hajj; expiation is not acceptable.
B) He has the choice between performing Hajj or giving kaffarah yamin.
C) He must give kaffarah yamin only; the vow is invalid.
D) He is not required to do anything because the condition is sinful.

Key Takeaway
For a conditional vow on an undesired act, the Hanafi school permits a choice between fulfillment and oath expiation, based on Abu Hanifa's retraction.

Choice in Conditional Vows with Haram Conditions
Critique of the expounder's objection on choice in haram-conditioned vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school maintains the choice (takhyir) in a conditional vow even when the condition is haram, and refute the opposing intra-Hanafi view.

Core Idea
A conditional vow on a haram act still gives a choice, because the expression combines oath and vow meanings.

This lesson examines a critical refinement by Allamah Lakhnawi against the position of an expounder who argued that if the condition of a vow is a forbidden act, the person should not have a choice between fulfillment and kaffarah. Lakhnawi defends the original Hanafi ruling that the choice remains, regardless of the nature of the condition. Through three arguments, he shows that the cause of the choice is the linguistic form of the vow, not the act itself.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling and the expounder's objection
Recall that when a person makes a conditional vow on a condition he does not desire, the base ruling (from the matn) is that he has a choice between fulfilling the vow or paying kaffarah. The expounder objects: if the condition is haram, there should be no choice because haram does not warrant concession.
The condition is e.g. 'If I commit zina, then I must fast for a year'.
2
Identify the expounder's reasoning
The expounder argues that choice is a concession (takhfif), and a haram act should not lead to a concession. Therefore, only fulfillment (al-wafa) should be obligatory.
The expounder's view is that the vow-maker must fulfill the vow without option.
3
Analyze Lakhnawi's first objection: fulfillment may be lighter than kaffarah
Lakhnawi points out that the vowed act (e.g., fasting one day) could be much lighter than kaffarah (freeing a slave, feeding 10 poor, or clothing), especially if the person is wealthy. So obligating fulfillment absolutely is not necessarily more severe; it could be a greater concession.
Thus the expounder's premise that fulfillment is always stricter is false.
4
Analyze the second objection: the choice arises from the servant's own speech
The expounder's reply: the choice in this case is established by the servant's own statement (combining oath and vow meanings), not by the Lawgiver. The Lawgiver should not remove a choice that the servant himself created. Lakhnawi notes that the Lawgiver respects the speaker's probable intention.
If the servant's words allow both fulfillment and kaffarah, the Lawgiver cannot force one.
5
Analyze the third and decisive objection: the cause (sabab) is the dual meaning, not the act
Lakhnawi's final argument: what is prohibited is that a haram act itself be the cause for a concession. Here, the cause for the choice is not the haram act but the fact that the expression 'If I do X, then upon me is Y' comprises both the meaning of an oath (prevention) and a vow (obligation). The haram act is merely the trigger, not the cause of the choice.
Therefore, the choice is validly established from the linguistic structure, irrespective of the act.

Source Text

And this is sound only because when he conditions it on a condition he does not desire, it contains the meaning of an oath, which is prevention, but in its outward form it is a vow, so he has a choice. I say: If the condition is a forbidden matter, like if he says 'If I commit zina,' it is appropriate that he should not have a choice, because choice is a concession, and what is forbidden does not necessitate a concession. Rather, in the case of a condition that he desires, the choice is not mentioned at all, neither in the nawadir nor in the usul; rather, fulfillment is agreed upon. The difference of the two narrations is only in the condition that he does not desire, whereby he intends to abstain from it. [1] His statement: 'If the condition...' etc.; its summary: If the condition that he does not desire is a prohibited matter – i.e., legally prohibited – whether it is absolutely prohibited or prohibitively disliked, like 'If I commit zina,' or 'If I drink wine,' or 'If I abandon prayer,' or 'If I play chess,' then upon me is a year of fasting, then it is appropriate that he should not have a choice when the condition occurs between fulfilling the vowed act and paying the oath expiation; rather, fulfillment is obligatory upon him because in choice there is a concession, and committing a prohibited matter does not necessitate a concession. And there is a discussion from several aspects: First: Fulfillment may be lighter than paying expiation, so there is no meaning to obligating fulfillment absolutely for the sake of severity, as if he says: 'If I commit zina, then upon me is fasting one day or praying two rak'ahs,' then performing such is much lighter than paying expiation, especially if the vow-maker is wealthy. And it is answered: Because the original establishment of choice in the Shari'ah is for concession, so the prohibited act is not necessitated. But there is a consideration: Mere choice does not contradict committing a prohibited act legally; do you not see that breaking an oath is prohibited legally, yet the Lawgiver gave the oath-breaker a choice between freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing. Second: Concession is only the choice that the Lawgiver has made, not the choice that the servant makes for himself. For if a man says: 'If I commit zina, then upon me is freeing a slave or feeding a poor person,' it would be sufficient for him to perform one of them. Here, the choice is established by the servant himself through his speech comprising the meaning of an oath and the meaning of a vow. And it is answered: Since his expression is probable between undertaking fulfillment and expiation, it is appropriate that the Lawgiver should incline the side of fulfillment so that it not be a concession. But there is a consideration: It is not for the Lawgiver to oblige a man with one of the probabilities of his speech definitively while there exists the other probability, for a person is taken according to what he utters. Third: And it is the solution: What is prohibited is only that the prohibited act is a cause for concession, but its being established by another way is not harmful. Here, the cause for choice is only that the expression is probable of the meaning of an oath and a vow. In summary: The objection of the expounder is invalid from its very root, even if he boasts about it.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, where the author, Allamah Abdul Hai al-Lakhnawi, responds to the position of the expounder (sharh) of al-Wiqaya. The issue is a person who makes a conditional vow (nadhr) with a condition he does not desire, but the condition is a haram act (e.g., 'If I commit zina, then I must fast for a year'). The base ruling from the matn is that such a person, upon the condition occurring, has a choice (ikhtiyar) between fulfilling the vow or paying the kaffarah of an oath (yameen) because the expression combines the meaning of an oath (prevention) and a vow (obligation). The expounder argued that if the condition is haram, there should be no choice because choice is a concession, and haram does not warrant concession. The author refutes this with three objections: (1) Fulfilling the vow might be lighter than kaffarah, so obligating fulfillment absolutely is not always more severe; (2) The choice here is established by the servant's own speech, not by the Lawgiver, so the Lawgiver should not remove it; (3) The true cause for choice is the dual nature of the expression (oath and vow), not the act itself. The author concludes that the expounder's objection is baseless. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the existence of choice.

Key Terms
التخيير (al-takhyir): The option given to the person who made a conditional vow with an undesired condition to either fulfill the vowed act or pay the kaffarah of an oath.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow; an obligation a person imposes on himself to perform an act of worship or charity, conditional or unconditional.
اليمين (al-yameen): An oath; a binding statement invoking Allah's name to affirm or deny something, with expiation (kaffarah) for breaking it.
الوفاء (al-wafa): Fulfillment of the vowed act; performing the action specified in the nadhr.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; options include freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them, or fasting three days if unable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when a person makes a conditional vow with a haram condition (e.g., 'If I commit zina, then upon me is fasting a year') and then commits the act?
A) The person must fulfill the vow (fast a year) because the condition is haram.
B) The person has a choice between fulfilling the vow or paying kaffarah of an oath.
C) The vow is invalid because the condition is haram; nothing is required.
D) The person must pay kaffarah only; the vow is null.

Key Takeaway
In a conditional vow on a haram act, the person retains the choice (takhyir) because the cause is the linguistic probability of the expression, not the prohibited nature of the condition.

The Ruling of Contiguous Exception in Oaths: Invalidation by 'Insha'Allah'
Ruling that saying 'insha'Allah' immediately after an oath nullifies it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on connecting 'insha'Allah' to an oath, explain why it invalidates the oath, and identify the condition of connectivity for the exception to be effective.

Core Idea
A connected 'insha'Allah' nullifies an oath; no sin or kaffarah upon breaking it.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when a person adds 'insha'Allah' immediately after swearing an oath, the oath becomes suspended on Allah's will. The result is that if the person later does what he swore not to do, he does not commit perjury and owes no expiation. The key condition is that the exception must be connected (muttasil) to the oath without a break. We will explore the evidence from hadith and the usul principle behind the requirement of connectivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing an oath and immediately saying 'insha'Allah'. The ruling is that the oath is invalid (batil) in the sense that it is not binding. Fulfillment is not obligatory, and breaking it requires no kaffarah.
The invalidation applies only if the exception is connected (wasl).
2
Understand the condition: connectivity (wasl)
The exception must be recited as part of the same utterance without any delay. If said separately (munfasil) after a pause, it has no effect on the oath. This principle is established in usul al-fiqh.
3
Trace the legal consequence
Because the oath is suspended on Allah's will, it is not definitively contracted. Thus, if the person later does the opposite of his oath, there is no sin (ithm) and no expiation (kaffarah). The person has a choice to proceed or leave.
4
Examine the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever swears and then makes an exception, if he wills he may go ahead, and if he wills he may leave it without sin.' This hadith is narrated by Nasa'i and Abu Dawud. The Hanafi school interprets 'exception' as saying 'insha'Allah' in a connected manner.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Some schools, such as the Shafi'i, do not accept this exception as invalidating the oath. They may require a different interpretation or allow a slight pause. The Hanafi position relies on the apparent meaning of the hadith and the usul principle that an exception must be connected.
The ruling is mukhtalaf fih, but the Hanafi mufta bihi is as stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن وصل إن شاء الله تعالى بحلفه بطل [١]قوله : ومَن وصل ؛ احترز به عمّا إذا قال بعد حلفه منفصلاً : إن شاء الله ، فإنّه لا يبطل به يمينه ؛ لأنّ الاستثناء إنما يعمل متصلاً لا منفصلاً، كما تقرر في كتب الأصول. [٢]قوله : بطل ؛ أي حلفه، فلم يجب برّه، ولم تجب الكفارة بحنثه ؛ لعدم عقده جزماً، بل معلقاً على مشيئة الله، وهي غير معلومة، وأصله حديث : «من حلف فاستثنى فإن شاء مضى وإن شاءَ تركه من غير حنث، أخرجه النسائي، والمراد بالاستثناء قوله : إنّ شاء الله على ما في «سنن أبي داود».
And whoever connects 'if Allah wills' to his oath, his oath is invalid. [1] His statement: 'Whoever connects'; he excludes from that when he says after his oath separately 'if Allah wills', then it does not invalidate his oath because the exception only works when connected, not when separate, as established in books of usul. [2] His statement: 'invalid'; meaning his oath is invalid, so fulfillment is not obligatory, and expiation is not required upon breaking it, because his oath is not definitively contracted, but rather suspended upon Allah's will, which is unknown. Its basis is the hadith: 'Whoever swears and then makes an exception, if he wills he may go ahead, and if he wills he may leave it without sin,' narrated by Nasa'i, and by exception is meant saying 'if Allah wills' as in Sunan Abi Dawud.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn statement that connecting 'insha'Allah' to an oath invalidates the oath. The commentator explains two key points: (1) The exception must be connected (muttasil) – i.e., said immediately as part of the oath statement, not separately after a pause. If said separately, it has no effect. This is a principle from usul al-fiqh. (2) The consequence of a valid connected exception is that the oath is not fully contracted; it is suspended on Allah's will. Therefore, if the person breaks the oath (does not do what he swore to do), there is no sin (ithm) and no kaffarah (expiation) required. The proof is the hadith reported by Imam Nasa'i and Abu Dawud, which gives the person the choice to go ahead or leave it without sin. The Hanafi school follows this ruling.

Key Terms
الوصل (al-wasl): The connection of the exception phrase 'insha'Allah' to the oath immediately without a break.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Saying 'insha'Allah' (if Allah wills) after an oath or vow to suspend its binding effect.
الحنث (al-hinth): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do, or not doing what one swore to do.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'By Allah, I will not smoke' and then immediately says 'if Allah wills,' what is the ruling?
A) The oath is valid and breaking it requires kaffarah.
B) The oath is invalid; no sin or kaffarah if he smokes.
C) The oath is invalid, but he must still avoid smoking.
D) The exception is only valid if he says it after a pause.

Key Takeaway
The connected exception 'insha'Allah' immediately after an oath nullifies its binding effect, so breaking it incurs no sin or kaffarah.

Ruling on Attaching 'Insha Allah' to an Oath
Hanafi ruling on oath voided by connected istithna

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath that includes a connected 'insha Allah' and explain the legal basis (dalil) for voiding the oath.

Core Idea
Saying 'insha Allah' immediately after an oath voids it; no obligation or expiation remains.

This lesson examines a critical refinement in Hanafi fiqh regarding oaths. When a person swears an oath and immediately adds 'if Allah wills' (insha Allah), the oath becomes void. The ruling is based on a hadith and the principle that the exception must be connected to be effective. We will explore the proof and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing an oath and immediately connecting 'insha Allah'. The ruling is that the oath is void (bāṭil).
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Sunan al-Nasa'i: 'Whoever swears an oath and then makes an exception, if he wishes he may proceed and if he wishes he may leave it without breaking the oath.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly permits leaving the oath without ḥanth when an exception is made, proving the oath is not binding.
4
Note the condition
The exception must be connected (muttasil); if disconnected (munfaṣil), the oath remains binding.
5
Identify the practical consequence
No expiation (kaffārah) is required if the person does not fulfill the oath, because the oath was never conclusively made.

Source Text
