Book of Oaths / Definition and Sections. And whoever connects 'if Allah wills' to his oath, it is void. Second: The alleviation is actually the option (takhyeer) made by the Lawgiver, not the option made by the servant for himself. For if a man says: 'If I commit zina, I am obligated to free a slave or feed a poor person,' it would be sufficient to perform one of them. Here, what causes the option is the word being susceptible to the meaning of oath and vow. In summary: the argument of the commentator (al-Shareh) is false from its origin, even if he boasts of it. His statement: 'and whoever connects' — he distinguishes thereby from if he says after his oath as disconnected: 'if Allah wills,' then his oath is not voided by it; because the exception only operates when connected, not disconnected, as established in usul works. Its origin is the hadith: 'Whoever swears an oath and then makes an exception, if he wishes he may proceed and if he wishes he may leave it without breaking the oath' — narrated by al-Nasa'i. (Footnote 1): By saying: 'By Allah, I will do such and such, if Allah wills,' it is not concluded: i.e., he does not break the oath at all, so it is not an oath. See: Sharh Mulla Miskin (p. 144). (Footnote 2): Imam al-Lakhnawi has already refuted the statement of the commentator (al-Shareh) Mulla Khusraw in Durar al-Hukkam (1:43), and Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar (3:69), and Ibn Kamal Pasha in al-Idah (q 71/a), and others. (Footnote 3): In Sunan al-Nasa'i (3:141), and Musnad Abi Awana (4:51), and others. (c) He said: 'Whoever swears and says: if Allah wills, has made an exception' in Sunan Abi Dawud (3:225), and others.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya provides a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the commentary of al-Wiqāya. The issue is the ruling on an oath that includes the phrase 'inshā' Allāh' (if Allah wills). The Hanafi position, based on the hadith, is that if the exception is said immediately (connected), the oath is void (bāṭil). That means no expiation is required if the person does not perform the action, and no sin is incurred. The author emphasizes that the exception must be connected; if said separately after a pause, the oath remains binding. The passage also refutes an argument by a commentator (al-Shārih) that the option (takhyeer) in such cases is an alleviation; the author clarifies that the option here arises from the wording's ambiguity between an oath and a vow, not from a divine alleviation. The hadith is cited as the proof. Thus, the ruling is clear: the oath is not concluded, and the person is free to act without penalty.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): Exception, specifically saying 'inshā' Allāh' after an oath, which suspends the effect.
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath, a solemn statement invoking Allah's name to confirm or promise something.
الحنث (al-ḥanth): Breaking an oath, which normally requires expiation (kaffārah) unless the oath is void.

C) The oath is valid only if he intends the exception seriously.
D) The oath is suspended until Allah's will is known.

Key Takeaway
A connected 'insha Allah' voids an oath; no expiation is due.

Oaths on Entering a House: Custom Overrides Literal Meaning
Explains Hanafi principle that oaths follow custom, not literal definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that oaths follow customary usage ('urf), contrast it with the Shafi'i literal and Maliki Quranic approaches, and apply it to determine whether entering a suffah, the Kaaba, or a mosque breaks an oath not to enter a 'house'.

Core Idea
Custom, not dictionary, defines an oath's terms – a suffah is a house; the Kaaba is not.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on oaths: if someone swears not to enter a 'house', entering a suffah (a temporary shelter) breaks the oath, but entering the Kaaba or a mosque does not. The key is the Hanafi school's reliance on customary usage ('urf) rather than literal or Quranic definitions. We will explore the inter-madhhab dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding oaths on entering a house. What is the general principle?
The passage states: 'Mabna al-ayman ‘inda-na ‘ala al-‘urf' – the basis is custom.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Recognize which other schools are mentioned and their different bases for interpreting oaths. Al-Shafi'i uses literal linguistic meaning; Malik uses Quranic usage.
These are the two main alternative methodologies.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the reasoning behind the opponents' positions. Shafi'i: literal meaning is default; Maliki: Quranic usage is authoritative.
The passage does not elaborate on their proofs, but we can infer.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine why the Hanafi school prefers custom. The text argues that people speak according to custom, and this is supported by practice of the Companions.
The principle of 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (actions by intentions) also supports custom as indicator of intention.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical difference: what changes in real life rulings due to this dispute? For example, does entering the Kaaba break the oath?
Hanafi: no. Shafi'i: possibly yes if literal meaning includes it. Maliki: maybe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالفعل
فصل اليمين في الدخول والسكنى
[1]
من حلف لا يدخلُ بيتاً يحنث بدخول صفة لا الكعبة، أو مسجد
[١] قوله : يحنث ; الأصل في هذا الباب أنّ مبنى الأيمان عندنا على العرف، ما لم ينو محتمل لفظه ; لأن المتكلم إنّما يتكلم بالكلام العرفي، وعلى هذا الأصل يتفرع فروع هذا الباب، وعند الشافعي مبنى الأيمان على الحقيقة اللغوية، وعند مالك لله على الاستعمال القرآني. كذا في «الفتح»" [٢] قوله : صفة ; - بضم الصاد وتشديد الفاء ، قال في «الهداية»: «لأنه تبنى للبيتوتة في بعض الأوقات، فصار كالشتوي والصيفي ، وقيل : هذا إذا كانت الصفة ذات حوائط أربعة وهكذا كانت صفافهم، وقيل: الجواب مجرى على إطلاقه، وهو الصحيح». انتهى وقال العَيْني في «البناية» (۳) : الشتوى : هو الذي يبني لأن يبات فيه الشتاء، والصيفي وهو الذي يبني ; لأن يبات فيه في الصيف، فالشتوي له جدران أربعة في واحد منها ،باب والصيف له ثلاثة جدران، وهوا الصفة، وذكر في «الفتح»: إنّ السقف ليس شرطاً في مسمّى البيت والدهليز.

Chapter of the oath by action. Section of the oath regarding entry and dwelling. [1] Whoever swears not to enter a house, he breaks his oath by entering a portico (suffah), but not the Kaaba or a mosque. [1] His statement 'he breaks' — the basis in this chapter is that oaths according to us (Hanafis) are based on custom, as long as he did not intend a possible literal meaning; because the speaker only speaks in customary speech, and upon this basis branch the branches of this chapter. According to al-Shafi'i, oaths are based on literal linguistic meaning, and according to Malik, on Quranic usage. Thus in al-Fath. [2] His statement 'suffah' — with dammah on the sad and shaddah on the fa. He said in al-Hidayah: 'Because it is built for staying overnight at certain times, so it becomes like the winter house and summer house. And it is said: this is when the suffah has four walls, and such was their suffahs. And it is said: the answer is given absolutely, and it is correct.' End. And al-'Ayni said in al-Binayah: The winter house is that which is built to be stayed in during winter, and the summer house is that which is built to be stayed in during summer. The winter house has four walls with a door in one of them, and the summer house has three walls, and it is the suffah. And it is mentioned in al-Fath that the roof is not a condition for the name 'house' and 'vestibule'. [3] His statement 'not the Kaaba...etc.' — meaning he does not break his oath by entering the Kaaba, even though it is called the House of Allah, and Allah said: 'Allah has made the Kaaba, the Sacred House, a station for mankind.' Likewise, he does not break his oath by entering the mosque, even though it is called the House of Allah, and Allah said (the Prophet ﷺ) concerning mosques:
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on oaths by action, specifically the section on oaths regarding entering and residing. The main ruling is that if a person swears not to enter a 'house' (bayt), he breaks his oath by entering a 'suffah' (a portico or shelter with three or four walls) but not by entering the Kaaba or a mosque. The commentator then explains the underlying principle: in the Hanafi school, oaths are interpreted according to customary usage ('urf), not literal linguistic definitions, unless the swearer intended a possible literal meaning. This is because people speak according to custom. All furu' (branch rulings) in this chapter derive from this principle. The commentator contrasts this with the Shafi'i school, which bases oaths on literal linguistic meaning, and the Maliki school, which bases them on Quranic usage. The definition of 'suffah' is discussed: it is a structure built for temporary overnight stay, similar to a winter or summer house. The correct view is that the ruling applies absolutely, without requiring four walls. The commentator also clarifies that a 'bayt' does not require a roof; the term includes any covered space like a vestibule. Finally, the Kaaba and mosques are excluded from the customary meaning of 'house' for oath purposes, even though they are called 'House of Allah', because custom does not include them in the general term 'bayt'.

Key Terms
صفة (suffah): A portico or shelter built for temporary overnight stay, traditionally with three or four walls and a roof
حنث (hanith): To break an oath by doing what one swore not to do (or vice versa)
العرف (al-'urf): Customary usage; what is commonly understood by a term in a given community

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears 'I will not enter a house', which of the following would break the oath?
A) Entering the Kaaba
B) Entering a mosque
C) Entering a suffah (portico) with three walls
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets oaths based on customary usage, so a suffah breaks an oath not to enter a house, but the Kaaba or mosque does not.
